THE RED PAGE.
Thus “ C.P.B.’’—
“ What is an epigram ? A dwarfish whole.
Its body brevity, and wit its soul.”
Some time ago I came across the above. Could you
give me the author’s name ? I cannot trace it. At the
time I bad been reading the works of Matthew Prior,
Congreve, Tillotson, Marvel, and others of that period.
Ignoramus ; but the hunt found Martial’s
Omm epigramma sit instar apis, aculeus illi,
Sint sua mella, sit et corporis exigtii.
In the guise of a bee make your epigrams all,
With its sting, and its honey, and body so small.

The pupils of a Launceston (Tas.) Ladies’
College were recently told “ Write what you know
about the War.” Some answers—
if there was no war all the place would be roving we
feeling that is very sad. one man picks another man and
then they fight and when they get killed they all get
trampt about.
It is a good thing for this war that is going to be won
and it is good fqr the soldiers too because they chose it
and because the-war is good is because we are going to
fight for our country to see which will win and it is the
men called boer’s who are going to fight too and I think
the wives of the men will feel very sad and the feelings of
the troops will not care for it much.
The Boas though they would like a little possession in
England. The people sent the troops away from here to
show Bravery & courage. It is getting fought between
the Boas & the English. I think the English are the
right. In to days news the Boas captured one thousand
mules from the English. There are more Boas than the
English but there are a lot more troops going to the
assistance of the English & the soldiers have sent to
England to ask for more soldiers because they think they
wont have enough.
A war is now going on between the English and the
Africans, at Transaval Republic in Africa. England is in
the right It is a very noble feeling that Tasmain was
feeling when the troops went away & is still feeling.
And their parents probably know just as much
about it.

A note —
Dear Bookfellow, —Tell one of your impressionist friends
to sit at the top of Lavender Bay steps for an hour some
evening after dark, and if he can paint what he sees I will
buy the picture—if the artistic mouth is not opened too
wide for my pocket.—Yours, Phyllis Tyne.
• * * *
D. J., after reading Percy Emery—
Was interested in this young man’s revelations. He
struck the bull’s eye accidentally, when he sold Pearson
some stuff for £2 2s. If youngsters will persist in yelling
“ Excelsior 1 ” and clambering the heights in search of
the edelweiss of literary excellence, they wall but bark
their shins to no purpose. The Parnassus of to-day is the
Corporation Tip, and he who would make money out of a
dunghill must- study those who love its odours. In a
word, no man can in these times afford to be great who
has not first been very small. He must rise on the breath
of the many-headed. Why not ? They are the greater—
who have a voracious appetite. > See then that you serve
to them the viands they like! Then you prosper,
and rise, and soon may flout your patrons
as the vulgar dogs they are. The first thing to
arrive at is the opportunity. Pearson and the People’s
Penn’orths afford that readily to the wise aspirant; ’tis
the lofty-souled youth, full of high purpose, who tries
first the swell publishers, and so goes bare of hack and
belly. 'A little thought would show him that he could
easier get at the great guns through Pearson’s and
Earmsworth’s than any other way. And meanwhile he
could live, and pick his teeth.

Bulls, by an Irishman of “Warrigal’s” acquaint-
ance
The promised dowry to his daughter is a cow, and
speaking to a friend, he said : “ Whoever gets my Polly
will get a cow.”
Showing me a pair of boots which had been too small
when he bought them, he said : “ I had to wear these
boots a fortnight before I could get them on.”

Artists Eullwood, Fischer and Hall Thorpe
have flitted from this continent —and writer
Law'son; E. J. Brady is in Brisbane, more or less
on the way to Carpentaria—and A. H. Adams has
returned to Maoriland, sooner or later on the
way to England. Of the last-named, by the way,
Morley Roberts writes in Eng. Revieiv of the
Week that
Mr. Adams has the melancholy which is not of the arid
bushland, even though he understands its charm. The
sprits of Maoriland have spoken with him ; .and new land
as it is to those of English stock, he knows it is very,
very old.
* * * *
y,e Gallienne’s new edition of his book about
George Meredith exposes an interesting change of
mental front. The first edition was all enthusiasm;
to-day’s “Postscript” doubts if any novel will
live except “Richard Feverel,” or any poem ex-
cept “Love in the Valley”- and “Modern
Love.” Meredith is a better philosopher
than artist, is Le G’s present criticism. Which
marks the critic’s passage into middle age.
Meredith, for the most part, is an author for the
delight of clever young men, with sufficient sur-
plus vitality to climb his stiles and leap his
ditches. As we get older, we have less life to
brmg and exact more from our author. To read
Meredith is to study ; after a few pages the
mental strain fatigues: to read Dumas is to be
inspirited and recreated —the does the work which
Meredith makes you do. So, as we grow older,
we leave Meredith and his kin more often on the
shelf, and more often return, with Thackeray, to
the inexhaustible “ Mousquetaires.”
* * * *
A Ruskin item. “ N.”—
The last mail brought all the obituaries of Ruskin. Not
one of these that I have seen has the faintest reference to
his marriage to the lady who afterwards became wife of
Millais. Yet surely it was the most powerful influence in
his life. A biography which leaves it out must be
incurably maimed. Millais’ Life, by his son, does not
leave out Lady Millais. It omits Ruskin’s name, but says
that the nullity of marriage, on her petition, was in
accordance with the principles both of Church and State.
Her family carried it through. Her mother admired
Millais, and insisted on his living part of the year at her
Scottish home. The biography has three portraits of
Lady Millais, first as a girl of distracting beauty. Her
eyes had a slight turn outwards. Husband and wife grew
very much alike. They blended beautifully in their four
daughters and four sons.
Bookfellow’s Mixture.
gd
The Influence of "Bad Smart
Society” in England.
WITH all Europe, excepting a few of
the Hungarian gentry, and some
Italian students of history, violent-
. . even passionately, opposed to
us; with our armies held in check by a determined,
wealthy, and well led nation in arms; with a
Ministry avowedly ignorant as the man-in-tho-
street of the trend of international events the
country watches .the meeting of Parliament in a
hush of apprehension. . . . The present state of
affairs is the direct result of unpreparedness and
want of prescience, for which the Government is
itself inalienably responsible. . . . The present
calamities are not due to the action of any public
department or section of a department in which
employment is open to all comers duly qualified.
The commissariat, the army medical.staff, the
company officers, the private soldiers, the Army
Service Corps, and the transport department have
done splendidly. There has been no failure in
connection with any branch of the public service
open to tile public. The scandal and breakdown
have resulted from the departments where the
staff is chosen entirely from a small and privileged
caste. Smart society has captured the Foreign
Office, one side of the War Office, the cavalry,
and the higher military appointments; What are
the results? In foreign affairs, on those matters
of detail in which the overworked Premier and
Foreign Secretary cannot be expected to interfere,
England has been bested whenever she has come
into conflict with foreign Powers. The seizure of
the Bundesrath and other German steamers, on
false information, and their subsequent surrender
under the humiliation of threats from Berlin,
should have been impossible if the Foreign Office
were Organised on plain business principles, and
worked by men who lost their billets if they were
stupid or idle, instead of being assured of pensions
the more stupid and the more incapable they
showed themselves to be.
The Canker in the Army,
In the same manner the British Armv, in its
higher branches, is a great social rather than a
fighting machine. The Queen’s uniform, that
should be a source of pride, while compulsory on
the private, is “bad form ” on the officer off duty.
The Germans do not thus regard the question—
nor do the Boers. Without going into more
detail, is it not a fact that the ornamental privi-
leged caste among our rulers has hopelessly failed
to justify its monopoly of certain branches of the
public service, and that, therefore, if this war is
not only to be won, but our national safety assured
against even greater calamities, English demo-
cracy must reclaim the power with which they
have parted, and by silent, peaceful revolution
insist on fitness and efficiency becoming the one
qualification for public employment ? . . .
The day before the news of Sir Redvers Buller’s
defeat on the Tugela was published by the War
Office, a statement—it was more than a rumour—
was circulated throughout the country to the
effect that Ladysmith was relieved. This state-
ment was so assiduously spread abroad that I my-
self know of its existence at 11 a.m. on the day in
question (1) on board an Isle of Wight steamer
from Cowes to Portsmouth, (2) about 10 a.m. in
the house of one of the Judges of the High Court,
(3) in Edinburgh before noon. Two great names
in smart financial society were mentioned in con-
nection with the statement. It was absolutely
and completely false. Now, this report was no
mere Stock Exchange rumor. It was an un-
scrupulous device of the financial hangers-on of
smart society for people, who knew that
Buller had failed, to sell mining stock to those
who believed that he had succeeded. When this
episode is probed to the bottom the public will
learn something curious and alarming as to the
price it pays for surrendering to smart society the
control of the services, including the manipulation
of news from the seat of war. . . .
Wanted—A Social Surgeon.
Before the French Revolution broke out, unpaid
peasants were compelled to flog the ponds all
night, to prevent the sleep of the seigneurie from
being distui-bed by the croakings of frogs. We
know the consequences. Privilege asserts itself
in other ways to-day. It snubs 'the “ Colonials,”
insults Volunteers, shelves the best generals. It
is successfully deceived by Dr. Leyds. It feasted
and fawned on the Jameson raiders. It condoned
the wicked Raid. It takes pay and pensions for
for work it does not perform. It jokes when men
are dying for us in the front. And, lastly, it
chatters idly in Parliament at the hour when
prompt and drastic action is needed to save the
State, and prevent our-good Queen’s grey hairs
from going down with sorrow to the grave. . . .
Smart society is the proud-flesh in a body politic
for the most part sound and wholesome. The
diseased area is spreading. Excision is the only
remedy. To diagnose the disease we must explore
its symptoms and lay bare the evil.
The unit of strong nations is the family. All
legislation, habits, ideals, policy, or ambitions
that increase the welfare and multiply the number
of happy families are good for the nation. Things
that stunt, belittle, or ridicule domestic life are
bad for the nation. This is copy-book common-
place, but bed-rock truth. Turkey is what it is
mainly because the harem replaces family life in
upper or wealthier classes. The note by which
bad smart society may be recognised is its con-
tempt for family life—its loathing of home.
Luxury and over-feeding seven days a week kill
desire'for aught else but feeding and luxury.
Plays and music-halls, restaurant-dining, eternal
card-playing, and the racecourse, produce satiety,
and satiety of the senses is the tomb of duty and
honour.
The art of conversation m smart society is
extinct. Slang shibboleths, composed of baby-talk
and Italian or French tags to the Queen’s English,
form the dialect of the smart set. Disreputable
women, who affect the conduct of Lais without
her graces, are among the leading spirits of smart
society. When the morals of the poultry-yard
flourish in the atmosphere of the stable, it is only
natural that the intelligence of the jockey should
be applied to the problems of Empire.
To enter the charmed circle, neither brains nor
breeding, birth nor influence, are necessary. AH
that is required is money, and then more money—
with an insolent contempt for the laws that are the
unseen foundations of civilised society. A use-
ful ” adventurer thus equipped can buy his way
into illustrious circles as easily as he picks up a
yearling at Newmarket. Every now and then
an explosion takes place, and the public learns
with bewilderment that cheating at caids is not
unknown in smart society; or that women who
are courtesans in all but name ai-e no more tabooed
in smart society to-day than they were excluded
from the Pavilion at Brighton in the days of the
Prince described by Thackeray as “the First
Gentleman and most finished blackguard in
Europe.”
The secret influence on Government wielded by
this Comus rout of Circes, sybarites, cynics, and
financiers is subtle and profound. It is revealed
to the public only in its effects. Legislation,
foreign policy, taxation, is not always settled in
Parliament. The real decisions are first made in
smart drawing-rooms in t lie season ; on Sundays
in country-houses; in boudoirs and restaurants,
Is a man “straight,” or a lady modest, and do
they decline to bow the knee to bad smartmess ?
Their reputation and chances are destroyed
by the aid of the janissaries of a smart society,
always on hire to the highest bidder. The
wrong class of American women-people who
.are not received in New York or Washington
-arc pushed into what is seemingly, hut not
really, exclusive society. During the last ten
years the speculative financial element lias be-
come prominent in places whore formerly it was
despised. These capitalists are not in society for
their health. They arc on the prowl. They spend
freely. Their largesse is an investment. As the
modern nabob is commonly of alien extraction and
not seldom of Eastern origin, he regards his
adopted country with detachment of mind,
Schemes are pushed by the force of public eilfchu-
siasm for Imperial expansion, which is adroitly
manipulated for the benefit of financiers and their
henchmen. Smart women without character, men
without self-respect, and an Administration that
is too philosophical, effete, preoccupied, or iudif-
ferent t 6 see that greatness is slipping
away from her, are the allies of this infamous con-
fcdcracy.

It is not the custom of the English people to
make- futile changes. Still, nothing less than
structural alterations will avail. Ihe House of
Commons is losing self-respect as it loses national
esteem Parliament will continue to Sink in
national esteem until it reflects the character and
brain of Britain, as well as it represents Irish
discontent and the capitalist, alcoholic, and social
interests in the Hinted Kingdom Through fee
Press and a reformed House of Commons alone
can the nation strike home at smart society. Men
can dp anything they like if they only want. it
3sa
verdiot against lutamy and licentiousness in high
places, forbids the interference of smart society or
its parasites With national affairs it is certain that
those who wash the end can find the means.
A steady deterioration of character seems to
affect politicians who become Ministers, A man
who in private life conducts himself as an honour-
abie gentleman is compelled to become the mouth-
piece of falsehoods designed to deceive the nation,
baffle the House of Commons, shield the Depart-
ment, and burke enquiry. Cabinet responsi-
bility, which means no responsibility for anyone,
prevents the impeachment of incapable Ministers,
The bureaucracy are equally irresponsible. In
1806—the dark age of an unreformed Parliament
—a Minister was impeached for dishonesty. The
Queen’s yacht turns turtle in 1900. Nothing is
done, except that the First Lord comes down to
the House to praise the designer. It is notorious
that official statements are often suggestions of
what is false, suppressions of what is true, or
both; while officials returns are not seldom pal-
pable frauds. Take the case of the Army Reser-
visfc, who is at the same time a Militiaman, and
also enlisted as a “special servist.” One man
figures as three to gull the public. The annual
speech of the First Lord of the Admiralty, in pro-
posing the Navy Estimates, is a public scandal.
If a Commission were appointed to examine the
business methods of the Foreign Office, I will
undertake to supply evidence showing where a
falsehood has been placed in the mouth of the
Minister by subordinates who either knew that it
was a falsehood, and should, therefore, be dis-
missed for fraud, or did not know that it was a
falsehood, in which case they should be cashiered
for incapacity. Smart society protects them.
Their pensions are secure. No impeachment is
even thought of in 1900, although democracy
believes itself triumphant. A few weeks ago a
signalman was indicted for an act of carelessness
that produced a collision, involving loss of life.
He was sentenced to three years without hard
labor. Protected by smart society, negligent and
incompetent servants of the State, if in l certain
caste, are immune from punishment for worse
misdeeds than the signalnian’s. If there is less
public spirit to-day than in 1806, the middle and
working classes, who know what is wanted, have
the remedy in their own hands. After Admiral
Byng was shot on his own quarter-deck, in Ports-
mouth Harbour, in 1757, the Naval service took
the hint and became efficient. From Admiral
Byng to the heroes of the Rand is a long step ;

Every dead Empire has had its bad smart set.
In Babylon, Persia, Greece, and Rome, and under
the two bad smartness contributed its
quota to the ruin of a nation and the fall of a coun-
try. Smart society has existed for centuries m
England. hen the occupants of the T
were themselves licentious and bad smart society
was in the ascendant, the nation rotted visi y a
the top. In the days of Elizabeth and Anne, how-
ever, smart society, so far from being universally
corrupt, rose to a ] high level of distinction. . Men
of action then filled the public eye Patriotism
was honoured rather than despised. It at an
mes an j i n every land the smart set diced, drank,
and danced> and revelled in the roses and raptures
of in tbe Empires that are dead, and even
Charles the Second was King, they went
outside their own set to raise money for their
pi easur es.

tion.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cloud and Sunshine.
Low in the cool, sweet grass
Beneath a gloss of leaves,
Trembling as faint airs pass
Along the forest eaves—
Low on the verdant sod,
Where myriad blossoms rise,
Searching the books of God
With yearning eyes.
A blue, far-stretching sea
Leans to a cloudless sky,
And seabirds sailing free,
White doves of peace, go by ;
A snowy, single sail
Hangs on the margin far,
Fleok of a foam-spent gale
From deeps afar.
All yesterday a storm
Blew from an inky pall;
And billows uniform
Did raging shoreward fall;
All yesterday my heart
In churlish anger gloomed,
Fretting my soul apart—
As one foredoomed.
But morning breaks in smiles,
My heart u-pheaves its c re,
And Ocean’s shining miles
In silent strength lie there.
What can a poor heart do,
Laugh of the Sea and Sky,
But join the music too
As Life goes by.
Tas.
BARKIS.
Bed Time.
Come a-tip-toe, stealthily,
Through the sleeping bush with me,
Where the lazy moon hath spread
Shadow-velvet for our tread,
And each wind-worn bough hath caught
Dainty fare for hungry thought.
Tiny sparks of diamond fire
Jewel every trembling spire ;
Come a-tip-toe, lest we press
One white drop of loveliness.
Near the river-gums we’ll creep !
Big, round, awesome eyes will peep;
Soft eyes, full of wonderment—
Who will break their sweet content ?
One rude step would mar the joys
Told by Night’s mysterious noise;
Joys of living,- joys of love,
Countless as the leaves above.
Hush ! I hear a lusty breeze
Kissing all the distant trees ;
Drouthy, from the haunts of men
It is hastening home again ;
When its merry love-lays pass,
We will sleep upon the grass.
N.S.W.
LESLIE OAKES.
The Sonnet of Revolt.
“ There is an atmosphere,” said the Colonel, “ in which
crime becomes virtue; and the children'of impulse are
truly the children of God.”— Villkbois.
Come, let us grieve !—too much of vulgar care,
Of vulgar joy, and vulgar, common woe
Oppress our souls, and stunted make us grow,
And keep our heads down-bent from the pure air
Where even flowers of Crime bloom fresh and fair,
And God round murderers’ brows doth aureoles
throw,
And blood-gouts as deific rubies glow.
Come, let us grieve, as only heroes dare !
Even our tears are vulgar, common, cheap ;
The heart’s emotions but a scullion’s sink
Of kitchen-washings gurgling to a drain.
Give us once more the god’s heroic leap
To Life’s deep Fountain, there to kneel and
drink,
And die : triumphant—in Promethean pain.
N.S.W.
FRED J. BROOMFIELD.
Dreamy Land.
There’s gold galore in the Dreamy Land,
There’s diamond store in the Dreamy Land ;
The sails are silk on a sapphire sea,
The buds they blow on a breezy lea,
The elves they trip on a tricksy toe,
The fairies flit when the lamps are low,
While Mab she waveth a fairy wand
And weaveth mirth in the Dreamy Land.
No tears are there in the Dreamy Land,
Our hearts are bare in the Dreamy Land,
Our brows are pressed in a flow’ry twine,
We drowse and drink of a wondrous wine;
Our voices lift in a purer air,
We carve good-bye on the grave of Care,
The stars they sing and we understand
The words they drop in the Dreamy Land !
The moon’s ablink by the Dreamy Land,
The tapers wink into Dreamy Land;
Ye close blue eyes to a gentle chime,
Ye pass the Gate in a breathing-time,
Your lids ye open again—and lo
The realm ye knew m the Days Ago !
The silver stream and the golden strand
And the Laughter sweet of the Dreamy Land.
The cradles rock in the Dreamy Land,
The children flock into Dreamy Land;
They fish for crowns in a shining sea.
They crowd and kiss at the Trystmg
They spread their wings and away they fly,
And kick the clouds as they wander by;
A wild, undutiful, beautiful band,
The children flit in the Dreamy Land.
The storms are stilled in the Dreamy Land,
The birds they build in the Dreamy Land ;
The young ones cling in a cosy nest,
The babes they laugh on a gentle breast,
The trumpets ring and the flags are flung,
The sad are glad and the old are young ;
O Life is blent in a texture bland
Of Rainbow Joys in the Dreamy Land !
Q.
P. LURRIG.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to 3. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient,

such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 2, 1900.
The Associated Mine and Other Matters.
The story of the Associated mine, in Wes-
tralia, is another powerful sermon upon a
text from which The Bulletin has preached
frequently—the text which says that the
influx of English capital has been one of the
chief curses of Australia, and that English
company-mongering and company-flotation,
and English syndicates, and English man-
agement have been four subordinate and
collateral curses naturally arising therefrom.
The tale of the Associated mine is also, more
or less, the tale of very many—perhaps most
—other English-owned and English-managed
mines in Australia. That is to say, the sys-
tem was the same, and the means for working
the swindle were the same, and the only
difference is that in some other instances the
occasion for the swindle hasn’t yet arisen.
The Associated was floated in England with a
huge capital. Only a very small part of the
capital ever came to Australia, for it is one
of the peculiarities of the alleged inflow of
British capital that most of it doesn’t flow.
This country, finding it had a great mine,
gave away about two-thirds of it to English
syndicaters on condition they supplied the
necessary pick and shovel to open up the
other third. The mine was managed in
England by directors who, most of them,
never saw a gold-mine and wouldn’t know it
from a tin prospecting area or a sewerage-
shaft. These individuals paid themselves
immense salaries for their ignorance, and
a London office was also maintained for them
to be ignorant in, and a London staff to wait
on them. These were as wholly superfluous
to the working of the mine as a staff of chap-
lains and astrologers would have been,
for men who really understood mining had
to be hired to know the things the directors
should have known but didn’t.
* * * *
The mine was worked in the interests of a
#mall clique in London. Reports of tonnage,
gold yields and prospects were sent to
London exclusively. If prospects were good
the news was invariably kept dark till
somebody in London had time to buy in ; if
prospects were bad the fact didn’t leak out
till somebody in London had time to unload
on a credulous public. The Australian
shareholders were denied information about
their own mine, or access to it—they are
now actually talking of starting a costly law-
suit in order to get a look at their own pro-
perty. The Associated made a great boom.
It was announced for a few months as pro-
ducing an almost incredible amount of gold
per month, and the Australian shareholder
had no fair chance to know whether it was
really doing it or not. It was reported
quite recently, via London, that the
rich stone in sight was equal to fully
750,000 tons worth 2 oz. or over, and
no Australian shareholder or share-buyer
had a fair chance to test the truth of the
statement. And when shares had gone up
almost out of sight, and the people in
London who wanted to sell had sold at top
price, it was calmly admitted that the
750,000 tons of rich stone was all a lie, and
only 35,000 tons of comparatively poor ore
was visible, that the bottom was going out
of the mine, and that, so far as can be seen
at present, the great Associated is quite
possibly broke and empty and done. In the
fall that ensued, Australian shareholders
probably lost more than they ever made out
of the Associated, and were left poorer in
the aggregate than if the great mine had
never been. Some time ago the scrip stood
at £l3 ; now it is less than £3. The /-influx
of British capital is apt to pan out that way.
* * * *
Of course it may be that Australian
shares will be largely bought in London
at waste-paper prices, and then it may
transpire that, after all, there are
1,000,000 tons of rich ore in sight, and
after being sold once, the Australian share-
holder may find himself sold a second’time.
His only satisfaction is that he is in no
worse case than the ruck of English share-
holders. The Australian holder, who is
often an expert and able to size-up the value
of the property if he could get into it, is
proudly booted out by his alleged servant,
the English director. The English share-
holder is 16,000 miles away. Under such a
system, if the English director likes to go
crooked, and selects his instruments with
sufficient care, he can work stupendous
swindles with impunity. The fact that he
established the system suggests a fixed in-
tention to go crooked—and it is the system
on which many English-owned mines in Aus-
tralia are worked. The average English
director isn’t a miner—only an exchange
jobber. A mine, to him, isn’t an area to be
worked legitimately for gold, but a stock-
exchange gamble—which is much the same
as saying that it is a counter with which to
play a game of legalised fraud. If he can
make a sufficiently profitable gamble in Lon-
don it doesn’t matter to him if he wrecks
every mine in Westralia, and throws the
whole mining population out of work, and
reduces the country to beggary. Westralia
isn’t his funeral.
* * * •
If there is a semblance of courage or
honesty about the Forrest Government it
will take strong measures with this evil forth-
with. Westralia is just now the especial
home of British-owned mining companies
and London mining frauds, therefore it is
Forrest’s duty to lead the way. The first
essential is to provide for the frequent
inspection of mines by State experts, and
the prompt publication of their reports. The
second is to enact that, on (say) one day
every week, any registered shareholder shall
be authorised to inspect his mine, and every-
thing belonging thereto, as fully as he
pleases, either personally or by agent, and
that it shall be also open to the press. These
two enactments would knock the bottom out
of the policy of secrecy on which the London
swindle, mostly rests, and they would do no
possible injury to anybody except the Stock-
Exchange swindler. A gold-mine is sup-
posed to be an honest industrial enterprise,
not a device by which the man who has in-
formation can defraud the man who hasn't.
All the owners are supposed to have an
equal chance to get money out of it in
proportion to the extent of their interests.
It is not understood that because one share-
holder is appointed and paid and trusted by
his fellow-shareholders to look after their
interests on the board he shall abuse this
confidence, and swindle the people who
trusted him, and suppress good news till he
has bought the shares of his partners for less
than their value, or bad news till he has.sold
his shares to them for more than they are
worth. And the value of shares is supposed
to depend on the value of the mine so far as
it can be ascertained—not on the conceal-
ment of its value.
• • * •
There is absolutely no sense in which
legitimate and honest mining can be injured
by the utmost publicity, and legitimate
mining is the only kind any Government
should encourage. If the mine looks well
it is to the interests of the people that it
should be known, so that they won’t be be-
guiled into selling below value. If it looks
badly it is to their interests that it should be
known so that they won’t be diddled into buy-
ing any plausible person’s shares at six
times their real worth. A mine is not sup-
posed to be a conspiracy, or a racehorse, or
a patent, or a secret still, or a midnight
murder, or a forged cheque, or a game of
under-and-over, or anything that requires to
be kept dark. It is understood to be a
concern the shares in which are sold on the
visible merits of the reef, and there is no
reason that can be adduced for keeping these
merits secret that isn’t a shamefully dis-
honest reason. Of course the compulsory
opening of mines to inspection wouldn’t put
all shareholders on an absolute level—no-
thing could do that. Some people would still
go in for share-buying without inspection.
Some would go in for it without the re-
quisite knmvledge, and if they appointed
an agent to inspect for them would send
the wrong kind of agent. Some would
still be foolish enough to take shares in
remote mines and get left through their
remoteness. The State couldn’t provide for
all these ; it couldn’t tear up the mine by
the roots and post it to distant owners to be
looked at and returned. But by providing
that every shareholder could see all that was
to be seen if he liked to come to his mine
either personally or by deputy, and by pub-
lishing the reports of the Government ex-
perts, the State would do the best it could
do—in this direction, anyhow.
* * * *
In addition to this, every mining co. should
be required to have at least some of its direc-
tors in the province where the mine is situ-
ated, so that some responsible person would
be within the jurisdiction of the law. They
and the manager should be answerable for
the prompt and accurate publication of the
amount of gold obtained at each cleaning-up
and the amount of stone from which that
gold was obtained. This return should be
posted up conspicuously outside the company’s
premises within half-an-hour of the figures
being ascertained, and should at the same
time be despatched to the most convenient
public authority and the nearest newspapers
for further publication. The issuing of
erroneous returns should be a criminal act.
The qualification for directors should be
fixed by law, and there should be a heavy
minimum penalty on any director who holds
office, even for a minute, without possessing
the full necessary qualification in his own
right and unencumbered. The director or
manager who issues false reports about his
mine should get a long term for false pre-
tences. And even then there would be the
Bull and Bear, the spec.-seller, and the
time-bargain man to be dealt with, for they,
by making artificial scarcity or artificial
abundance of any particular stock, would
still force prices up and down irrespective of
the merits of the mine. The most dead sure
way to deal with them would be to annul by
law every share transaction in which the
scrip was not actually attached to the trans-
fer, or if for any reason this was impossible,
in which the numbers of the transferred
shares were not inserted in the transfer.
Also the inserting of numbers which did not
represent shares actually dealt in should be
a fraud and punishable as such.
* * * *
Of course these reforms would deprive the
Bull and the Bear of their bread and butter ;
an absolutely unproductive class which is
neither of real use nor of real ornament
should be deprived of its bread and butter—
likewise of its boots. Doubtless they would
also stop the “influx of English capital ”
into Australian mining fields and necessitate
a further enactment that all Australian
mines shall be managed in Australia and
have an Australian register only. Both
results would be commendable. The clean-
est, most honest, most consistently profitable
mining fields in this country have been those
where the mines were locally owned and
managed. On such a field as Bendigo
there are practically no big swindles, for the
local shareholders are constantly inspecting
their properties, and such a thing as the
great Associated stench would be destroyed
by publicity. The directors are practical
men who (though some of them are seini-
millionaires like Lansell and Dr. Atkinson)
take ss. for a board-meeting—not titled
ignoramuses at £lOOO a year. There are
no expensive offices or costly ornamental
managers. When an expert is wanted to
overhaul a mine, a first-class local man will
ao it thoroughly for a very few guineas,
instead of an ornamental man being sent all
the way from Europe at the cost of thou-
sands. Kalgoorlie might have been another
Bendigo if the laws above suggested had
been enacted some years ago. Then, instead
of a few great fortunes, mostly in England
and America, and many great moral stenches
in Australia, and a few huge booms, and a
multitude of huge bursts, there would have
been a settled industry living on its merits,
paying large and steady dividends to Aus-
tralian shareholders, and not breaking the
ten commandments more than is done in
any other worldly avocation
Imperial Interference with the Common-
wealth Bill.
Mr. J. H. Symon, Q.C., discourses to The Bul-
letin on Imperial Interference with the Common-
wealth Bill: —
Who is to forbid the banns of Australian Union ? The
Federating provinces are of competent age. They have
settled their own marriage-articles. The parties are will-
ing—the settlements are satisfactory—all with apparent
parental approbation. For England to interfere and say
“ I am a partner in this ” would be like a man’s mother-
in-law insisting upon his marrying her as well as his wife,
yet that in effect is what many people at this moment
are foolish enough to contend—that England is a partner
in the Australian Federal bargain—not only a partner,
but the predominant and dominating partner. So much
so that she can alter or add to the contract without say-
ing “by your leave.” We thought we were free. Mr.
Chamberlain rudely undeceives us. He cracks in our
faces the whip of the British Parliament. Now, England
has neither part nor lot in the business so far as the
Federal compact is concerned. No proof is needed
beyond the fact that she was not represented in the
Convention. Had she been a party, she ought to and
would have been there. That she never asked or invited
herself to come or even to watch the proceedings con-
clusively admits that the framing of our Federal Consti-
tution is peculiarly and exclusively our own affair. It is
only now, when even colorable arguments to justify
Imperial interference with the Australian Charter are
scarce, that this specious absurdity is trotted out.
* » * »
The British Empire is not a partnership. We share
with England the Queen’s sovereignty and the flag, but
there any semblance of partnership ends. The dreamers
of Australian Federation planned a partnership, but
without the possibility of practical result. Even if you
call the relation between England and the self-governing
Colonies partnership, she is constitutionally onl y a sleeping-
partner. The supremacy of the Imperial Parliament is a
theory. Hitherto it has been vigorously kept out of sight.
Now, for the first time, it is to be exerted by a Tory
Government, which has been living on its pretended
devotion to the spread of freedom amongst the citizens of
the Empire. It will be a fatal mistake if that British
interference is persisted in. It will be equally fatal if we
sit down under it. The former will be an open assertion
of the right to interfere with Australian self-government
on the part of the British Parliament. The latter will
mean acquiescence on our part in that assertion, and the
creation of a precedent it may be hard afterwards to dis-
lodge.
* * » *
What Federal progress we have achieved so far has been
based upon trusting the people to decide their own
destiny. The Bill of 1891 died practically still-born,
because it had no popular sanction. Since then the
accepted policy has been to commit the business direct to
the people. Through stagnation, apathy, misrepresenta-
tion, and other obstacles, they rose to their responsibility,
and grappled the work with unexpected vigor. No table
was large enough to permit of nearly four millions of
people sitting around it to actually frame the Constitu-
tion. But they would not trust any Parliament. So they
elected a special Convention of experts—at any rate, of
men who had their confidence. That Convention met to
frame a Bill for adoption by the people of Australia whom
they represented- not for revision by the Imperial
Parliament. Nor did the people accept it subject or with
a view to any such revision. The Imperial Parliament is
not competent to deal with it.
* * * •
How could it be ? Our own provincial Parliaments
were infinitely better fitted, yet they were discarded.
They were not even allowed to choose the Convention.
They Were only permitted—a sop to Cerberus—to worry
at the first draft of the Bill and offer suggestions. The
Convention was specially chosen for the work. It repre-
sented Australian prejudices and passions, local interests
and local conflicts, provincialism, nationalism, and Im-
perialism. It represented all political parties and shades
of opinion ; it was instinct with provincial narrowness
as well as with national aspirations, and in touch with
Australian sentiment. The Imperial Parliament is obvi-
ously destitute of any pretence to equipment so essen-
tial—in fact, that Parliament in its corporate character is
as ill-adapted as any body of men in the world to under-
stand the Australian people or interpret their wishes.
The Convention, however, was singularly well adapted
to the work. Weeks and months, split into three sessions
and spread over a year, they passed in daily consultation
and debate—-in anxious and arduous labor to execute
worthily their great task, influenced by love of country
and the aspirations of Australian nationality—until at
length they presented the finished fabric of Australian
self-government—the Commonwealth Bill —and recom-
mended it to the people for acceptance.
* * « *
That fine instrument was full of compromises—one
thing here balancing something else there—so that to
change any part of it was to re-open the whole. All the
parts are so necessarily inter-dependent—one might say
interlaced —that to excise anything or introduce anything
means to disturb what the Convention finally agreed-to
as the best result of their joint councils. Anil so it was
laid before the people, who pronounced it good, and
accepted it in whole and in every part. No man can say
one clause was more voted upon than another; the
Referendum affirmed every word and line and clause as
well as the Bill in its entirety. No higher act was
possible to any self-governing people—affixing the seal to
the Constitution not only of their own choice but the
fabric of their own loom.
* » * •
Why is it that people shrink from their own creed, and
desert their own policy ? What kind of Australian pal riots
are they who, at one time foremost in shouting “ trust
the people! ” are now loudest in decrying the Referendum
and inviting outsiders—men who are certainly not of their
own people—to lay violent hands on the people's work.
They resemble a discontented partner wl»o should hire a
stranger to forge in the partnership-deed alterations to
which the other partners would not consent.
What is claimed for the Imperial Parliament is the right
to veto' the people’s Referendum. Are the Australian
people abject and spiritless enough to submit to that?
One cannot believe it. If they are, then they have not
emerged from tutelage and are unfit for national existence
or a national career. Are not our provincial Parliaments
bound by the Referendum ? Could they have refused to
pass the message transmitting to Her Majesty the Com-
monwealth Bill for formal enactment? Of course not.
Then, if the provincial Parliaments, who represent the
people, are so bound, why not the British Parliament
which neither represents nor is accountable to us ? Sup-
pose such a referendum had been taken in England,
would not the British Parliament have been bound?
Undoubtedly it would. Then why not bound by the Aus-
tralian Referendum in a matter which solely concerns
Australia? Is it said that an Australian Referendum is
in some way of inferior value to a similar vote in England,
or that the Australian people are not quite responsible for
their actions, and must be controlled by some power not
themselves, which they cannot call to account as they can
their own Parliaments.
* * « ,
The Imperial Parliament has no more right to alter our
Federal Constitution, without the people’s direct consent,
than to tax us. Whether it looks so or not, one is tvranny
as much as the other. Surely it is as much coercion to
compulsorily fasten upon us a law-court we have said we
don’t want as to impose a tax. No one objects to the
Imperial authorities civilly remonstrating, and pointing
out that both tax and law-court are blessings in disguise,
and asking us to reconsider. But they must not attempt
to force either upon us. We will have no Imperial legis-
lation either for taxation or law-courts, without repre-
sentation. Suppose the Commonwealth Bill had pro-
vided for an elective Governor-General, or even for com-
plete separation ? That is the strongest case which can
be put. Can it be contended for an instant that the
Imperial Parliament would change or amend that without
the consent of Australia? They might send the Bill back
and respectfully ask us to think it over again. But no-
more. And if we insisted, the Constitutional position
born of the American revolutionary war is that they must
yield. So, now. If, for example,'they think a Court of
Appeal in England better for us than one of our own at
our doors, they may say so, and press it upon us. If they
regard Australia as a “ nation,” we should be consulted
as befits the dignity and self-respect of a nation. The
boon—if they think it one -must not be forced upon us
with a high hand, as though Australia were still a
dependency. If, unfortunately, it is forced, we need not
refuse the mutilated Constitution. Though its symmetry
were marred with many amendments, we should probably
accept it so long as the elementary bond of union were
left, for we desire to be united. But we should never rest
till the wrong be righted. The venue would perhaps be
changed to the Federal Parliament, but there right would
be done sooner or later. Imperial interference would give
us some things that might take much settling. None the
less because of the seeds of bitterness and disappointment
which would follow in its train, Australian Federation
would be inaugurated with a grievance and cursed with a
source of friction only to be cured by excision.
••» ' •
Mr. Chamberlain is not a real Imperialist. He is an
overgrown municipal statesman. Recent events in South
Africa have given him a prominence and an importance
agreeable to his-ambition but beyond his wisdom. His
career has outpaced his capacity.' He does not realise
Australia. He forgets it is not a dependency or Crown
colony. If he remembers this he is seeking to usurp our
self-governing functions or flout the verdict of the Aus-
tralian people. In his speech on the introduction of the
Commonwealth Bill he spoke of Australia as “ the new
nation.” Does he comprehend the significance of his own
expression? If he does, what sort of a nation—self-
governing nation—will he think us if we tamely submit
to be shorn of the power of choosing our own courts and
dispensing final justice within our borders? Does he
know of any well-ordered nation—any free community


worthy of nationhood—in ancient or modern times, which
nhHieated its primary function of doing justice, or
suffered itself to be despoiled of that power ? Nationality
is a sham without it. It would be Home Rule with one
arm in a sling. Justice would come to us ma, foreign
", ar b The fact is Mr. Chamberlain is held in thrall by his
fears of Irish Home Rule. He aims to treat Australia
like another Ireland,
» * * *
We have control of our own land and sea forces. We
have entire control of the civil forces—a condition which
Mr Chamberlain himself in a memorable letter Mr.
Parnell called “tantamount to separation. Yet
weare not to have control of our own Justice!
We are to be trusted with all the forces of peace and
war but not to adjust disputes between man and man.
Are' Australians going to accept that stigma to bow
their shoulders for the brand of that scarlet letter ? You
that are unjust, say these so-called Imperialists, be unjust
still We are federating to hold up our heads amongst
the nations. How are we to do it without blushing for
the disability Mr. Chamberlain wishes the Imperial Par-
liament to place us under ? _
Our Federal self-government is embodied in the Com-
monwealth Bill. The only question is, how much will the
Imperial Parliament filch from us 1 The loss, it is a com-
fort to reflect, can only be temporary. A day of reckon-
ing and amends shall come.
J. H. SYMON.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Melbourne’s Great DrunK.
A man who was there writes :
Melbourne went ten different kinds of mad on the night
when the news of the relief of Mafeking arrived, and
enormous was the drunk and delirium thereof. ihe
hysterical character of the French and the dignified calm of
the Anglo-Saxon are favorite themes in this country, but
l’aris never more completely threw its dignity to the
winds, or went more wildly out of its degenerate mind—
not since the days of the first Napoleon. All the tenth-
rate pugs and their hangers-on, lavishly decked-out with
small Union Jacks and other evidences of hysteria, raged
up and down. Black Petek Felix, the alleged champion,
led a select band of Jingoes with a Jubilee medal thing on
his manly bosom, and behind followed his colored brother
Pomi'ey and other lesser lights. Further in the rear came
the scum of the boxing halls, mingled with an
assortment of miscellaneous larrikins, all waving the
British flag. Wild, excited Fatmen and gilded youths
from Toorak cavorted down the main streets, embracing'
persons thev wouldn’t touch with a pitchfork at ordinary
times Staid respectable business men who used to wax
sarcastic at the attitude of the French over Dreyfus and
the hysteria of the Yankees during the Spanish war drove
their fists through their top hats and shrieked by them-
selves. Red-eyed youths roared inarticulately with open
mouths dropping saliva. Respectable girls and respect-
able married women permitted themselves to be mauled
about by strange men and thrown over their shoulders
with much display of form, and the raving multitudes
gave three cheers for their legs and their underwear. An
a ,r ed parson bounced down tiie hill of Collins-street yell-
ing like a motherless calf and leading a band of men who
charged like Dervishes. The venerable divine had a
small flag sticking through the ventilator of his bell-
topper, and another Union Jack tucked across his
bingey as a sort of false' vest. A third flag he
swung ecstatically around his head, and when
a cynical onlooker advised him to have his white beard
dyed red, white and blue, the shepherd rushed off with a
loud whoop, apparently to act on the advice. Men who
would pass any two doctors paid half-crowns for penny
flags. Sedate ‘ fathers of families threatened to wreck
fancy-goods shops when the proprietors plaintively
stated that they had already sold out all their tin trum-
pets at a thousand per cent, profit. As early as 2 p.m. an
elderly magnate of the Stock Exchange, with a seven-
guinea suit on and a 80s. belltopper, was pounding a
kerosene-tin with his umbrella in Collins-street. Another
magnate was calling to a procession 300 strong to stop
and let him shout 300 sixpenny drinks all in one act. The
procession had an appointment down street, but it said
it would come back for the drinks. When it came
back its benefactor was still there, but no one
would have given 2s. 6d. for bis suit and his
bell-topper together. He had been trying to stand on his
middle-aged and wealthy head in the gutter with his
coat-tails hanging about his ears—and the gutter was
wet. The stockbroker was the boss drunk and goat and
giddy ox of the carnival. He was everywhere. He led
the processions. He ran mad in Collins-street as distin-
guished from the bagman who ran mad in Swanston-
street, and the Chow who was delirious in Lilly-Buck-
stleet. He raged in public places, red with champagne
loyalty, blatant, lost to every sense of decorum and all
respect for his high Paris hat. He sang push songs,
and beat on banks and insurance - offices with his
gamp till it was a wreck. At first it was a British
flag with which he advertised his emotion) but as
the day wore on and the liquor bit in, he seemed to lose
discrimination, and he shook any flag that he could grab.
But, perhaps, the most wonderful sight of the afternoon
was a six-foot citizen of high repute, in a suit that had
been glorious in the morning, but was now a complica-
tion and a wreck. He stood in a high place as the crowd
flowed by, gazing out upon chaos with red, sightless eyes,
ever and anon tooting an imbecile toot on a crumpled
penny trumpet that he had bought for two half-crowns.
He had lost his identity and his knowledge of earthly
things in the outbreak, but he never forgot his trumpet,
and a sane crowd might have laughed itself to
death at the unimaginable incongruity of the tableau
formed by that immense personage and his little tin
horn. At night the crowd had grown till it
choked the streets. Burning crackers were thrown in the
faces of the dense crowd, singly and by the hundred,
colored lights were blazing everywhere, sticks and gamps
were flourishing, and wild “pushes” were charging up
and down, bearing on their shoulders soldiers filled with
beer or rakish signboards advertising the Throne and
Person. The fair sex raged with the rest—girls of all
ages, from 16 to 03. One well-dressed girl, accompanied
by a parent or guardian, was brandishing her umbrella
and singing “ Soldiers of the Queen ” in Bourke-street.
“Where’s your colors, Sis?” yelled one of a drove of
youths,, and there, under the fierce white light of the
electric orb, the damsel snatched up her skirts to her
knees, displaying a garter banded with red, white and
blue—“ There’s my colors ! ” she cried hysterically,
and the lads yelled, and joining hands danced around
her for a moment and then went howling awaj'.
And so the night went on, with a sleet of crumpled hats
and dead crackers and cigar ash and lost coat-tails
driving down Bourke-street. Some pubs, were still open
at 4on Sunday morning. The Law was paralysed. Even
the railway staff wasn’t so sober as it might have been
and was despatching trains with blind luck and letting
them take their chance. The last this writer saw was a
large citizen watching his bell-topper which he had put
on the tram-line, and cackling a drunken cackle as he
waited to see the tram-car go over it. He didn’t even
know the cars had stopped three hours previously.

The “ Abolition-of-Appeal ” Myth.
Sydney Telegraph, with its neat knack of
anti - Federal misrepresentation, until the other
day, persisted in referring to the Common-
wealth Bill as containing a clause enacting
“ the abolition of the right of appeal to
the Privy Council” (leading article, May 16) —and
the Telegraph knows very well that the Bill does
no such thing. Here is the clause :
74. No appeal shall be permitted to the Queen in Coun-
cil in any matter involving the interpretation of this Con-
stitution or of the Constitution of a Stats, unless the pub-
lic interests of some part of Har Majesty’s Dominions,
other than the Commonwealth or a State, are involved.
Except as provided in this section, this Constitution
shall not impair any right which the Queen may be
pleased to exercise, by virtue of Her Royal Prerogative, to
grant special leave of appeal from the High Court to Her
Majesty in Council. But the Parliament may make laws
limiting the matters in which such leave may be asked.
The clause thus deals with three classes of ap-
peals—
First, matters involving the interpretation of the
Federal Constitution or the Constitution of a State where
Imperial interests are not involved. In this case appeal
is not. permitted.
Second, matters involving the interpretation of the
Federal Constitution or the Constitution of a State where
Imp.rial interests are involved, fn this case appeal is
permitted.
Third, all other matters. In this case appeal is per-
mitted where the Federal Parliament does not make a
law to limit it.
The Commonwealth Bill, then, abolishes nothing
at all, since (in the first and second classes) the
Federal Constitution and the States under the
Federal Constitution are its own creation. Feder-
ally speaking, they do not exist, and their rights
do not exist, until the Bill becomes law and the
Bill cannot abolish what is not is existence. As
to the third class, the Bill gives to the Federal Par-
liament power to limit appeals ; but the Bill does
not even limit them. Unless the Federal Parlia-
ment limits them the existing rights of appeal re-
main intact—so far as the Bill is concerned. And
even the Federal Parliament cannot abolish— it
can only limit— the Queen’s prerogative remaining
unchanged, if dormant. So to talk about the
Commonwealth Bill “ abolishing the right of
appeal to the Privy Council ” is first, nonsense ;
secondly, misrepresentation ; and, thirdly, some-
thing which ought to make the anti-Federal tele-
graph darned well ashamed of itself.

Forrest’s 57th New Attitude.
Bigjohn Forrest the other day called Westralia’s
Parliament together to propose that the Federal
Constitution be sent to the people just as it stands,
for their acceptance or rejection. Therefore
Bigjohn has got back to the place where lie stood
about two years ago when he promised to send
the Bill to the people and let them decide. After
that he got a new Bill drafted and promised to
send both measures to the people, but he proposed
it half-heartedly and the idea was rejected. Then
he appealed in a weak tone of voice to the British
Government to enforce his new Bill—the one
which his own Parliament had rejected and his
own people hadn’t accepted. And now he gets
Dack to his original standpoint. But lie gets back
very feebly, and Bigjohn’s present principles look
about as hazy as they ever were. This is the
telegraphic summary of his speech :
The Premier said there was a feeling throughout the
colony that they should not be isolated in sentiment or
interest from the rest of Australia. They could depend
that the Bill would act adversely in some ways, and
advantageously in other ways. The people desired a
closer union with their fellow-colonists to build up a
nation indissoluble. He would tell the people that he
would vote for the Bill, but he could not see any advantage
to come from it for West Australia for some time to come ;
in fact, he thought there would be many difficulties to
overcome. Under Federation, Freetrade would be aimed
at, and the tendency for taxation would be on property.
It was not so bad a Bill that he could say that it should
not be referred to the people. He considered Parliament
had a perfect right to deal with the question of the referen-
dum as it liked. He did not like ttie referendum, as he
was not used to it; but the people clamored for it, so he
would not block it. He thought they would hold their
own under Federation, owing to their great resources.
John the Wobbler calls Parliament together and
asks it to send the Federal Bill to a Referendum
at which its acceptance is practically certain. And
he tells Parliament that he doesn’t see any imme-
diate advantage to be gained by the Bill, and he
dislikes Referendums ; also, that it has a perfect
right to refuse the Referendum if it pleases. So
John’s Upper House will probably take the hint
and refuse it. If the old man had said that he
would dissolve the Assembly if the Bill was
thrown out, and stump the country on it, and if
he failed to carry it again he would wade in to re-
form the dreadful Upper House, and everything
else failing he would resign his billet, the Council
would probably have taken that hint also. The
Council dislikes trouble and riot. But John isn’t
that kind of Federalist—in fact, he isn’t a very
definite kind of anything.

The New Sydney that is to he.
Almost the first gleam of light that has ever been
shed on the decayed, moth-eaten, insolvent and
utterly incapable municipal government of Sydney
is to be found in the report of the municipal con-
ference which recently sat in inquest on the
remains of the city politics. The conference, con-
sisting of representatives of both city and subur-
ban municipalities, proposes these 12 most valuable
ideas:
(1) That Sydney, Alexandria, Annandale, Ashfield,
Balmain, Botany, North Botany, Burwood, Canterbury,
Camperdown, Concord, Darlington, Drummoyne, linfleld,
Erskineville, Five Dock, Glebe, Hunter’s Hill, Hurstville,
Kogarah, Lane Cove, Leichhardt, Manly, Marrickville,
Marsfield, Mosman, Newtown, North Sydney, Paddington,
Petersham, Randwick, Redfern, Rockdale, Rookwood,
Ryde, St. Peters, Strathfield, VaUcluse, Waterloo,
Waverley, Willoughby and Woollahra (42 in all) shall be
absolutely amalgamated under one Council.
(2) Council to consist of 75 members. Area to be
divided according to population into 25 wards with three
aldermen apiece.
(3) Whole council to be re-elected every three years,
every adult who has been for six months a tenant in the
municipal area to have one vote. No plural voting.
(4) Mayor to be elected annually in the same way as the
aldermen.
(5) Rates to be levied on land values only.
(6) Council to have power to seize upon and sell or let
property to recover arrears of rates.
(7) Railway Commissioners, Gas Co., Hydraulic Power
Co., Telephone Department, electric lighting companies
and the like to pay rates for the use of the city streets.
(8) Council’s accounts to be subject to annual Govern-
ment audit, and any deficiency certified to by the auditor
to be covered next year by a special deficiency rate.
(9) Council to solely control: Roads and streets, licens-
ing of vehicles and control of street traffic, water-works,
water-supply, sewerage and drainage, gas-supply, supply
of electricity, hydraulic power, tramways, docks, ferries,
fire stations, prevention of fires on land and in harbor,
markets, parks and open spaces, cemeteries, free libraries,
common lodging-houses, night-shelters, public health,
destruction of garbage and house-refuse, abattoirs, public
baths and wash-houses, housing of the poor, public pawn-
offices, municipal art galleries ; and have power to acquire
and carry on any of these services, and in addition have
power to resume slum areas, improve and widen streets,
dedicate parks, and acquire land for other public purposes,
to re-sell or lease any portion thereof ; to issue deben-
tures and negotiate 'loans for the purpose of defraying
expenses; to have power to proclaim improvement
areas, and impose betterment taxes within such
areas: to establish labor bureaux and registration
offices’. To establish a metropolitan statistical depart-
ment. To compel registration of ownership of land. To
license hotels, wine-shops, and places of public amuse-
ment. To regulate and test weights and measures. To
regulate and license noxious trades. To regulate and
license dairies and milk-supply. To license auctioneers
and issue all trade licenses, and all powers exercised
within the area by the Board of Health, City Improve-
ment Board, Metropolitan Transit Commission, Tramway
Department, Trustees of Parks, such branches of the
Harbors and Rivers Department as control the foreshores
and docks, such branches of the Roads and Bridges
Department as control roads and bridges within the area,
such branches of the Public Works Department as control
the design and execution of works of water supply and
sewerage within the area, and all powers exercis-
able both within and without the area by the
Water and Sewerage Board and Fire Brigades Boa.rd.
(10) Assets and liabilities of the whole 42 municipalities,
and the estimated deficiency of yearly revenue from trans-
ferred services, to be determined by an independent
commission, and be takerfover by the council; for a period
of ten years from the establishment of the council the
Government to grant it an annual subsidy equal to such
estimated deficiency. . .
ill) Council to have power to issue debentures tor the
conversion of existing liabilities, and fertile due exercise
of the various powers vested in it.
(I D 411 Crown lands within the area not immediately
required for Government purposes to be vested m the
council.
A few meek and spineless alternatives are sug-
gested for patchingtlicold system insteadoi m king
a new one, but the above 12 propositions represent
the brains of the new plan. They are all ideas
which The Bulletin has long been advocating.
In two leading articles published on 24th June
and Ist July, 1899, this paper suggested exactly
the system of State audit above suggested,'
the deficiency rate, the exact, franchise above
suggested, the triennial election, the election of
the mayor by the people, the land-value taxation,
the method of recovering rates, and almost the
exact powers which the above report proposes en-
trusting to the new Council. Barring the fact
that this paper proposed to amalgamate 41 munici-
palities into one council with 80 members instead
of 42 into a council with 75 members, and that it
didn’t propose sticking up the State for a subsidy,
this paper is proud to say that it planned out
practically every item of the above scheme nearly
a year ago. The pity is that it is proposed to sub-
mit such an admirable project to the 42 weak, in-
competent and utterly discredited councils that
they may judge it according to their wisdom —
when the whole trouble is that they haven’t any.
What is wanted is a Government that will blow
the 42 councils on one side and submit the question
to the people by referendum, and let them decide.

More About a Certain Tasmanian
Railway.
Tasmania’s Great Western Railway Syndicate has
of late been humming a little louder than usual on
its island, if such a thing be possible. The syndi-
cate was floated so very far back in the dark ages
that no one exactly remembers the date now. It
was to build and own and work for its own profit
the Great Western Railway, and to receive from
the State a stupendous land-grant of 1,000,000
acres or so for doing it. It never raised any
capital or turned a sod of its railway in all the
years that followed. The syndicates never did a
farthing’s-worth of real work on the line, and the
company they tried to float didn’t float any better
than a ton of lead. Their concession ran out with
the grey procession of years, and was renewed,
and still they did nothing. They started to
demand “further assistance” from the State.
The people who wouldn’t put their own money into
their own cat asked that the State should ad-
vance them £200,000 as well as the land-grant, and
proposed that the State should guarantee their
debentures for about as large a sum as it could
have built the whole line for itself. In other
words, that the State should find the whole legiti-
mate cost of the line and the co. should get the
profits and the 1,000,000 acre land-grant as well.
This great scheme didn’t come off, but one almost
equally weird is now under actual and serious con-
sideration. It is as follows
(1) That the Tasmanian Govt, shall build the first sec-
tion of the Great Western Railway itself at a cost of
£200,000, and lease it to the company (which the promo-
ters have never managed to form as yet) for £7OOO a year.
The unformed and non-existent co. offers no guarantee
whatever tha it will ever pay even one month’s rent. It
hasn’t managed, in all the years it has been fooling
around, to raise enough capital to do that little thing.
The Government is to have the promoters’ bare promise
that, if it builds a bit of railway for them, the fragment
won’t be left on its hands.
(2) The co. (as yet unformed) is to put up £150,000 capi-
tal (as yet it hasn’t put up 150,000 pence). That is to say,
though the whole affair is the co.’s speculation for the
co.’s private profit, the co. proposes that it shall only find
£150,000, while the Government puts up £200,000. And
it offers no guarantee that it will ever find the £150,000,
or that it won’t all go in first robbers’ profits if it is found,
or that one penny of it (if found) will go in legitimate
railway-building.
(3) Also, the co. which has so far failed to raise any
capital or any debenture money, is to raise £400,000 by
the sale of debentures.
(4) And the co., in consideration of its promise to put
£150,000 of its own money in the scheme, is to get a grant
of land worth, probably, £500,000.
(5) Also, the promoters, who have hitherto failed to
keep any of their engagements, or to raise any capital,
want the Tasmanian Parliament to commit itself to the
expenditure of the above-mentioned £200,000 before they
find any of their money. The party which has failed in
all its engagements demands that the one which has
always kept its engagements shall “ show its boua-Jides ”
in the matter.
Once more : Will some sane Tasmanian legislator
rise up and demand that the State railway depart-
ment shall have first show in this matter ? Before
the State throws away 1,000,000 acres to induce an
uncertain and bungling syndicate to build the
G.W. Railway will it set its own Railway Com-
missioner and its best land expert to consider
whether its own Railway Department couldn’t
profitably build and work the G.W. line provided
the said Department received an endowment of
1,000,000 acres, and applied the rents therefrom to
supplement the earnings of the line ? This is a
question which should be considered before any
land-grant railway concession is given, and if
Tasmania’s Parliament doesn’t know enough to see
the point it is even a worse fool than it looks.
“Loyalty”—at the Ratepayers’ Expense.
The action of Melb. City Council in preferring an
English tender for electrical supplies to a German
—though there was no imputation that the German
goods are inferior, and the German tender was
£4793 lower—is a beautiful example of perverted
Jingoism —at the ratepayers’ expense. What
Melb. City Councillor would do as much for
Britain if his own private interests were involved?
There is not one. If the facts are correctly re-
ported, it is a gross malversation of public funds—
just because they are public funds, and the
money thrown away comes out of the public
pocket, not out of the pocket of the councillor
who sit at the board. Why should not Australia
deal with Germany just as with England? She
does, where private interests are concerned.
During the last decade the percentage of Austra-
lian imports from Germany has increased,
while the percentage from Britain has de-
creased. And why not, if it pays Australia?
This country hopes to live, to a large ex-
tent, on its commerce. Why should it
cut its own commercial throat by penal-
ising nations other than Britain ? To what
purpose do we hold exhibitions of our products,
and send specimens of our products to exhibitions
elsewhere, if not to promote trade ? And how
much return are we likely to get from Britain for
sueh superabundant “ loyalty ” ? Will Britain pay
Id. per lb. more for Australian meat than for
American ? Will she prefer Australian wool
at a greater cost to Argentine of the same
quality? The question is too ridiculous for
answer. Besides, why single out Germany for
“loyalist ” affront? Does not Britain trade with
Germany—on precisely the same terms as she
trades with Australia? Are not our German
colonists worth fully as much as the British?
Really, the action of Melb. City Council—always
provided that it is correctly reported—caps the
climax of municipal stupidity, and worse than
stupiditv— at the ratepayers’ expense.

Machine-made Patriotism.
Shortly after the despatch of troops from N.S.W.
to the seat of war in S’Africa was first determined
oil, a prominent departmental official invited the
head-masters of the Ist and 2nd class public-
schools to meet in his room for the purpose of
establishing a teachers’ branch of the Patriotic
Fund. The step was reputedly taken, not so
much with the mere sanction as with the active
co-operation of the Minister for Public Instruc-
tion. Certain resolutions were drawn up and pro-
visionally adopted. Public-school teach ers were
shortly thereafter invited by circular to meet in
rlie Girls’ High School, and to them as a bodv the
resolutions were formally submitted. The writer
has been unable to obtain a copy of them, but their
dominating note amounted to the proposition that
the salaried teachers of the State schools through-
out IN.S.W. should, one and all, be invited to con-
tribute, either monthly or by single donations
which would run to a capitalised equivalent of a
substantial monthly subscription, to the fund. Is
it needful to add that in the circumstances under
which the meeting was called together, the reso-
lutions were carried unanimously ? The adver-
tised records of the general fund show that as a
result of the machinery thus set in motion, £2OOO
has been collected up to date, the second con-
tribution being thus announced in the morning
papers of 18th May
Second instalment from the Minister, includ-
ing- inspectors, officers, and teachers, De-
partment of Public Instruction (making a
total of £2000) ..£l,OOO 0 0
Particularly note the word “instalment.” Masters
have been told off in different districts to visit a
certain number of schools, to see that the process
is kept continuously alive, and, if any particular
teacher is found not sufficiently responsive to the
holy obligation here sketched out, the hint is
promptly given that the line of wisdom in the
personal sense would be found in making an early
start. The public may accept it, if they like,
that the public-school teachers of N.S.W. are im-
bued, beyond the general body of their fellow-
citizens, with a deep sense of patriotism, and that
a set of functionaries, whose salaries work out to
an average of £l2O per annum, are building up
these generous instalments of their own free oice.
But more probably the business, whoever may be
responsible for it, works out to nothing less than a
peculiarly mean piece of blackmail. The Bul-
letin knoivs that many of the contributions
are sent in with feelings of loathing and
contempt, and merely because, in the dependent
position of the contributors, they feel that it is a
choice between submission and either being frozen
for once and all to some lower grado in the service,
for which the ingenuities of officialdom can easily
find independent cxcuso; or, perchance, on some
flimsy pretext, fired out of it altogether. It will
bo the simplest thing in the world to send forth a
smug contradiction to the case, as hero set
out--the simplest thing in the world to deny that
the faintest hint savoring of pressure has been
used. There are, however, numberless methods of
conveying threats otherwise than by spoken words.
The question stands wholly apart from sympathy
with, or antipathy to, the causes which have made
for the war with the South African Republic. Nor
would it be less than an impertinence to offer a
word of comment either upon action or motive, as
regards those members of the community whose
views of the situation prompt them voluntarily to
contribute to patriotic or other forms pf war fund.
But the pretence that tho free initiative of the
public school teachers of New South Wales has
anything to do with the enormous tax of £2OOO,
with instalments yet to come, departmentally
levied upon them for the purpose of subduing tho
Transvaal, runs to an experiment upon human
credulity which one should hesitate to propound
even to the corps of marines.

The most astonished and bitterly-aggrieved organ
in all Australasia at ChamrerlainG partial for-
saking of his proposed amendments was Brisbane
Courier. This plastic journal had been vigor-
ously defending Chamberlain’s would-be altera-
tions for weeks ; had been whooping joyously for
the interests of the Empire; had been patting
Oily Jimmy Dickson on the back and hailing
him as the First of Patriots. And then came
the incredible tidings that Chamberlain was
actually “turning dog” on the Empire. There
was but one thing left and that was to castigate
Chamberlain. Wherefore, the Courier set to
work and showed conclusively that Joseph’s first
attitude (in mangling the Bill that expressed the
will of the Australian people) was perfectly cor-
rect ; that his subsequent decision not to mangle
it quite so badly was a most damnable proceed-
ing ; and that Delegate Dickson was the only
consistent champion of the Empire. Listen to the
mournful conclusion :
The wise man among the delegates and the man
who most deserves well of his constituents is the man who
has been boycotted for his willingness to have the clause
(74) expunged. For the working of clause 74 as it now
stands we can only trust to that strong common sense and
irrepressible recognition of justice, etc. etc.
Isn’t it touching ? And, in spite of it all, Bob
Philp didn’t get ennobled, the Courier didn’t
get any kudos for Imperial patriotism and James
Dickson is still plain Oily Jimmy— and likely to
stay so !
If the present Queensland Govt, is as good as its
word—if its Premier didn’t simply lose his head
at a public festivity and run wild—then it is going
to put up a record which will make even the old
Boodle Governments fade into insignificance :
At the Stock Exchange smoke concert held to-night in
honor of the relief of Mafeking, the Premier, in replying
to the toast of the Ministry, acknowledged the excellent
support accorded himself and his colleagues by the mining
industry, and the great advantage accruing to the country
by the ‘energetic development of the mineral resources.
He said he might take them into his confidence in
stating that the Ministry was prepared to meet any legiti-
mate mining proposition which covered the building
of railways to places where the nature of the country
made the risk too great for the Govt. The Govt, had
under consideration proposals for five or six private lines,
and was inclined to extend approval to any private syndi-
cate that would come to Queensland to build railways to
open up mines which it was undesirable for the Govt, to
undertake on account of the risk. Further, it had been
abundantly proved that private enterprise could work
mining railways cheaper than the Govt.
Considering that none of Queensland’s privately-
owned mining railways has really got to work yet,
Premier Philp’s. “abundant proof ” of their extra
cheapness requires some explanation. But no one
was expected to be very accurate on the night of
the Mafeking drunk.

The withdrawal of Dr. Abrahams, representative
of the Hebrew churches, from the Vic. Royal
Commission on religious instruction in State
schools should sober the zealots who dream of
drafting what, if successful, would really be a new
brand of State religion. When years ago the
Mikado sent a commission to Europe to collect
the points of the various creeds professed there so
that he might, by picking and choosing, produce a
new, up-to-date religion for his Japanese subjects,
a mighty burst of laughter went up from the
Christian churches; but the Mikado was no more
ridiculous than are the doctrinal enthusiasts who
constitute tho Vic. Royal Commission. If the
members sat to establish a gamut of juvenile
