(Saturday Afternoon.)
Last week was largely a week of fading
away and shutting up in Sydney theatrical circles.
Charles Holloway was the first to go; he played
the “Shamrock and the Rose” for the last time
on Saturday night, and then hauled down his flag
and left the Lyceum tenantless. C. Holloway
had held the kopje valiantly for 60 weeks, and he
made an orderly and solvent retreat carrying, it
is alleged, very considerable profits with him.
The enemy, if there was one, didn’t manage to
overtake Billolloway’s relative as he retired. The
departing manager gave many good shows wherein
the badness of the drama was disguised by the
goodness of the scenery and the stage-manage-
ment, and he kept the Lyceum clean and respect-
able, and reasonably free from the incursion of
pea-nuts and other vegetables, whose public con-
sumption should be discouraged by all good
citizens.
** * *
The second week of his season finds
Victor, the Illusionist, hanging out the pale
shingle of a blameless life to moderate audiences at
Sydney Palace. Victor is a curly-headed man ;
most illusionists somehow appear to be curly-
headed, but whether their hair curls naturally or
not this writer is unaware. Also, he has found
that most curly-headed men are illusive. Victor
is alleged to be only 21 years of age, and a self-
taught genius in that profession which consists so
largely m borrowing the audience’s hat and then
publicly finding baby-linen in it, and on that
basis he is a marvel. But his tricks are
mostly those of the late lamented Dante (the
famous Burnt Woman and the finding of
the dinner in the drain-pipe among them),
and he does them almost as well as
Dante but not quite so decisively. As a
ventriloquist he is excellent, but ventriloquism i 3
rather a weary art. His best and neatest perform-
ance is probably when he makes the slim, non-
committal-looking boy lie on nothing in the
atmosphere. Victor is rather a wearisome patterer.
His remark as he seizes each new piece of
apparatus, “ I now take a box ” or “I now take a
sheet of paper ” gets on the nerves. He is always
“ now taking ” a house or a tank or an elephant or
a sleep or a bath or something, and, though he
never takes anything that doesn’t belong to. him,
the expression becomes monotonous as time rolls
°n. The Bulletin hasn’t quite made up its mind
about Victor, but if he was not such a close
mutator of Dante his prospects would be better.
There is to be a great military and official night
at the Palace this (Thursday) evening.
** * *
Everybody’s old friend, Bland Holt, is due
at Sydney Royal on Saturday with “ Women and
Wine”—a play which is new to Sydney. The
name sounds attractive, at all events, though the
British are not a wine-drinking race, and “ Women
and Beer ” would be more accurate. When this
issue reaches the public eye, therefore, the Brough
season will have only two more nights to run, and
Australia’s most reliable and consistent purveyor
of pleasant comedy will then leave for some place
whose gain will be Sydney’s loss. An Act of Par-
liament to keep Brough’s eo. compulsorily in the
country (except when Mrs. Brough is allowed to
fo for a holiday on heavy bail, and leaving her
est clothes behind as security for her reappear-
ance), with a State guarantee that the takings
won’t fall below a certain figure, is urgently
needed. Lyne’s title, and the official reports
about the infected area, and Fitzgibbon’s poems,
and Parson Boyce on the annual Drink Bill
are still with us, but they don’t compensate
for Brough. The last days of the departing
show have been tearful and scrappy, the public
supplying the grief, and Brough the scraps. Most
of the best pieces on the co. ’s list have had a one
or two nights’ revival, and the catalogue ends, as
usual, with “Dandy Dick” and “Niobe.” Brough
mostly winds up with “Niobe.” The story of the
ancient Greek lady who cursed at large on the
house-top of a modem London villa, having pre-
sumably got up there through the hole left for the
gas-man, seems to do more to alleviate the public
sorrow on a last night than any other production.
* # * *-
The massive Nance O’Neil is closing her
season at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, with a revival
of “Magda,” that drama wherein the prodigal
daughter comes back clothed in over £2OO worth
of raiment, and is forgiven conditionally by the
broad and bitter parent who threw her out years
previously without any visible reason for his vio-
lence. The new version is better than the old
one, chiefly by reason of the substitution of
Thomas Kingston for Clay Clements as the pastor.
Magda relents towards the pastor, especially
when he tells her how her father became a harm-
less lunatic after her departure and a public
gatherer of caterpillars on the highway; and at
the end it is left in doubt whether she marries him
or goes back to the stage to live once more by
selling her top-note to an appreciative public.
The author seems to have purposely left the mat-
tsr in doubt. And the old pastor was so distinctly
a part of the old, stuffy, drab, commonplace life
—the sleepy, respectable existence with tne dread-
ful horsehair sofa in it—that the possibility of
Magda wedding him was too awful. Kingston
looks a cheerful possibility, at all events, and not
a well-meaning hearse. Harry Plimmer is now
the dull lawyer, who was the father of Magda’s in-
fant, and he is also an improvement on his
predecessor. The man with whom Magda goes
astray shouldn’t look utterly stodgy. These re-
marks are in the nature of a farewell to the co.,
which is leaving for Melbourne to take possession
of the biggest theatre in Australia—the resurrected
Alick, now also called Her Majesty’s. William-
son s new opera co. comes to Sydney in its stead,
and starts on Saturday with good old “Pinafore.”
** * *
“ East Lynne ” is still bathed in woe at
Sydney Criterion, and a tearful audience, mostly
women,. takes Archibald Carlyle as its model.
Part of it wonders if it will ever have the chance
to marry such a heavenly man, and the other half
repines because some such weary saint didn’t come
its way earlier. Mrs. Henry Wood’s heroes are
practically all built on the same pattern; they are
tall, calm, holy, dignified, conscientious, and look
at you with a mild eye while they utter platitudes
in a frock-coat. None of them are athletic, or are
described as having any particular muscle, and
they never do anything wrong ; they are merely
tall and holy and mild and dignified. Mrs.
Henry W.’s tall heroes are ever mentioned as
taking a bath, but if they did they almost cer-
tainly did it with their clothes on lest they should
shock their own modesty. Archibald Carlyle is
one of the tallest and most sacred bores in Mrs.
W.’s museum, and any person willing to subscribe
towards a society for his suppression is invited to
communicate with this office.
** * «
At Sydney Tivoli the latest production of
the silent Martinettis is a tragedy-burlesque in
which the star dies pathetically while turning
somersaults. Strange that so clever a man as
Martinetti should always make the same fatal
mistake of drawing his things out too long and
thin. Lion Comique Bain continues to roar his
cheerful parody on the Preoccupied Mendicant.
The much-boomed “Dainty Irene Franklin,” with
the famous songful paws, is not up to boom stan-
dard. She glides on to the stage and reminds one
of a charming little school-girl who has lost her-
self. She sings nicely and refinedly, and makes
quiet, pretty gestures; but her voice is not un-
usual m any way.
** * *
Melbourne thus far has taken kindly to
J. C. Williamson’s new theatre, so the conversion
of the shabby “ Aleck ” into Her Majesty’s looks
like one of those deeds which should have been
done long ago. Refurnished, painted, gilded,
carpeted, and with a lot of new light on the im-
provements inside and outside, Her Majesty’s
is already an A 2 place. Further improvements
will qualify it for the A 1 degree. The whisky
that maketh glad the heart of man between the
acts is not obtainable on these unlicensed pre-
mises, but seeing that the same objection applies
to certain other theatres, where liquid refresh-
ment spells insanity and death, and no reasoning
mortal ever dreams of buying a drink, Her
Majesty’s is not seriously affected by the lack of
bar room. Women like it all the better on that
account. They get more male society and less
aroma of cloves and coffee-beans during the
evening. Meanwhile, the present scribe regrets
to have discovered a pub. next door to the pit
entrance.
* • * *
Comic opera-cwn-biograph has filled the
bill these two weeks. On Saturday a revival of
“Pirates of Penzance” brought Miss Winston-
Weir into Melbourne notice, and the lady’s high
soprano voice was thankfully encored. She walks
through her task in a colorless style, without any
pretensions to take first prize in a beauty-show,
but she warbles “Poor Wandering One” more
effectively than any previous Mabel in Australia
—bar one. Years ago Frances Saville sang the
part at the Princess’s, and the artist who can beat
her record must have a rather sweeter voice than
Miss Winston-Weir’s. All the same, the new-
comer is a charming singer. Wallace Brownlowas
the Pirate King, Kenningham as the humane ap-
prentice, and < leo. Lauri, whose major-general is a
broad caricature of Lord Roberts, come next to
the chorus in order of merit. The chorus is more
than half the battle in “The Pirates.” Miss
Dorothy Vane has been doomed to impersonate a
large, unprepossessing person aged 47, on this
occasion, and perhaps it is because Miss V. feels
about 9 inches too short and 6 stone too light for
the character that she doesn’t trouble to appear
less than 20 years too young. But the absurdity
of little Miss Vane as poor old Ruth is only a
shocking detail, for which the chorus, especially
the girl part of it, makes ample atonement.
* * *
Miss Nance O’Neil and the dramatic co.
from Sydney are to appear at Her Majesty’s on
Saturday (2nd). Manager J.O.W. has stated to
sundry interviewers that he was prompted in the
first place to lease the theatre for the particular
purpose of producing Miss O’Neil, therefore Mel-
bourne looks forward to her with particular agita-
tion, and has already begun to call her Nance, as
though she were one of the family. Nance will
make her first impression in “ Magda.”
** * *
At M'elb. Princess’s the rushful narrative
of “Jones ” will quit the bill on Friday. Bagman
Jones and the impossible things that happen to
him have proceeded for several weeks, and this
week is the time when the tail-end of public
patronage comes wagging to the pay-box as a
matter of duty. On Saturday (2nd) Charles
Arnold and co. will take themselves more seriously
in “The Professor’s Love Story,” a quaint, senti-
mental comedy by J. M. Barrie, “The Little
Minister” man. This is said to be one of the
prettiest little plays produced in London since
V.R. had her last jubilee, and the second act
appeals to agricultural sympathy with a wheat-
field scene.
«* * *
The Haytors, who were well-beloved by
Australia three years ago, are back again under
some more engagment to Rickards, and their new
outbreak at the Bijou got a joyful reception last
Saturday. The one very weak item at the Bijou
just now is a golden-haired lady who sings long
songs about dying orphans, and appears to have
been born under a gloomy star. Otherwise the
programme is mostly up to proof strength, or a
few degrees higher. Four young daughters of
Williams give a couple of pleasant turns, Miss
Francie Adler’s singing is almost worthy of her
tuneful pedigree, and Amann, Charles Seel, little
Fannie Powers, and many people worth mention-
ing are in their places as usual.
** * #
At the Athenseum Hall the British Bio-
graph Co. (Lim.), are giving two shows daily of
the most striking Bio. pictures that have struck
this continent up to date. Some of them can
hardly be said to flicker, and the figures in all cases
have the lifelike movement which is only obtain-
able from films working at the rate of 40 to the
second or thereabouts. Among the war pictures
the finest is a view of Spion Kop district, with
Buller’s ambulances and stretcher-bearers wending
their weary way down from the abandoned grave-
yard. Some of the stormy seascapes are photo-
graphic marvels, and the picture where a cavalry
horse blunders at an exercise jump and rolls over
its rider is a thrilling incident, especially for the
rider, who escapes every time with a severe pain in
the ribs.
*
Scherek’s Orchestra, with 50 instruments
on the platform, started another season at Melb.
Town Hall last Saturday. All things considered,
the Orchestra did remarkably well in sundry
classic compositions of the class that is not too
advanced for the average man in the street. Also
Miss Una Bourne, now grown very long for a
somewhat short skirt, distinguished herself more
than ever in a Rubenstein concerto, whereas tire
lady who gave an aria from “Robert the Devil”
didn’t distiuguish herself at all. Next Saturday
afternoon the soloists will be Mrs. Grace Ward,
and violinist Zelman, junior.
** * *
The same old “Ladder of Life” is up
against them at Melb. Royal, and the last nights
of the cockney Heelanders are still waiting for
announcement.
The essential lesson of Dr. Tidswell's
plague-discourse to N.S.W. Medical Association
is, keep on kill in;/ rats —even if the plague dies out
altogether. Otherwise the rats who were driven
away will presently return to their old infected
haunts ; and the plague will be born anew.
The Federal delegates seem to be lying
placidly at rest at the very time when they should
be fighting hardest. Chamberlain's so-called
“concessions” (like all diplomatists, he claimed
more than he ever meant to enforce) have ap-
parently blinded them to the gist of the matter —
which is that the Imperial Parliament has no
business to alter the Commonwealth Bill at all.
A Bendigo (Vic.) miner recently wagered
that he would eat 20 hard-boiled eggs at a sitting.
Numerous side-wagers were laid, and his friends
freely accepted even-money about the event. It
looked a certainty for the ogre’s backers till the
15th egg was swallowed, but over the 19th they
dropped their money, thus losing by two points.
The crowd around the Melbourne Yarra-
bank “ orators ” was, on a recent Sunday, treated
to a little diversion, when a beery old gent fell
into the smellsome stream. A life-buoy was
thrown to him, but in his vain struggles to get it
round him he would probably have gone under
had not a bystander advised him to walk out—
which he did, the water just there being only
waist-deep.
Queensland is about to hang aboriginal
Broome, convicted of a criminal assault and sub-
sequent murder near Stanton Harcourt. No
hanging has taken place in Kanakaland for eight
years. At latest the hanging had been postponed
because it was suspected that a white man had
taken a hand in the crime.
Melbourne Evenin’ ’Erald says that
President Kruger is “in his own uneducated way,
a student of the Bible.” “ His own uneducated
way” is good. Millions of Bibles are hawked
around the world every year, translated into every
language, and forced upon people who have to
take the dose in “their own uneducated way,”
That’s just what is contended for—the right of in-
dividual interpretation, and the jealous main-
tenance of the privilege has produced the various
splittings-up of Protestantism. The Holy
Romans know better, and let their tohungas run
the Book. But, poor old Paul, —to fall even under
the lash of the Evenin’ ’Erald’s irony !
The A.U.S.N. Co issues a neat guide for
the Australian coast containing much information
about where to go for a holiday with ease and com-
fort and how to enjoy one’s self when one has got
there. There are time and distance tables to show
how to reach any part of the Continent in the
shortest time, and several portraits of people who
just now are kicking up more or less dust; also an
interesting account of every sea-port town in
Australia. Altogether it is a very useful volume.

Maoriland ended its financial year with a
glorious surplus of £559,490, and there was £45,80 left
out of the previous year’s surplus (after transferring
£450,000 to the Public Works Fund), so the total credit
balance is £605,351. In view of these facts it seems as if
Maoriland could now afford to start a sinking fund, after
the South Australian fashion, for the gradual repayment
of its public debt, or swear off all further foreign borrow-
ing, or do something a little more than usually unusual
in the way of repairing the terrible financial blunders of
the old Tory regime.
New South Wales, despite its great increase of
revenue, has a deficit of £271,183 on the ten months’
operations, and there are only two months left in which
to get the accounts square. Probably they can be
squared if Premier Lyne follows Reid’s nefarious dodge
of not debiting the year ending 30th June, 1900, with the
interest on certain loans for the half-year from Ist
January to 30th June, but shunts that charge into next
year. Otherwise the prospect is only moderately bright.
No doubt, it will be rough on Lyne if he does
put this dismal fraud right, for then he will have
to pay 18 months’ interest on certain loans in one year ;
but on the other hand if he doesn’t put it right then the
awful Reid might almost as well have stayed in. And
the Lyne Government is making a fearful and wonderful
splash with loan expenditure. During the past 10 months
it spent £1,881,018 of borrowed money, which is £244,005
more, than was spent in the first 10 months of last financial
year.
Westralia for the ten months ended 30th April
spent £228,741 less than its revenue, and the deficit was
thus reduced from £247,349 to £18,608. Unless some-
thing unexpected goes wrong, Westralia should pay off
all its deficit this year.
Queensland has a surplus on the ten months of
£682,680, but large bills for interest yet to come in will
probably swallow this up. With ail its huge increase of
revenue the increase of expenditure is still larger, and
Queensland is £4311 worse off than it was at this time last
year. And this is despite a large increase of loan expendi-
ture which is £959,875 for two months (£2 7s per inhabi-
tant per annum), or £114,404 more than the loan
expenditure of the corresponding ten months of last year.
The short and simple tale of the present Australiap boom
seems to he : Loans make a fictitious and artificial pros-
perity. The prosperity, while it lasts, makes increased
revenue. Therefore, the province which is spending
loan money most furiously has the biggest revenue in-
crease, and the next biggest borrower has the next biggest
increase, and the smallest borrower has the smallest in-
crease.
South Australia’s revenue increased by £88,207
for the first nine months of the financial year, and its
expenditure increased by £lo2,lo6—largely through the
Contingent disease. Up to 31st March the shortage was
£24,535. The Treasurer still keeps a stiff upper lip and
talks about an assured surplus, but prospects look none
too certain.

Victoria had a big improvement in its revenue
in the earlier months of the year, but it is fast disappear-
ing, and at the present rate the chances are that the last
shreds of the dreadful Shiels’s reputatian as a financier
will fade by June 30. Shiels assumed the year’s revenue
at £7,294,000, indignantly throwing aside the cautious
Turner’s previous estimate of £7,156,225. On Shiels’s
figures the 10 months’ receipts should have been
£6,078,334, so that rash old-young man is already about
£46,000 behind. No expenditure accounts are published,
but Shiels’s estimates showed a probable deficit of £2OOO,
or so, even if he got all the revenue he expected. And
Shiels is a Jonah sitting under a fast-withering monetary
gourd.
* * * *
The City of Melbourne has a municipally-owned
electric-lighting system, which is making steady progress.
It isn’t a very large affair as yet, but it is growing and
extending, and if all greater Melbourne was one munici-
pality instead of about a dozen, so that the whole area
could be served by one system, it would probably grow
very much faster. For the year ended 28th Feb., 1900,
the city’s electric-lighting business paid full interest on
cost of construction (4 per cent, on £112,402=£4496), pro-
vided £3372 towards the sinking-fund which is to cover
repairs and repayment of borrowed money, and left a very
handsome profit of £4163. The municipality borrowed
£112,402 for the establishment of its electric-lighting sys-
tem so far as it has gone, and it has already accumulated
a sinking-fund of £17,364 and a profit-balance of £4968,
which makes £22,332 available towards repayment of bor-
rowed money. If the municipality continues to manage
its affairs as well as this, the time must come when Mel-
bourne will have a complete municipally-owned electric-
lighting system absolutely free of debt, and returning a
splendid profit annually to help the city revenue.

There are some notable points about the A. M.P.’s latest
balance-sheet. The year just closed showed the largest
premium income, the largest income from interest, and
the largest addition to the funds in the society’s record ;
and these are things ti be proud of. On the other hand,
expenses have gone up, and the rate of interest realised
on investments keeps going down. For the last nine
years expenses have been as follows upon total income :
1891 .. £9 8 10 percent. 1896 .. £8 18 2 per cent.
1892 ..9 6 2 „ 1897 .. 8 13 0
1893 .. 8 15 6 „ 1898 .. 8 19 10 „
1894 ..8 7 0 „ 1899 ..9 7 7 „
1895 ..876 „
The increase is not very great, however, and it
still leaves the A.M.P. the most cheaply-managed life-
office in Australasia, and one of the most cheaply-
managed in the world. But the drop in the rate of in-
terest realised on the funds is more serious. For the last
nine years the A.M.P.’s pile has produced the following
return :
1891 .. £5 19 1 per cent. 1896 .. £6 0 6 per cent.
1892 .. 5 18 3 „ 1897 .. 4 17 0 „
1893 .. 5 13 6 „ 1898 .. 4 14 10 „
1894 ~ 5 11 10 „ 1899 .. 4 11 10 „
1895 ..562 „
This is a great and continuous decline, and though all other
offices are in the same situation it is none the less un-
pleasant. The estimates are based on the assumption
that the society’s assets will only yield £3 10s. per cent,
per annum in the future, so there is still a solid tl Is. lOd.
to work upon, but the margin has narrowed so fast that
there are already fears that it may prove too small. So
it is mentioned as one of the possibilities of the future
that a further reduction of estimated interest—probably
to 3 per cent. —may have to be faced, and £48,813 is
wisely set apart as the beginning of a further reserve for
the purpose. The A.M.P. is the first Australasian office
to lay its plans for a reduction of estimated interest
below 3J per cent. The fall in interest is partly owing
to the fact that the A.M.P. Society keeps a far
larger proportion of its funds in Government securities
than it used to eft. Possibly it is also owing in some
small part to the fact that some of the securities are not
worth the values set against them. The A.M.P. has a re-
serve of £266,692 to cover depreciation, and if it wrote
this sum off the values, instead of keeping the reserve on
one side and the full paper values on the other, it would
certainly make the interest rate look better. Its method
of bookkeeping is against it in this particular.
In most respects, however, the balance-sheet
looks perfectly satisfactory. The amount of interest in
arrear is moderate if the sum mentioned is all the interest
in arrear. “ Properties acquired by foreclosure, £624,420 ”
is a pretty large item and the worst-looking one on the
list, but as the A.M.P. is not largely expanding its mort-
gage business, it will probably not increase much further.
Tnere are no “agents’ balances” or “office furniture”
among the assets, nor any “ deferred instalments of
annual premiums.” There is a very fine surplus of
£506,183 for division—this after setting aside £48,813
with a view to a further reduction of interest-estimates
and writing £12,375 off securities. Probably the really
objectionable feature is that another attempt is to be
made to spread the society’s business to “ other parts of
the British Empire,” instead of confining it to Australasia
as heretofore. In most other parts of the British Empire
mortality is far higher than in Australasia. In some of
them interest-rates are even lower than they are here, low
as our rates have fallen of late, and both these matters
mean possible loss. Moreover, it is possible to spread a
business beyond the limits of proper supervision. And,
in any case, it is always a good thing to know when to let
well alone, and, if the A.M.P. doesn’t feel that it is doing
well enough, it is a sour and dissatisfied institution.

The Bank of New South Wales, with a net
profit of £99,859 for the half-year to 31st March, 1900,
shows the best results it has produced since the days of
the 1893 crisis. Its earnings in these seven troublous
years have fluctuated as follows :
March, 1893 ..£111,376 March, 1897 .. £87,898
September, 1893. 83,033 September, 1897. 89,462
March, 1894 .. 78,451 March, 1898 .. 88,128

March, 1896 86,460 March, 1900 .. 99,859
September, 1896. 87,661
Said profits are at the rate of £6 6s. 10d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds. The B.N.S.W.’s
capital has now been raised to £2.000,000 by the sale of
the remaining £50,000 worth of shares, and the usual 9
ner cent dividend therefore runs to £90,000. _ The
premium on the new shares (£46,406) and from
profits are added to the reserve fund making it £l,-50,000,
and £26,280 is carried forward. The grand old bank has
put up some remarkable rcsults dunng the haff-. ear Its
deposits have increased from £19,09.),321 to £-L2i2, ,
note circulation has increased from *»33,464A0 £916,1i6 ,
cash on hand has expanded from £4,,61,552 to£6 481,445 ,
and cash and liquid securities taken together have boomed
up from £6,715,029 to £9,149,927. The B.N.S.W has the
largest deposit business in Australasia, the largest resei e-
fund, the largest cash reserve, pays away tergest sum
in dividends, is the oldest bank—in fact, it holds all the
records worth holding, save three : It hasn t the Ingest
paid-up capital, it doesn’t pay the largest percentage of
dividend, and it doesn’t show the biggest percentage of
earnings in proportion to the shareholders funds. In all
other respects, however, it is on top.
The Bank of Australasia had a great time for
the half-year ended 16th October, 1899 (the ’Asia s balance-
sheets are aged history before they reach this country).
Profits were £119,870, which compare with some previous
earnings in this wise :
October, 1892 .. £80,346 October, 1896 .. £40,400

The management admits frankly that this isn t all
ordinary earnings, but includes unusually large sums
(amount of same unknown) recovered from debts
previously written off as bad. Still it seems that
business is improving all round with the ’Asia. The
half-year’s earnings (including the recoveries) are at
the rate of £9 19s. 9d. per cent, per annum on the
shareholders’ funds (capital £1,600,000, reserve- fund
£800,000), which is something exceptional. A dividend
at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum absorbs £64,000 (the
’Asia’s dividend was 10 per cent, in’92 ; 7J in’93; 6 in
’94 ; 6 per cent, for ’95, ’96 and ’97 ; 6 per cent, again in
’9B; 7 per cent, for the first half of ’99, and now 8 per
cent.) ; £20,000 is written off premises account; £35,000
is added to reserve-fund, making it £835,000 —the first
addition to the reserve for a dozen years ; and the re-
maining £B7O, added to previous balance, leaves £11,153
to carry forward. This is quite an unusual awakening
from the old, respectable, petrified condition of the ’Asia,
which seemed to have abandoned all ideas of further ex-
pansion. There is a further small increase in deposits
which are now £13,646,325, and the reserve of cash and
liquid securities is as solid as usual, being £5,795,920,
equal to 34 per cent, on all liabilities to the public. Also
the balance-sheet winds up as heretofore, with the
honored signatures of Jeans, manager, and Blogg, account-
ant. This world would seem los and lonesome without
that familiar autograph of Blogg, accountant.
• • • *
The balance-sheet of the West Australian Bank
for the half-year ended 26th March, 1900, shows a further
drop in earnings. It is only the second audited balance-
sheet this bank has issued —for something like 58 years it
didn’t even profess to possess the luxury of an auditor —
and apparently the decline in profits and the audit com-
menced together. Earnings of recent years have been :
June, 1894 .. £8,387 June, 1897 ..£19,685
December, 1894.. 9,623 December, 1897.. 18,251
June, 1895 .. 10,315 June, 1898 .. 15,542
December, 1895.. 10,972 March, 1899(9 mos ) 29,763
June, 1896 .. 12,281 September, 1899.. 13,736
December, 1896.. 16,703 March, 19 )0 .. 11,779
The profits are equivalent to £7 17s. Id. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds (capital £lOO,OOO,
reserve fund £200,000), as against £9 3s. 2d. per cent, for
previous half-year and £lB 4s. 7d. per cent for the period
to March, 1899. A dividend at the rate of 17| per cent,
per annum absorbs 1-8750, and the other £3029, added to
previous balance, leaves £17,307 at credit of profit and
loss. Business in some respects has improved, but
advances have diminished, and the W.A. Bank has the
enormous sum of £791,641 coin and bullion in its coffers
—equal, without reckoning £280,000 of other liquid
securities, to 47 per cent, on all liabilities to the public.
The Board remarks sadly that cheap money is invading
the province, and profitable outlets for the bank’s cash
are becoming scarcer than they were. Certainly an insti-
tution which keeps practically the whole of its current
account deposits, or a sum equal to more than 2J times
its capital and reserve fund, on hand in specie, is an
unusual kind of bank, and with all this non-profit-earning
dead-weight it is surprising that the profits are so large
as they are. When the cheap-money invasion is a little
further advanced there will probably be a further decline
in earnings unless the institution climbs out of its quiet
old groove in something of a hurry.
* • * •
The Citizens’ Life Co. has had bad luck and
good luck over the War. Two of its big military “ risks”
—Major Eddy (£2600) and Surgeon Hopkins (£3OOO) —fell
due for payment some weeks ago, also two or three £250
insurances among the First Contingents from S. Australia
and Tasmania. But, whereas the Tassy and S.A. Govern-
ments closed with the Citizens’ Life Co.’s offer at the time
when the Australian troops were supposed to be booked
for a picnic rather than a campaign, the Vic. Govt, didn’t
want to insure their Contingent until things began to look
ugly. Then the company told the Govt, to go away and
whistle for the chance it had so unwisely refused.

The two rival tramway-purchase syndicates are
both advertising their private bills in the Adelaide press,
but Premier Holder has thrown a bombshell into their
camps by announcing that the Government will oppose
both schemes. Holder saj's the Govt, has not decided
whether it will go for municipal control or buy and run
the lines itself. Meanwhile the action of Adelaide
Corporation in throwing away its rights to “ W. Gentry-
Bingham’s” London syndicate has produced healthy
opposition among local bodies.
* • * *
A company has been floated in Dunedin (M.L.)
to exploit a patent mode of branding export mutton,
which is free from the disadvantages of those now in use.
The brand is applied to the warm carcase, and by suction
the skin is raised into any desired pattern. The brand
stands out in relief as clean as that on a pat of butter.

The Conrad mine, Bora Creek, contains, it is averred, one

Blayney (N.S.W.) mine is said to have found a
very important new lode; and it is averred that when
Burraga mine (N.S. W.) gets two blast furnaces to work it
will produce more copper than Great Cobar. Lewis
Lloyd, Burraga’s former owner, used to aver that he could
smelt 12 per cent, ore nearly all the time.
• • • •
How long are the Australian Governments
going to let Bull, of London Stock Exchange, play confi-
dence and other tricks with our (the Australian public’s)
mines ? For the Australian public hasn’t sold its mines—
it has only leased them—to Bull. Yet Bull is permitted
to act as if he owned them, and as if Australia had no
further interest in them. Bull, as mining matters now
stand in Australia, is allowed to publicly gamble with
loaded dice. The Bulletin means to agitate until no
mining lease is issued in any Australian province which
does not provide for the regular inspection of all mines by
expert State-officials, and the prompt publication of their
reports ; also, for the inspection at any reasonable time
. of any mine by any shareholder therein or his appointed
agent, and by the press.
» » * •
“Moses Moss,” of London Truth, continues to
predict great things for the Westralian mines and market.
Seven or eight weeks ago he foretold a substantial
advance and a great recovery in values. The “Bear”
raid waB nearly over. A satisfactory and wholesome
lesson was about to be administered to the ursine brigade.
He proceeded to give ruling prices and to indicate how
much higher they were going. Kalgoorlie Miner has
long been in the habit of clipping out this boomer’s
article and publishing it weekly—of course on account of
the “ nice ” things he always says of the Westralian
mines. This particular article must have been rushed in
without much editorial discrimination. Hence we have
the sad spectacle of the prices given by “M.M.” when
they were supposed to be at their lowest, right beside
the prices ruling in London and Adelaide on the day the
article was republished. Brown Hills were £lO 12s. 6d.
in one column and £8 12s. 6d. in the other —only a fall of
£2 per share in six weeks, while “M.M.” predicted a
much higher rise in a much less time. Lake Views re-
presented a drop of over £3 a share while that of Ivan-
hoes was nearly as great. In Associateds the difference
was between £5 10s. and 70s. ; Golden Links were repre-
sented by a drop of £l, and so on ad infinitum,. For one
day, anyhow, the Miner made itself a comic paper.

* • * •
Golden Horseshoe: Chairman C. D. Rase
recently told shareholders that the sulphide question
at Hannan’s was greatly misunderstood there
was no difficulty in treating the ore, it was
merely a question of the best method for getting
the best extraction at the lowest cost. Exactly—
but in this is often involved a great deal of difficulty.
The Associated treated the ore and got some gold out,
but it left too much behind and the cost of getting the
little was too great. It seems a misnomer to call the ores,
in the deeper levels of the Hannan’s mines, “ Sulphides.”
The name would imply that they are heavy in
sulphur, whereas they are so low in sulphur as to
become refractory from the very want of it. They would
be much easier to treat if they were much heavier in
sulphur, and the presence of a higher per centage of
lime doesn’t improve things. Amalgamation is
next to impossible without previous roasting,
and C.vanidation is ineffective unless the dirt
is reduced to slime and then filter-pressed. Roasting
will probably be the most expensive item. The Diehl
process claims to do away with roasting, and to do with
bromo-c.vanide what cyanide itself has failed to accom-
plish. Where does the patent come in ? Hannan’s Star
is using it, but results are not yet given, if known.
Brown Hill Go. is also about to run it, and the Lake View
Oonsols is putting it in.
• • • •
Kalgurli was a fashionable local mine for
a long time. South Kalgurli held pride of place and
got a lot of local attention because it was thought it
would double its value as soon as the plant got to work,
but there being no signs of that, attention was recently
turned elsewhere. The Associated Northern Blocks have
got it now ; these shares have gone from tl to 48s. od.,
and have lately been hovering about 40s. in consequence
of the horrible shaking given to the Blocks’ parent—the
Associated. It has some rich ore here and there, but
apparently nothing to warrant present prices.
« * * •
Westralian Market has the mumps, and the
relief of Mafeking has had the opposite effect to that pre-
dicted. As a rule, Adelaide waits on London, but on
Monday morning after the relief, and before London Sab-
bath had expired, it started in to boom things. Even
Associateds appreciated to the extent of over £lOO,OOO.
Next morning everybody wanted to see the London
market cables before breakfast. Alas 1 the relief of Mafe-
king had given some of them a nasty knock. Ivanhoes
only dropped about a-quarter-of-a-million ; Lake Views
fell £150,000; Brown Hills, which nearly everybody
expected to firm on “ developments,” dropped from over
£9 to considerably under, and so all along the line. Some
people believe that the now-assured success of British
arms in S’Africa will have the effect of diverting attention
from Westralia to the Rand. It is unusual to have two
mining booms at one time. There is not enough enthu-
siasm or cash to go round. It would not, therefore, be
surprising to see the Westralian market even more
neglected in the course of a few weeks.
A big row is brewing over the Associated affair.
Ex-manager Irwin isn’t comfortable under the charges of
Chairman Landau and his expert. Irwin did not pick the
eyes out of the co.’s chute ; did not swear to the eternal
duration of good stuff in leases ; did not over-estimate
the value of the property, but under-estimates the
capacity of his successors and of the management to do
as well as he (Irwin) did. Between “bulls,” “bears,”
fighting directors, quarrelling managers, and jubilant
lawyers, pity the sorrows of poor shareholders who bought
scrip at £l3 10s., now down to under £3.

be 7ft. of stone sampling 25oz.—the Great Boulder
manager means to open out at 700, 800, 900 and 1000 ft.
before going down with the sink to 1100 ft. The bore-
holes in the face of the 1000 ft. level are reported to show
4oz. per ton.

The prospectus of the Snowy and Thredbo
Rivers Gold Dredging Co. (No Liability) appears in this
issue. The manager is Mr. F. A. Waller, of 85 Clarence-
street, Sydney. Shares have all been taken up privately,
so the prospectus is published as an item of information
only Go. has a 100-acre lease of the river-bed at junction of
Snowy and Thredbo rivers, 40 miles from Cooma (N.S.W.),
and has made arrangements for a further 50 acres. Also,
it believes that the wash will pan out for the very hand-
some yield of 6idwt. per cubic yard. Certainly it is in a
highly-auriferous country, which is much in its favor.
Many gold-dredging co.’s nowadays haven’t that advan-
tage.

At their Port Pine Works, the Broken Hill
Proprietary Co. has just completed the erection of another
Ropp Straight-line Roasting Furnace, making five in all.
These furnaces are used for desulphurising the matte, on
which material they have a total capacity of from 250 to
350 tons per day.' The “ Ropp ” is in use elsewhere in
Australia —at Mount Morgan, also in West Australia at
the Associated and Kalgurli mines. The Parke and Lacy
Company is the patentee throughout Australasia ; as also
of the Wethey Multiple Hearth, and the Schorr Furnaces.*

North Kalgurli: Another mine with a good
name and in a good position, if that break did not come
in between it and the Kalgurli. It has probably done
more downright prospecting in shaft-sinking and cross-
cutting than any other mine around, and it has done it all
without finding anything, and it will possibly go on doing
it till the end of the chapter. It has a market value of
over £lOO,OOO, but what its real value may be is guess-
work.

Princess Louise : How many persons have heard
of this mine? It is a small, irregularly-shaped lease ad-
joining Hannan’s Brown Hill, and was lately taken over
by that co—’tis said, in return for 6000 shares which then
represented a value of about £60,000 ; it is worth £lO,OOO
less to-day by reason of the all-round slump. There are
several questions which might reasonably be asked by
shareholders. Who were the original owners of this
lease ? Did they get the whole of the 6 )00 shares ? and if
not, how big was the balance, and what became of it ? In
any place except London a matter like this would receive
attention.
• * • •
Hannan’s North: An English co., vigorously
working a lease away to the north of the Kalgoorlie town,
fully three miles from the big mines, and in the imme-
diate vicinity of the Golden Zone, Reefers’ Kureka, and
other mines proved to be not worth as many pence as
the pounds spent upon them. This mine was fortunate
enough to strike a little vein containing much gold, but as
it is not likely to improve in size or in value in the deeper
and harder ground, where it will be much more expensive
to work, the chances of success may be considered remote.

The Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South
Melbourne, have delivered the first set of electrically-
driven triple double-acting plunger pumps for the ,1 unc-
tion Deep Leads. Those pumps are a fine sample of
colonial munufacturing skill. The total weight -of the
pump is close on 100 tons. All the gear wheels, which
are Bft. diam. and D2in. face, have machine cut teeth.
The Otis Co. had to design and build a special machine to
cut these wheels. Two other sets of these pumps have to
be built to go down the shafts on adjoining mines. They
will all be operated from a central power plant now
nearing completion.*

Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, Mining Machinery
Merchants, 606 Little Oolhns-street, Melbourne, report
that the Luhrig system of concentration is gradually es-
tablishing itself in the principal mills of Australia. The
latest Luhrig vanner has a large capacity, and owing to
important improvements the life of the belt, &c., is greatly
increased. Vanners and spare parts are stocked in Melb.
and prompt delivery is thus assured. A small testing
plant is available near Melbourne. Upon receipt of
necessary data, estimates for complete plants, including
batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

SOCIETY
The late outbreak of disaffection among
prisoners in Berrima Gaol makes some members
talk of calling for a Royal Commission on N.S.W.
prison methods when Parliament meets. The ex-
periment of taking Capt. Neitenstein from a
juvenile training-ship and putting him over the
adult prisoners of N.S.W. was admittedly risky,
and a Commission may serve the purpose of show-
ing exactly how the experiment has worked out.
The lash for prison offences is too often heard, if
report speaks truly, in Berrima and other prisons,
“solitary” is too common, and the gaol-libraries
are “faddy” and mawkish to a degree. Comp-
troller-General Neitenstein’s occupations previous
to his taking a post on the Vernon training-ship
were clerking in an English commercial office and
serving at sea as a seaman.
* * * *
Thus the cable on the second reading of
the Commonwealth Bill in the House of Com-
mons :
King Oscar of Sweden and many Australians were
present, including Sir Julian Salomons, Sir W. P. Manning,
and Mr. C. J. Roberts, O.M.G.
This is the sort of stuff ladled out to the daily
newspapers under their monopolistic agreement
with the cormorant Eastern Extension Co. The
cables are so vague that the exact nature of the
agreement arrived at is still uncertain, but room
is found for this advt. for one or two second-rate
local politicians. It is small thanks to Julian
Salomons, anyhow, that the Commonwealth Bill
is now before the Imperial Parliament.

lie talk about the Harms worths and the
establishment of a big Australian newspaper. A
Harmsworth agent visiting Sydney about a year

ously drunk on Mafeking Monday. Most busi-
ness premises were closed, but because Charlick
Bros., grocers, chose to remain open, a riotous
mob gathered and smashed every window in the
place. In addition an employe was injured. The
police were simply powerless. The firm claim
that as Govt, showed its absolute inability to keep
order, the amount of damages, several hundreds
of pounds, should be paid by the State. The
attack on Charlick’s had, however, little or
nothing to do with the views of the firm on the
Boer question. For years the grocers’ assistants
of the city have yearned to pay off an old grudge
founded on the fact that Charhcks obstinately re-
fuse to shut on Grocers’-Picnic Day, and have
shown lack of sympathy with other movements in
favor of the employes generally. This feeling
culminated when the crowd surrounded the shop.
That was all. The blare of brass bands also
helped in the work of destruction.
* • * *
What “loyalty” means. At a Melb.
State school a couple of youngsters took it upon
themselves to ring the bell in celebration of the
relief of Mafeking. The teacher interfered, and a
barney ensued, in which casual strangers took a
hand. Then a Jingo female called the school-
master “a Boer,” and smote him hard an,d often
with her gamp. In self-defence, the man of letters
smashed the gamp, whereupon the collected males
fell upon him with great patriotism, and sat on
him in the mud whilst the youngsters rang the bell.
The Respectable Press regards this as a very
spirited and amusing episode.
* » * *
Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat, has dis-
covered the reason of things S’african :
At first there were disasters—Nicholson’s Nek, Storm-
berg, Tugela, Magersfontein—but success dated from 11th
March last, when the nation in humility besought the
God of Nations to work out their deliverance. When the
nation acknowledged its dependence, and called for de-
liverance, God answered, and from that time Lord
Roberts began to roll back the enemy.
Which is the conventional talk of the conventional
piety-monger. The British nation didn’t trust in
God to turn the tide—it sent out its two best
generals and 100,000 more men. If Britain had
simply prayed, and left Buller and Methuen to
wipe out Joubert and Cronje without reinforce-
ments, there would have been some show of faith
to brag about. Does Sam’l Ballarat mean to say
that 35,000 Boers would have licked 250,000
Britishers but for the special prayer ? If he had
said that in Melbourne on the great Mafeking-
drunk night the mob would have taken him up by
his gaitered heels and pounded his head on i
barrow of 16 speckled-bananas-for-3d.

The experiences of one M.L. contin-
genteer, sent back in disgrace, make a dramatic
little story. Insubordination and indifferent grog
gained him “irons” on his way to the Cape; a
similar thing occurred when in camp ; and finally,
after putting in a record wild and woolly time, he
was court-martialled and ordered to be shot. In-
tercession secured commutation to 7 years’ penal
servitude, and that again petered-out into the sack
and send-home. Another man (an officer) per-
formed so on the voyage that he had to resign
before the Cape was reached to evade court-martial.
He is now a private in a Natal field company.

A peculiarity of a Sunday-trading case,
heard recently by Carlton (Melb.) bench, was
that the alleged illegal consumer was him-
self a constable, whom two of his comrades
saw enter the pub., leaving his two children
outside. The other constables (in plain
clothes) then knocked, and, according to
their account, were not admitted for several
minutes. An extremely strong
defence-case proved that there had
been no drinking in the matter;
yet, to the astonishment of every-
body who didn’t know how this
precious Carlton bench always sides
with the police, the publican was
convicted and fined.

in a certain N.S.W. large country -

Melbourne, which was troubled lately by
a cataleptic Jap, has now a cataleptic white man
on hand. He was found at Narre Warren, Gipps-
land, and brought in by train, a local grocer
having delivered him up to the police. The
grower says that the patient walked into his store
and bought some biscuits, and as soon as he had
oaid for them lie became rigid and speechless.
Yfter he had stood quite motionless for an hour
;he grocer grew tired and had him removed. The
doctors are puzzled about his symptoms, but the
present writer, though not a medical authority, is
prepared to swear to one aspect of the case -any-
how : When that young man recovers he will find
that the biscuits are gone, and that the money he
paid for them just before he lost consciousness
hasn’t come back.
A recent Bulletin par. anent
che re-naming of “ New Zealand ” on
account of tne Boer war roused to
action Christchurch Press, the editor
whereof recommended the very un-
fetching name of Sealand. Equally
painful were the efforts of a horde
of correspondents who at once took
possession of the open-column, the
vast majority of names suggested being
clumsy and inappropriate. Some of
them : Cooksylvania, Femland, Brit-
onia, New Fortune Land, Rataweka-
moa, New Erin, Antipodia, Moaland,
Notonesia, Kingsland, Cooksland, &c.
The unfertility of imagination of
“Pro Bono Publico,” “Constant
Reader ” and co. is remarkable.
Nothing better than “Maoriland”
is likely to be advanced as a desig-
nation for the country, but meanwhile
general interest has been aroused in
the question.

Queensland girl and the cheque (8.,

make me a present of the piece. He

the highest penalty allowed by law.

“ H.” : Last Sunday my parson
fave a capital argument for Sab-
atarianism. It is a scientific fact
that the telegraph wires get tired.
They are better on Monday than
Saturday, through the degree of
rest. Scientists say the wires
ought to get a complete day off
once in three weeks.

of Sydney Stipendiary Court Solici-
tor Moss to a bashful witness who
falters in the rehearsal of a love-story :
“ You were a bit foolish ; don’t mind
now, let us have it. We were all like
that. I was that way, and his Wor-
ship there on the Bench—just look at
him—was once upon a time just as big
a fool.”
Sydney’s official illumination in honor of
Mafeking, last Saturday, wasspectacularly, afailure.
The chief Govt, buildings were lit up (mostly with
devices left over from the Diamond Jubilee), also
the banks and the newspaper offices, but that was
all. The Mayor and Corporation decided that in
these days of retrenchment no money was to be
spent in display. So the Town Hall was in dark-
ness. Even the proprietary of that Sabbath
journal which for the past six months has been so
enthusiastically collect ng from other people in
the sacred cause of Jingoism did not go to the
expense of a solitary rushlight.
• * * »
The Q. Ministry is beating the drum of
bogus patriotism for all it is worth, but the people
do not respond. When the public holiday was
declared to celebrate the relief of Mafeking the
Brisbane Trades Association resolved to disregard
the proclamation and hunt the nimble shilling as
usual. Then Govt, worked up rjgreat demonstra-
tion, and invited private citizens to bring their
carriages and vehicles and to decorate their
premises along the line of route. The main
street of the metropolis, as a result of the
invitation, was gaily illuminated—there were three
Chinese lanterns in front of the Bank of N.S.W.
and a lavish waste of gas in the Town Hall—-all
the other manifestations of exuberant loyalty
hung on public buildings at the country’s expense.
The procession itself was an unutterable fiasco-
consisting wholly of the local defence-force and
volunteers and five Government lorries hung with
Chinese lanterns. An effigy of President Steyn
so closely resembled Hugh Nelson that the audi-
ence didn’t know whether to groan or cheer. Not
a single Brisbane citizen, outside the official
clique, countenanced the demonstration or contri-
buted a sixpence to the expense. The whole
miserable and humiliating farce was a vulgar
Ministerial advertisement at the country’s ex-
pense.
• # m *
Whenever any procession takes place in
Melb., the tram co. monopolises the track with its
empty trams, so that bystanders can’t get a fair
view of the proceedings. At the 8-hours show,
the Druids’, the Chinese, and last week’s the Mafe-
king Relief show, the tram people ran to the
terminus of the march, and then proceeded to
block the road with their unwieldy machines,
thereby spoiling the pleasure of half the spectators.
At night the affronted populace made a demon-
stration against the co., by lifting the trams bodily
from the track and turning them over; but no
damage worth noticing was done after all, the
crowd being too good-tempered to wreck the cars.
This was the first instance of any protest against
the high-handed and domineering spirit of that
giant sweating monopoly, the Tramway Co.

According to N.S. Wales M.L. A. B. B.
O'Conor (just returned from S’Africa), the matron
of the Volks Hospital, Bloemfontein, told him
that she liked best to deal with Australians,
“ because, no matter how severely wounded, they
were patient and tried to make the task as easy
as possible, which was in marked contrast to the
conduct of the British officers and regulars.” Are
we only just beginning to learn what fine fellows
we are ? —or is it only the Australian “ casual-
ness ” again ?

“ Titus Salt ” to The Bulletin :
I have always admired the Victorians as a rather ener-
getic race, and, when I crossed the border, the other day,
was much struck by the vigour and completeness of their
plague-precautions. I arrived at Albury in the grey, cold
dawn of the morning. The male passengers were promptly
undressed on the platform, and examined with a micro-
scope. Any with suspicious spots on them were fastened
to a post for further inspection. All clothes and luggage
were scalded by a steam-jet from the locomotive and then
dried in an oven. Hats were carefully boiled, and boots
disinfected and polished by a State official. Then passen-
gers had their hair cut short, and were washed and put
cursing into a disinfected Victorian carriage, and their
boiled hats and luggage were handed-in to them by a dis-
infected official. Meanwhile, the ladies had been taken
into a large room, the violent cases being carried in by tho
police. They were undressed and inspected by two lady
doctors and a certificated nurse. Their hair wasn’t
cut off, but their hair-pins were taken out and fried to
destroy germs, their heads shampooed, and themselves
manicured and put through a Turkish bath. After this
their boiled clothes and bonnets were restored to them,
their cleaned and disinfected boots put on, and they were
carried to the Victorian train on germ-proof shutters,
being by this time too hysterical to walk. Then sulphur
was burned under the train and we started. At least that
was what I thought would happen, but it didn’t. What
did happen was just this. We bundled out of the train
with half-an-hour for breakfast, customs inspection, and
medical exam. We weren’t examined. A cold-looking
man gave us each a paper to fill up, setting forth name,
age, where from, whither bound, opinions on the Boer
war, father’s chest measurement, and whether mother was
ever run in for shop-lifting—or something like that. \Ve
borrowed pencils from each other, and filled them in
shakily in the gloaming as it gloamed coldly up from
the East. We didn’t have our pulses felt nor our
tongues inspected. In the dim light I could
have had 567 bubonic spots with complete impunity. And
they quite forgot to collect my paper or my mate’s. We
burned them sadly and disgustedly at Wangaratta. 1
don't know whether the other papers were collected or
not. One old man filled in his document with the state-
ment that he was 154 years old, was never born, came
from nowhere and was going to the same place, and
would appeal to the Privy Council against this outrage.
So far as I saw, he didn’t even have his hair cut for his
impertinence. There is nothing like taking adequate
precautions against the spread of the plague.—Your dis-
n ected friend, Titus Salt.

Melbourne has a death-rate of 17*77 per
1000, which means (remarks S.M. Herald) a loss
by death of about 1250 per annum in excess of the
number who would die if the sanitary and other
features of the city were equal to those of Sydney.
Had the conditions of Melbourne been as salu-
brious as Sydney’s during the past 10 years, its
population would have been 10,000 to 15,000
greater than it is.

Some Sydney municipal employes, having
umigated a certain building, were then, according
to regulations, filed into a police-guarded vehicle,
which drove straight to the Woolloomooloo depdt.
Here the men disembarked, expecting to be them-
selves fumigated, but were surprised to find that,
having allowed the publio to see them taken
charge of, nothing further was required of them
than an unostentatious dispersal.

Axioms by a degraded Bulletin con-
tributor signing “Frederick ” :
No one is capable of original sin till after 30. The
sins of youth are imitative and complimentary to those
elderlies whom youth wishes to propitiate.
The wages of sin are diamonds.
Crime is the recrudescence of antique virtue.
The difference between the curiosity of man and the
curiosity of woman is that woman seeks to know that
which somebody else knows ; man, that which nobody
knows.
Divorce is only a futile effort to rehabilitate primal
desire.
History is purely a matter of costume.

How is it that almost every important port
in Queensland has been attacked by the bubonic
plague while other provinces only suffer from the
scourge in one port at most? Brisbane, Rock-
hampton, Townsville and Cairns have each their
quota of plague patients, Rockhampton suffering
severely. Is it because the Asiatic alien was
always made welcome in Queensland, and the
bubonic bacillus (himself an Asiatic) got to
know it ?
* * * *
The cunning secretiveness of the Celestial
was again in evidence in Sydney recently. At a
Chow cabinet-making establishment one of the
workmen got welly sick, and his mates, scenting
danger boxed him up carefully and removed him
to a considerable distance. Whether the box has
since been used to store cabbage is not recorded.
The case promptly died of what proved to be
plague, and four contacts were removed from his
alleged residence. The manufactory escaped
notice, and several hilarious pagans are gaily
building welly ni cheap furniture therein.
» • * *
B. C. Simpson, C.E., of the Sydney-North
Shore bridge shindykit scheme, published half-a-
column of farewell curse the other day, and shook
the dust of N.S.W. off his disgusted feet. Simp-
son says that Lyne’s proposed State-owned bridge
is a fraud, and can’t compare with the bridge that
Simpson would have built. Also, lie actually
tries to demonstrate that railway communication
with North Sydney won’t be wanted for many
years, perhaps will never be wanted at all, there-
fore Lyne’s idea of a combined railway and road
bridge is a foolish waste of money. Further, he
alleges that Lyne’s bridge is “ clearly impossible ”
—he doesn’t say why, but presumably because, in
Simpson’s idea, no kind of bridge is possible
except one built by Simpson. One way and
another, Simpson’s curse reads like the yell of a
very angry man, who is pounding irrelevantly
with his dead, frozen wild cat on the door of the
man who didn’t appreciate the animal while it was
alive, and calling him incoherent things through
the keyhole.
“ Bush Teacher ” writes :
In reply to “ T.D.” (B. 19/5/’00), re N.S.W. metropolitan
teachers being condemned to spend three years in the
backblocks, the majority of teachers brought up and
trained about Sydney manage to stick there, while those
without influence niay be sent anywhere. On the other
hand, the teacher who has been brought up in the back-
blocks rarely gets the chance of a metropolitan school.
The rule is that a teacher who obtains a 2nd-class cer-
tificate out of the training-school shall take charge of a
small country school before being eligible for the higher
positions in the service, and shall be two years in a large
school, as assistant, before being due for a country ap-
pointment. And these appointments, as a rule, are at a
salary from £136 to £159. The only teachers at 15s. and
thereabouts are pupil-teachers, and it is the rule of the
Department to have these young people placed in schools
as near as possible to the homes of their parents or
guardians. The statement about a girl being required to
leave her Sydney home and “do for herself on 15s.
a week ” won’t hold water. I am one of the teachers,
by the way, who unwillingly subscribed to the Patriotic
Fund, because I knew that, if I didn’t, I should probably
be a marked man.
» * • *
“ Trav.” to The Bulletin :
Re your comment (12/5/’00) on the Barsch case. The
rate of pay for male and female teachers in charge of
small unclassified N.S.W. schools is the same as in the
case of third-class teachers. Single teachers (male or
female) are paid as follows :
Ist Class Provisional .. £72 per Annum.
2nd „ ~ 88 „ „
10th ~ Public .. 91 ~ ~
9th ~ ~ •• 118 ~ ~
Bth „ „ •• 136 ..
7th „ „ •• 159 .. »
3C, 38, and 3A, are the classifications required to hold
the various public schools. Married teachers (whose
wives teach sewing) are allowed £lO a-year extra and
residence (or house rent where there is no residence
attached to the school). So, in a school such as the
Wellesley one, the salary would be the same for either
male or female. As to there being a dearth of men at
the low salary, I know of young men rushing from the
Northern coastal districts to the extreme West and South
of the province to take charge of such schools and
clad to fret them, however disappointed they may bo
later on, when they find how slowly their promotion
comes. All the same, the salaries paid for these remote
localities are ridiculously small, and the circumstances are
not likely to conduce to morality, because the young men
who are sent out to these places are, generally, not much
out of their teens, and are placed where there is little or
no decent society to keep them in the straight path.

Dear Bulletin. —I note your determina-
tion to regularly look up the “religious” papers
for amusing paragraphs. Following are gems
from the diary of Rev. John Walker, now stump-
ing the country for subscriptions towards the

ROSES.
In my heart is a red rose,
In yours is a white ;
My rose is a dark rose,
But yours full of light ;
Take care lest my red rose should darken your white !
If the wind bend my red rose,
Fanning its glow,
The white rose will fold it, and
Drown it in snow ;
O, heed lest your white rose should melt in its glow !

What a hero-worshipper is lovely woman.
On Mafeking Day in Melbourne she was jubila-
ting in her multitudinous and shapely thousands.
Neither riot, nor crushed hat, nor dishevelled
back hair could crush her enthusiasm.. And the
charm of her lovely form (as a rule) showed she
had got as near as possible to the idol of the hour,
the gallant 8.-P., by putting the beautifying P.D.
corset next to her heart.*
Simplest—Most Durable —Best.
