A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 29, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
Hast 1 riday, there was such a mysterious affair
on; closed doors, no women admitted, and no
press allowed near. At the University, too!
And what was all the mysteriousness and sus-
piciousness about? A Young Men’s Debate,
where they settled nations and solved the problem,
“Is Modern Journalism a curse?”
His Ex. came, and along with him the Hon
Charles, our new aide. While I think of it—him,
I mean—let me tell you what a “ dear boy ” every-
one votes him. His Ex. sent Home for somebody
who would love everybody. Out came the Hon.
Charles, quite prepared to do it. And, do you
know, it looks like a case of “Do unto others,”
and the end of the quote —particularly the end.
He is a fair, curly-haired boy, with plenty to say,
shrewd, jolly, and hanging out a sense of humor
that ought to be gratified to the top of its desire
at Govt. House this winter, when the Evening
Parties are turned on. In short, a delightful
secretary to bring into a gloomy, moony place
like Sydney a.d. 1900,
In looks and voice His Ex. is marvellously well
represented by his new secretary. The likeness
is striking. The only difference to a casual
mind is that Charles can say what he thinks,
while His Ex. can’t even think what he says,
always. At the University, though, it was
different. At any rate, we like to imagine so.
For, believe it if you can, His Ex. took the Curse
side. No. He didn’t believe in Modern Journal-
ism. No. He did not approve of Personal para-
graphs. His Ex. did not say so, but I fancy his
notion of the Modern Journalism, which he so
strongly condemned, was clinging wholly to the
thought of that sort of item beginning, “Lord
Beauchamp is a mild, very refined-looking young
man, with the manners of a dove,” etc. Though
where on earth His Ex. could have ever seen such
a par. I cannot imagine.
. Then came the subject of a Red Paper that
isn’t steeped in gore. Said His Ex., very nicely
and in his own words, “I admire The Bulletin
for all it has done to foster Australian literature,
and I admire it, too, because it has taken The
Opposite Side about the war, and because it has
stuck to that side, stuck to it, and has not sacked
its editor.” Evidently His Ex. thinks an editor
is something that can be sacked, like Rome. I
don’t know whether the rest of the comment was
m a similar strain of admiration. My informant
suddenly grew a little confused just here.
But you will be glad to hear that soon we are
going to stop being Dormice. Things are getting
ready to wake us up. Govt. House starts with
an evening party on June 25. Music will pre-
dominate, arranged by Herr Vollmar, our distin-
guished ’cellist. I hear that the music, by Lady
Mary’s special request, is to be as English as pos-
sible. Italian opera rigorously excluded. Is that
patriotism ? Or just pure pity ?
Speaking of patriotism, how glad you must
have been to hear that N.S.W. won the Great
Cable Competition. Little. Booridgeree, too —a
main street, some kerosene lamps, three public-
houses and two policemen. You wouldn’t expect
a little place like that to be so brilliant. “ Con-
gratulations /: Booridgeree all boozed—admires your
courage. ”
After Booridgeree,—but out of Competition,
unfortunately—was the telegram a fat lady was
laboriously sending through a suburban office
lately. Even the operator grinned as he read
aloud, “ So-glad-to-hear-you-are-made-a-lady.”
By the way, N.S.W. Birthday honor falls on a
woman universally beloved (it isn’t my place to
make personal remarks about the man, although I
could say something nice there too.) A thin, grey-
haired quiet-mannered woman, who unobtrusively
slips through life, keeps off committees, and dis-
penses her unflagging charity at home and abroad
without the aid of advertisement.
Theatrically, what an excellent time we have
been having lately ! Fascinating Nance O’Neil
m a long succession of plays. Change after
change at the Royal, where the Broughs, alas,
are now putting in their last nights. Packed
houses haven’t been the rule anywhere, though.
Ever since plague took it, the theatres have been
very coolly treated— shuaned, in fact. Well, you
must admit, that if you don’t want to catch fleas
you should keep away from theatres. Luckily
for the theatres, there are always a few people
who like to catcli them. This sort of discourse
would be most vulgar at any other time, but so
deteriorating is plague that we can now talk of
fleas at the top of our voices, and be considered
merely scientific.
To return to the theatres, a huge, packed house
watched the Broughs “Sowing the Wind” on
Queen’s Birthday. Mrs. Brough looks charming
as Rosamund—one of her best parts, per-
haps. She wore a costume-frock of brick-
colored velvet, high-waisted, and very be-
coming, with a floppy brown feathered hat.
Grace Noble’s giggle legitimatised one’s current
desire to slap her for her mincing little manners,
which mince to such an extent sometimes that
only her dainty taste in dress is left whole. Bessie
Thompson, of the clear, clear voice, played a
slavey showing men the door; which was a pity, I
thought, as the back of the gods takes no interest
whatever in slaveys, and the d.c. doesn’t know a
slavey out of doors. Dartrey made one of his
biggest hits as Bob Watkins. The aged crinkle
in his legs was a marvellous match for the crooked
old quaver in his voice. As a wicked old man,
Dartrey is hard to beat. ( Not apropos of the last
remark, off the stage Mr. Dartrey is an intelligent
edition of beautiful Marcus Julius Superbus, the
Only.) And I must not forget the Leading
Juvenile m a moss-green costume-suit. Girls who
never could settle the question before, suddenly
made up their minds once for all that Lovell in
green velvet was the Lovell they were going to
pin their longest faith to.
Mr. Gregan M‘Mahon, a well-known amateur
actor, who was reading for the Law until lately
has joined the Brough co. Mr. M‘Mahon has
often delighted Sydney audiences, chiefly with the
University Dramatic Society, in comedv r parts
He was married last year to pretty Miss Molly
Hungerford, whom you have also seen with the
University dramatics.
And Game and Kingston are both going back
to England. Carne has always been much ad-
mired, despite his persistent shunning of anything
like an afternoon-tea, and also despite the fact
that, seen near, he is almost an old man. As for
Kingston, I really believe it is too much admira-
tion that is driving him out of Australia. All
men are vain, and generally a man’s vanity gives
him away (whereas a woman’s is her safe-guard).
But to shower an actor with bunch after bunch of
roses and pink ribbons, as they do at Her Majes-
ty’s every first night, is carrying admiration back
to the prehistoric days, when a man showed his
admiration of his squaw with a hard knock on the
head. Ridicule is too hard a knock for an actor.
‘‘You’ ll never sleep a wink if you see Nance
U .Neil as Nancy.” I was so solemnly assured of
it that I stayed away until the very last night.
But what a disappointment! I was quite sleepy-
before the end of the play.
Certainly, Nance strains realism pretty thin,
and without much excuse. She is dragged out of
the room by Sikes. Then comes an awful moment
of suspense. You hear blows. That’s Sikes mur-
dering Nancy. Then Sikes conies back with red
hands. And presently the door opens, and Nancy
crawls in, her hair down, almost on the floor.
Her dark hair falls all over her face, and
you can see nothing sensational. She crawls
and crawls, and the house gets ready to faint
or shriek. High up the room she crawls
gagging along on her red, red hands. Then she lifts
her head. Limelight, too, on her face, and you
cannot miss a detail. I calmly looked, hard, and
—laughed. Mind you, if you can’t laugh, that
murdered face might get on your nerves. The
eyes are gouged out, blood streams from an im-
mense gash on her forehead, and every night at
least three ladies faint in the audience.
And yet someone said to me, coming out, “Why
don’t they have the dog in, and let him walk
down the stage with blood-stained paws, leaving
footmarks behind him ? ” If Nance wants to play
B. Sikes’s best girl in the country, where they
like a lot for their money, she might consider that.
To turn to something pleasanter. The Ama-
teur Orchestra gave a feast of a programme to a
splendid house at the new season’s concert last
Thursday. . Perhaps the menu read a shade better
than it tasted. “ The Invitation to the Waltz ”is
alight, exhilarating number, which the A.O.S.
took too seriously for my fancy. Still, we owe
Hazon and his Society hearty thanks for the kind
of music they prepare for us. “The Pastorale
Symphony,” for instance, formed the chef-d'oeuvre
of Saturday’s concert. Like Wagner, his disciple,
Beethoven makes great demands on all the instru-
ments, but “The Pastorale” employs extremely
simple musical means—except in the Tempest—
and the A.O.S. gave an excellent local rendering.
An interesting item was a bit out of Ernest
Truman’s new opera, “ Mathias,” founded on the
well-known “Bells.” It was an Intermezzo from
this that we heard on Thursday, and as far as
one could judge from the first performance,
the music had “something in it.” Miss Grace
Fletcher, prettily gowned in white satin, was the
only warbler. Her French accent was more re-
freshing than an interval.
Very charming, very cultured, very musicianly,
a'.u Elsie Hall’s recitals now going on at the
Y.M.C. A. I went to the first on a wet Wednes-
day and found a fair audience only, where a big
crowd might reasonably have hurried in. The
gentle pianiste wore a pale pink frock, spectacles,
coiled hair, and no hat. As for her playing, her
style has broadened by leaps and bounds. The
Chopin Sonata in B. Minor bore ample wit-
ness to this. To myself, I always call this
“The Sonata of Appeal,” regardless of anything
Chopin may have said about it. (Why not, when
everybody gives the name “Moonlight” to a
sonata that Beethoven meant to suggesft a Swiss
lake?) Maestoso, opening a little coldly, until
the poignant Chopin feeling that one can never
mistake suddenly flows into the music. Scherzo,
with its vagaries leading into the heavenly Largo,
low chords in the left hand, the high notes striking
out the loveliest lagging melody. The appeal dies
hard. And the end of the sonata, is it a triumph
of renunciation ? Elsie Hall’s interpretation was
a surprise. A little more glow of spirit, if you
insisted on asking something, but her touch is
deeper and fuller, less spectacled, and you will be
glad to hear that she plays this sonata again
shortly.
If the Haddon-Chambers family—who live in
Sydney, and play the organ, and sing hymns as if
there was no such person as the gay Haddon
painting London red—are correct in tneir informa-
tion, Australia’s distinguished playwright is not
going to marry Melba. They say that Haddon is
already married. And, strangest part of the
affair, they are evidently deeply and darkly
opposed to the very thought of his mating with a
public character like the great Melba. They take
it as a wound. They don’t like it at all.
The Birthday Dinner —in honor of Her Gracious
—showed that several of Sydney’s tip-top digni-
taries—the Governor, the Admiral, and General
French—aren’t “playing speaks,” The bottom
of the quarrel is shrouded in mystery. There
were this year three rival dinners. The Admiral
hastily got up a dinner of his own. General
French gave a dinner of his own. Therefore,
the Governor’s representative dinner came off at
Govt. House minus Admiral Pearson and minus
General French.
Mrs. Arthur Tooth, of Darlinghurst, has started
a terrifying variation of the “Novel” tea and
other-quotation afternoon. The guests are
requested to bring portraits of themselves
as children, and the game is to find' out
who’s who. But will not identification of person
also include identification of age? Because the
child with pantalettes to the ankles obviously
belongs to a further-back generation than the one
whose stick-out frock barely conceals the edge of
her lace-covered bifurcations, while the lady who
can show her infancy with her sash swathed above
her knees and her hair waved rather than in
tallow-candle curls can assert a still further degree
of juvenility. The game is hardly likely to com-
mend itself to the elderly spinster or the cold-tea
married lady who explains tier 16-year-old cub by
the frequent assertion that she was such a child,
but Tom simply wouldn’t have a long engage-
ment.
The people Mafekined on Saturday afternoon
in a steady, mechanical sort of way. The banners
which had been drying before household fires all
the week now disentangled themselves from little
Jimmy’s flannels and waved gallantly among the
citv chimneys. Queen Victoria held a reception
at her square. She wore a bridal wreath of white
and green, and was otherwise profusely orna-
mented ; but Albert the Good merely had his
pedestal smartened up and an electric wire fes-
tooned round his ankle, which latter decoration
made the deceased prince look as if he were
meant to represent a Potts Point great-grand-
papa. In the evening the city retired into obscurity
and became merely a background for fiery
messages of peace and portraits of 8.-P., Roberts,
Queen Vic. and other people more or less responsi-
ble for the Relief, with various colored lights,
wreathed like flowers, surrounding the latter’s
bronze presentment in the Square. On the harbor,
the Royal Arthur, outlined in yellow lights,
looked like a huge topaz, in its setting of black
velvet. At 9 o’clock anyone who wanted to go
down King-street had to take the tram. There
was no other way. Numerons sensible women
carried small children into the crowd, and chival-
rous men who proffered assistance wandered about
with babies that didn’t belong to them, in their
arms, and other men’s better halves clinging to their
coat tails.
The great Nance finished up the end of last
week with “Magda” revived. Nance’s pride of
form still incites her to adopt garments which
magnify her to abnormal proportions. Therefore,
in the first act, she continues to make a square of
herself with the vast black-blotted cloak, which
she afterwards throws over half the stage ; and in
the second, has her blue dressing-gown tailings
flying to each wing of the stage as she careers up
and down “rousing”on papa. The scene where
papa tries to throttle Magda in the interests of
virtue is irresistibly funny. Papa rushes up at
Nance’s majestic throat, and the effect is some-
thing like one man trying to stem Niagara.
It cleared up on Saturday, but evidently not
quickly enough to permit such of our Best as re-
main with us to race into their best frocks and
make for Randwick. So that resort was mostly
filled with bookies and the racing lydies who
never miss seeing a “bit of blood,” or putting
their sovs. on the horse they dreamt of last night,
or on the certain information given them by their
best boys. Chiefly the sort of lady who wears the
feather-trimmings of her hat down her face, her
hips fashionably large, and several sheaths of
crumpled gauze skirts trying to look like one.
The Hay-Burdekin alliance eventuates in Sep-
tember. Miss Burdekin’s 19th birthday, 26th
May, was spent in receiving congratulations on
her engagement.
Amott House, Wynyard Square, Sydney, so
long associated with the name of the late Mrs.
Casey, is the first instance in Australia of a
boarding-house run by the local R.C. priests for
the accommodation of lay persons, male and
female. Mrs. Casey bequeathed the property to
the clergy. The house was called Amott after
one of the dead lady’s husbands —“ one of my
various husbands,” as she would say (Mrs. Casey
was both lively and enterprising when the city was
young). The brother of Mr. Amott was the Cork
baronet who died a millionaire about a year ago
and by his will divided a large Dublin newspaper
property among the employes thereof. A score
of years ago Mrs. Casey was offered £17,000 for
the house on Wynyard Square and lived*to regret
not accepting it.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The front of Parliament House during the
Mafeking Relief demonstration was not a dressy
show. The Govt, had reserved the steps for the
members and their womankind. The politicians’
feminine possessions are very frumpy as a rule,
most of the family income being needed for cricket
clubs and church bazaars. Waxellency Madden’s
wife and daughters, however, somewhat redeemed
the crowd on the front, the lady in a mauve toque.
The Madden girls looked picturesque but most
frozenly solemn. In the effort to look dignified
and live up to Govt. House, they only succeed in
being wooden. Pretty girls should never try to
look dignified. A woman should be content to
look graceful until she is thirty-five, and dignified
when she puts on flesh ; grace before meat as it
were.
“Mayor McEacharn’s management of his horse
was the comic feature of Melbourne’s Mafeking
procession. If Malcolm Donald’s boats didn’t
steer better than his nag, the coast of Australia
would be strewn with wrecks and the casual
citizen would not be able to sleep for fear of a
coastal steamer putting its nose into his front door.
“What a Vic. Bushman’s contingenter got in
presents from the crowd between St. Kilda-road
barracks and Port Melb. : 24 handkerchiefs, 13
odd gloves, 2 gold rings, 1 brass do., 7 tin match
boxes (one containing a miniature ivory elephant
and another a set of dice), 6 letters—one being an
offer of marriage upon return from the war—
numerous ribbons and photographs.
“The two tall, slim daughters of J. M. Pratt,
M.L.C. —perhaps the dressiest girls in Mel-
bourne during the Mafeking celebrations—paraded
ln bnght blue with a white turned-down laee
collar and a thin band of red ribbon round the
neck, working the three colors in cleverly and
with taste ; which was much better than merely
sticking on a slab of the tri-color anywhere. Alice
Crawford, the young Bendigo elocutionist, was
smartly dressed in bright navy blue with a white
sailor collar, and a big red bow underneath an
acre or two of white picture hat. Most women
had red, white and blue somewhere. French
visitors no doubt thought the affair was all on
account of the Paris Exhibition.
.Remarks heard in the streets during the
Baden-Powell term of lunacy remind me of Arch-
bishop Carr’s return to Melbourne. After the
thanksgiving ceremonies in St. Pat’s Cathedral, I
saw two old women with tears streaming down
their cheeks standing on the steps, and heard one
t° the other, ‘Och, Glory be to God, sure
he 11 never see another day like this until he dies.’
, ”P n Mafeking night a smart audience saw
Pinafore” at Her Majesty’s. Prominent ex-
hibits m the circle-show were some naice white-
frocked bridesmaids escorted by one of the hunting
U atsons. Also Mr. and Mrs. Gordon Lyon (nee
Calvert), back from their honeymoon. Before
opening night (May 19th) lessee Williamson had
100 hands working hard at Her Majesty’s, and
the whole neighborhood smelt of burning brim-
stone. So that the Naicast and Smartast may
forgive, even if they can’t forget, the fleas of long
ago.
“ In ‘ Pinafore,’ dainty little Josephine (Carrie
Moore) wears a chippy white walking-frock, tan
shoes and hose, feathered white hat and a white
bow at the end of her jaunty pig-tail. When she
dons her scrumptious white evening gown a big
white bird roosts on her head. ‘Pinafore’ being
a nautical opera, said snowy fowl is most likely
‘ a "hid sea-gull, a bold sea-gull.’ To this scrilie
it looked like the albatross that haunted the
Ancient Mariner, but that night ‘God Save’
and ‘Rule Britannia’ had been sung and re-
sung, and gaudy flags had fluttered, till
one’s nerves played up. Dorothy Vane, as
Buttercup, appears as a l>ow-logged frump
in goggles, nlaid shawl, and down-tailed red
petticoat. However, she’s less appalling than
that outrage on the sex, the amorous spinster
(Sallie Booth) in ‘What Happened to Jones.’
Stage love-sick old girls should be prosecuted as
libels by the Society for the Protection of Petti-
coats.
“ On the first night of ‘ Pinafore,’ Brownlow, in
naval uniform, provoked the maddest feminine
excitement. Girls shook showers of posies,
streamered with red-white-and-blue, at the lime-
lit vision, and gasped, ‘ Oh, the darling ! I want
to kiss him.’ Meantime, Kenningham, acting 40
times as well, fell comparatively flat. Mummers
should never flaunt their marriage-lines. Let
them be as much married as Solomon, if they like,
but don’t send the bellman round with the news.
“I witnessed a lovely little comedy in one spasm
in the Princess’s dress circle t’other night. Arnold
deals out filtered ice-water to his D.C. patrons
these evenings, and a large, stout, hot matron, who
was well dressed but had hardly a rag to her back,
was suddenly Confronted by an affable small boy
bearing a tray of sparkling glasses. The red
dame, who had been looking depressed, beheld
those glasses as in a vision, and her face lit up with
great expectation. She reached for a glass, bore
it to her lips, and then her nose discovered the
error. She shuddered, recoiled, and hastily re-
placed the water untouched, and sat through the
rest of the interval with the air of a person whose
confidence had been imposed upon and whose
tenderest sensibilities had been ruthlessly out-
raged.
“ No Birthday honors came to Victoria this year.
It is doubtful whether recommendations for titles
and stars made by a Lieut.-Governor, not per-
sonally known at the Colonial Office, will carry
much weight. Hopetoun caused an unusual
number of knighthoods to be made, and Brassey’s
record was not bad. But it is many years since
not even a bit of ribbon has come Victoria’s way ;
and there are poor old Fitz., and George Jenkins,
and Speaker Mason, and others who have been
expecting and hoping such a long, weary time.
“ Before Society discovered the Japs., the
’Varsity girls froze on to them, and oame in for
ship-teas, and sich. In consequence, the little
brown men take all Melb. women for blue-stock-
ings, and act accordingly. Miss Bums is intro-
duced to Lieut. Jappy-jap-Jappy ; he asks
eagerly, ‘ Descendant of Scottish poet ? ’ If your
Christian name is Beatrice, a chubby little middy
drags Dante into the confab.

The plague has had its advantages for some
people, though not for most. The Sydney Safe Deposit,
for instance, has had a great accession of business during
the last few months, consequent on persons having de-
cided to prepare for the worst-by placing their papers,
&c., in safety. The fear is that when a house is quaran-

PERSONAL ITEMS.
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly now sports three
knights—Lyne, Abbott, and Mat. Harris.
Marshall Hall: “I am dazzled by the
magnificent injustice of Britain.”
According to London Daily Chronicle,
Lord Roberts may be the first Governor-General
of Federated Australia.
It is wired that Oom Paul has been
putting away a “stocking.” Evidently not an
“Absent-Minded Burgher.”
T. C. Just, who died recently in Hobart,
was at one time member of the Tas. Assembly,
and made the island-record stonewall speech of
eight hours.
Archbishop Murphy, of Tasmania, is the
only surviving bishop, with the exception of Pope
Leo, appointed bv Pope Gregory XVI. He was
bom in 1815.
There are strong symptoms that Premier
Lyne’s most intimate friends are determined not
to call him “Sir William.” He is, they say, to
be “Sir John.”
The Bulletin congratulates Premier Lyne
on becoming a Knight. Anyone who absolutely
belongs to the head-station should bear the brand
of the head-station.
A picturesque figute at the Sydney Birth-
day levee, in full uniform, was the bent form of
Surgeon-Major Crooke, aged 86 years—and still in
practice as a doctor.
Zadkiel’s prophetic almanac for 1897 made
Queen Victoria die in 1899. Even prophets are
not always good business men. Good business
talent would have stuck to generalities.
London Sphere recalls the fact that
General Pole-Carew—familiarly known among his
intimates as “Polly Carey”—was private-secre-
tary to Hercules Robinson while Governor of
N.S.W., 1872-1879.
At North Melbourne, the other day, an
a-icient Hibernian of 90 was fined for failing to
send his son to school —said youth being only 12
years of age And the old boy said he was “ good
for a few more off-shoots yet ! ”
The able Henry Gullett, erstwhile editor
of the Australasian, associate-editor of Sydney
D.T., and afterwards editor (in Mr. Cumow’s
absence) of S.M. Herald, is spoken of as a
probable starter for a N.S.W. seat in the Federal
Parliament.

bauble. Is it that Justices Stephen and Owen
have never dabbled in politics ?

Though Sydney D.T. cabled (per J.
Randal Carey, President ”) to Lord Roberts and
to Baden-Powell its congratulations on the relief

An old Sydney identity passed over last
week in the person of Butcher “Billy” Buttel,
aged 66 years. Mr. Buttel was a native of Sydney,
born in Druitt-street, and was full of old Sydney
lore. “ Buttel’s Cow ” was a prominent figure in
King-street for many years, as, “all on her own,”
she wended her way to and from the Domain,
poking her head into hotel bars for provender.
If Donald Macdonald is to get the legiti-
mate measure of kudos for his Ladysmith book, it
should be issued immediately in London. The
material is as good as any that the war has brought
out, but the race is to the quick, and men like
Julian Ralph and veteran Burleigh knew what
they were about when they published their per-
manent records before even leaving S’Africa them-
selves.
Fred. Co wen, recently appointed conductor
of the English Philharmonic Society, speaks
gratefully of the good time he had as chief of
Melbourne Exhibition orchestra eleven
years ago. He then conducted 3(50 con-
certs, including 30 oratorio performances,
with a choir 700 strong, and so “ gained
the experience of a lifetime in a few
months.” Cowen’s father, it seems, was
private-secretary to the late Earl of
Dudley—the musical potentate who
wore his hair very long in order, it was
said, to hide a pair of bogus ears—a
result of being found trespassing in the
Sultan' of Turkey’s harem.
The late James Hopkins, M.L.C.,
was not the last M.L.A. whom a
N.S.W. electorate supported by sub-
scription. Jacob Garrard, ex-Minis-
ter, and now on Sydney Water and
Sewerage Board, enjoyed that class
of support for several years. No
N.S.W. legislator who accepted pay-
ment from his constituents died with-
out regretting it. David Buchanan paid
by gold-diggers, A. G. Taylor by
feneral voters, Garrard by Mort’s
iockers, Dalgleisli by ironworkers,
Angus Cameron by carpenters, and
Hoskins by miners, have all condemned
the hard fate of having to collect their
stipend from constituents.

his constituency kicked him out. Ditto with

generation, would not have any adjournment, be-
cause he feared people wouldn’t come back sober.
A pub. opposite used to give away drouthy red
herrings, and there was apt to be a fish-like smell
about the court.

Nothing so far has been publicly said as to
the trouble apparently existent between Gov.
Beauchamp and Major-General French, command-

The row is understood to be over something about

An amazing spectacle during the Melb.
Jingo bout was a Supreme Court Justice, who
worked himself into a frenzy at the big meet-
ing, tossed his shiny head-gear about, worked his
arms like semaphores on the loose, and lost every
oz. of the dignity he has accumulated by years of
dirge-like homilies to the unfortunates passing to
the gallows through his hands.
General Hutton was the loudest-swearing
commandant the N.S.W. military ever had. When
he arrived in Sydney, a D.T. reporter went to
hear his first address to the forces, which went—
“ Well, you’re a pretty decent set of men, dammit,
but you’re blanky badly officered.” And so on for
a column or more. But the oration didn’t go into
print that way ; a discreet editor submitted the
proof to the General.
A newly-appointed J.P. was fined at a
N.S.W. country Police-court the other day for
being drunk and disorderly. Yet when the list
appeared, including this character’s name, as well
as that of an unoertificated, bankrupt, no notice
was taken by the Minister of the people’s objec-
tions, and the necessary documents wore for-
warded so that they could be sworn-in as
magistrates of the province.
Ballington Booth has lodged a preliminary
legal application, in the U.S., to change the name
of his twelve-year-old son William Booth to
Charles Brandon Booth—which naturally excites
curiosity. Is the General’s name a contamination
to his grandson ? Interviewed, Ballington is made
to say that he will give startling reasons for the
change. It is reported that the General, when
last in America, had a talk with him.
Concerning Ben Harkus’ death, Trooper
Barclay, of Singleton, writes from Bloemfontein :
I suppose you will have heard of Harkus’ death by the
time you get this letter. The poor fellow died the other
day, presumably from enteric fever, but more likely from
a broken heart. Since he rejoined us, he has never been
the same man as when he left us. Some of the boys asked
him how he came to volunteer after going home. He re-
plied, “ A man couldn’t live in Sydney.” His last words
were to ask for his children.
Melba, it is printed, avers that she is
delighted to get rid of her husband, “Kangaroo
Charlie ” Armstrong, and means to be happy with
her spouse-prospective, Haddon Chambers, the
Australian playwright, in view of which happen-
ing she has already bought a house in Great
Cumberland-street, London. Haddon Chambers
(who, by the way, was born in Sydney, where
some of his people still live, and practically started
his literary career by writing for The Bulletin)
is a smart and vivacious man, who threatens to be
eternally young.
Dear Bulletin. —Why insist on calling
Premier Seddon “Digger Dick?” Although he
has graduated from a small goldfield township
into prominence, Seddon is not, and never was, a
digger. He has lived long on the goldfields of the
West Coast, and still represents in Parliament his
old electorate of Westland, which no doubt makes
people think Dick had been delving for the pre-
cious metal. As a matter of fact, during his long
residence on the goldfields his occupations were
storekeeper, gold-buyer, publican, and mining
advocate.—Ki \vi.
Bertram Mackennal, the brilliant young
sculptor who has caught on so well in Europe, had
no honor in his own country. A few years ago
his modest little studio in Swanston-street, Melb.,
was an unfrequented place, and when he sent to
the trustees of the Public Library opposite the
fine bit of statuary which was afterwards awarded
a high distinction at Paris Salon, he received no
encouragement. Mackennal was starved out, and
left for London. His father, a very talented
designer, is one of the most genial and popular
men in Melbourne.
Like the late Frank Grey Smith, whom he
succeeds as chief manager of the National Bank,
J. G. Addison got most of his training for the
office in Adelaide. A dour Scotsman is Mr. Addi-
son, whose “Your cash, please,” when he was
inspector, sent cold-water down the backs of
several branch officers. It was his father—he died
only a few years ago—who, accidentally sitting on
his old clay pipe, rebuked himself with, “Auch
mon, ye ocnt to be cairfu’. Twa for a happenny
at hame, an’ I gi’ed a penny for it here.” And
still the old gentleman was the head of a really
generous, warm-hearted household.
Hopson P. Walker, Chief Clerk in Equity,
and bugbear of Melbourne Law Courts, has retired
and his place has been filled by G. H. Neighbour,
one of the few barristers who took advantage of
the Amalgamation Act and practised as a solicitor.
His backsliding gave him an insight into practice
which he would never have obtained by remaining
a mere advocate—hence his reward, £l2OO a year.
The late Chief Clerk,was noted for two things—
his fiery temper and his affection for red tape.
The first practical joke played on every articled
clerk was to send him into Hopson P. Walker’s
room with a tapeless bundle of papers r
Premier M‘Lean, of Vie., is painfully
lame, and is compelled to hobble about slowly
between two sticks. He owes his affliction to
having been badly gored by a mad cow. One
day, while selling a penful of cattle, he straddled
the fence in the orthodox manner, when one of
the animals suddenly became infuriated and
charged him. Both horns penetrated his thigh,
lacerating it horribly, and dragging him into the
midst of the savage mob of cattle. Mr. M ‘Loan had
a narrow escape for his life. Like ex-Premier
Turner, M‘Lean has not a personal enemy on
either side in the ’Ouse, and very few outside.

Cherubic Dr. Bevan, the parson with
speeches to make about everything that’s going
around Melbourne, managed to get his tongue into
serious trouble the other night. One of his Con-
gregational Church brethren had dared to spout
in favor of a “native trained ministry,” and
Bevan, (who was himself imported on a fat salary)
said that the other, fellow’s doctrine savored of
Protection, “ which as everybody knows is a
coward’s platform and a coward’s policy.”
Whereat the good shepherd’s spiritual flock gave
him to understand by hisses and other un-kerris-
tian evidences, that he was fluting the wrong tune.
The words to be particularly noted in Dr. B’s ex-
posure of his fiscal faith are “as everybody knows.”
The much-flattered and over-rated tonguester sums
himself up as Everybody.

Aboriginalities.
“Myall ” : To R.W.S. (B. 12/5/’00). The
blacks on the Maranoa River, S.W. Queensland,
make fire as follows. They take a piece of dead pine
and out two notcho* in it, which intersect at right
angles. They then rub a sharp-edged piece of
hardwood, usually brigalow, in this notch. The
sawdust, formed by the friction, as it smoulders,
is lifted up by a blade of grass and they then
throw fragments of dead grass on it, blow gently,
whereupon the grass lights. The whole operation
when once the sticks have been prepared does not
take more than 3min. On the Mitchell the blacks
use a pointed stick which, by rubbing lietween the
palms of their hands, they cause to revolve rapidly
in a hole on another flat stick. The tinder is then
applied as in the other method.
• • • •
“ A.C.0.” : Re animal chivalry. It is not
usual for the male quadruped to feel any interest
in his offspring —or, for that matter, in his own
mother when she is nursing another family. 1 do,
however, know of a pa cat who takes an interest
in ma puss while she is engaged in maternal duties.
He is a big orange cat living next door to the
matron ; and, every morning for the fortnight
since the birth of her one kitten, the orangeman
has brought a food-offering, such as a young rat or
a bird. Once it was a lizard. He never enters
madame’s house—that she will not permit—but
comes to the kitchen-door and calls, sometimes for
an hour before the lady appears. When she does,
he lays the present before her and sits by whilst
she fastidiously examines, and occasionally eats it.
She doesn’t appear at all thankful for the attention,
and once gave the donor “one” on the ear for
merely looking at the kitten.
• • • •
“T.G.F.” : Re “ W.M.B.’a ” bush recluse
at Narrabri (8., 5/5/’00). On the Dubbo-to-
Cobbora road, a few years ago, lived “ Mad
Mary” who, for 15 years, tramped regularly
between Dubbo and Cobbora, seldom remaining
more than a day at either end of the journey.
Her husband, who had boen a hawker on this
ioad, was burnt to death one night whilst sleeping
under his van, and the poor woman became de-
mented in consequence, and started on the old
beat in search of her husband. She would accept
food, but never shelter, even in the worst of
weather. I saw her early one winter’s morning
near Ballimore mineral springs, lying near a frost-
covei-ed log. She was at last found stiff and cold
a few yards off the road, clutching in her hand
her old black “billy-can.”
“ W.M.F.”: Recently saw two
eagle-hawks operating on a hare. While
one chased the quarry the other wheeled
round, waiting. Every time the hunter
stooped the hare dodged and disap-
peared in a cloud of dust, but by cun-
ning work the one hawk drove the hare
towards the other. Then a sudden
swoop of the latter and the play was
over—for the hare.
The drought has pretty near put a stop to
alluvial mining in N.Q., and hundreds of old fos-
sickers are at their wits’ end. Rain or Dunwich
asylum it now means to them. And—the irony of
it—just when tin is so high in the market, too !
Came upon a pair of ancients looking for their
dinner t’other day. They carried a dilapidated
muzzle-loader charged with tin and minus a cock,
a small shoeing-hammer, and a forked stick. I
became interested in the proceedings and watched.
Soon they sighted a lonely cockatoo perched high
up on a tree. Carefully they stalked the quarry,
and then one of them adjusted a cap and took
careful aim, resting the barrel on the fork.
“ Now !” he cried. And with a gentle tap from
the shoeing-hammer the other exploded the
charge—and the cockatoo flew off. However,
they assured me, amid much regretful profanity,
that such a miss was an exception, and their
mediaeval hunting method was nearly always suc-
cessful.
* • • *
If Australian bush law take pattern from
the Narrabri brand adversity will get cruelly home
on the swaggy. A pair of “travellers” were
lately haled before a local Rhadamanthus charged
with “languaging” a squatter who refused them
rations, and were ordered to quod. “But,”
Rhadamanthus said, “apart from the abusive lan-
guage, I can send you to gaol for being unlawfully
on premises, begging being unlawful.” Squatters’
runs are therefore “premises,” and a footsore
swagman asking work or a ration to carry him on
is doing an unlawful thing on the premises,
and may be arrested. Swaggy’s days are darken-
ing. He will have to retire from the bush and
take to an infected area in the capital.
* • • +
A country bank-manager’s story, illus-
trating the progress of knowledge of man and
things as it moves in the out-bush. Jim had £lOO
on fixed deposit in the local bank. This repre-
sented the savings of years, and in the eyes of the
unsophisticated Jim tne £3 per annum it earned
him was an income. Jim went to Melbourne, and
stayed a month. Shortly after his return his
deposit came due, and the capitalist called upon
the banker with the importance of a man whose
business meant a great deal to the institution.
“Well, Jim, are you going to renew again?”
“ Renew ! ” said James, “no fear ! Look here — I
lost a hundred quid through havin’ that capital o’
mine tied up here. In town I met a cove what
offered to give me a hundred in hard cash if I
could show him as much that I’d saved out o’ my
own earnin’s. And I hadn’t more’n two Jim in
my kick. No more fixed deposits for me.”

“A.P.F.”: Re emus swimming (“ Mut-
ton-8.,” 8., 12/5/’00). Last year myself and some
others ran an emu into a bend of the Namoi River
and surrounded him, when, seeing he had but one
way of escape, he rushed into the water, and to
our surprise, dived and came up near the opposite
bank, which he soon reached.
• • * •
As Dunedin (Maoriland) is to the Scotch
colonist so is Kelly’s Basin (Tas.) to the Irish.
The Basin (in Macquarie Harbor) was named
after Capt. Kelly, an old Hobart identity, and is
now the terminus of the North Lyell Co.’s railway
and the port generally for the Darwin and North
Lyell mines. It is populated almost wholly by
Patlanders. The principal hotelkeeper is a Lynch,
everv second man in the street is called Pat, every
third man Mick. Kathleens and Marys are as
plentiful as rain-drops ; gintlenien who pay the
rint infect the moist air and plough the streets
with ferocious persistency, and every family roo-
ster wakes the dawn with a distinct brogue.
• • • *
“80-Peep”: “ Gilgai ’’-holes occur only
on the plains west of the Blue Mountains, espe-
cially in the region of the Bogan and the Mac-
quarie, and then only where the subsoil _is
calcareous; the cause of these depressions being
the unequal subsidence of the limestone; which
again may have some relation to the action of
artesian currents. The holes generally extend in
a long chain —sometimes for miles, though single
holes are not uncommon. Gilgais occur also on
Victorian plains, but are there called “crab-
holes.”

“ W.M.”:
Anyof yourreaders
ever seen crows tackling
fowls? Intliisneighborhood
(Aberdeen, N.S.VV.) they will, if they get the
chance, soon put an end to any fowl that shows
signs of feebleness. Never saw them tackle any-
thing larger than a three-months’ chick elsewhere.
Strange, too, that in some localities magpies and
butcher-birds are death on young chickens, while
in others they never touch them.
« * * •
Some idea of the ravages of drought
in Queensland may be gathered from these
facts. Saltern Creek, formerly running 187,000
sheep, now possesses only a few thousands. Cor-
nish station has come down from 30,000 to 0000.
By the middle of April 320,000 sheep had been
booked for the coast on the Central Railway,
and 240,000 on the Northern line. One station,
which, a year or two ago, ran 300,000 sheep, now
keeps 70,000 alive by the use of maize. Archie
Meston recently made-a lengthy Western journey
and saw patches of grass twice in 1000 miles. The
locomotive and cattle trucks are important factors
just now. “Fight the enemy along the railway
line” is the motto just now in both Queensland
and S’Africa.

man lxiints in selecting leaders (they don t do it
bv inriutncc. anyhow). In every dairy-yard one
cow is always boss, though liable at any lime to be
defeated and supplanted. Same with
brumbies, sheep. pigs or fowls—there
is always a leader, which is followed,
if the lot are chased.

“ YV.M.B.” : In drought-stricken West
Queensland I came across a curious bullock turn-
out. Painted on the waggon in large white letters
was— “still living,” and from a kind of tent on
the top of an enormous load of wool seven little
heads gazed out at me in wonderment. The old
woman (pronouncedly Irish) drove the team, and
made it evident that in the shape of cus3-language
she had nothing to learn from any male “per-
suader.” The old man was not visible, but I
presumed he was asleep on top. Amongst the
pots, pans, and so on\underpeath the dray,
were tied two goats, followed by as many
kids, and a dissipated-looking parrot in a
battered cage suspended from a pole en-
livened the mournful procession with numer-
ous profane remarks on his own
account. The cavalcade was brought up
by an equine skeleton vainly casting
round a lack-lustre eye in search of the
grass which “ was not.”
“Old Bus”: Be “ Wil’s ” par. (B.
12/5/’00). On the road from Walhalla (Vic.) to
Moe are hundreds of stringybarks which have
been almost entirely stripped of their bark by
“black cockatoos” in search of grubs. Grubs in
this non-agricultural district form their staple
food.

“A.A.A.”: Mooney was just out, and
joined the party at White Cliffs. But you
couldn’t tell him about your mosquitoes. He
wasn’t afraid of “such flies as thim.” First night
out the camp was pitched in a small gully, and all
squatted around the dung-fire bar Mooney, who
went aside to a small hillock and lay down on his
blanket, after telling us what lie thought of our
cowardice in being afraid of the mosquitoes, which
had now begun to swarm. .Very soon, however,
groans and scratchings were heard from the direc-
tion of Mooney, who at last jumped up and
rushed over to the fire. “ I give in, bhoys—-I
can’t sthand thim no longer,” Then we had a
look and found on the poor devil dozens of bull-
dan ants which had stung him from head to foot.
He had camped right on top of a “ nest ” !

“ R.W.S.” :
Recently saw an
echidna (porcupine ant-

“ N-d-NV ” : Should like to know on what
authority “ Chas. F.” (B. 28/3/’00) states that
rabbit-proof netting (I suppose, netting fencing)
is no got si, 1 have had many years’ experience of
netting-fences, and I can’t understand how rabbits
can “crawl under” netting sunk Gin. in the
ground, or how they can “break through ” ordin-
ary 17 gua. netting, which gives a boar-pig a tough
job to break. And who has ever seen a rabbit
climb a netting-fence? 1 have seen a rabbit in a
netting-trap make frantic rushes at the netting
and scramble nearly to the top, and possibly a
hunted rabbit might do this in the case of an
ordinary netted fence, and fall over, but to say
that bunnv climbs fences is simple bosh. Also,
how does'the “swaggie” who wants work,
manage when he “heaves rabbits over a fence?”
Does Tie carry a few traps in his swag and catch
bunny that way, and (after setting his broken leg)
heave him over, or does he (swag and all) run him
down on foot? And, suppose a rabbit is fool
enough to let swaggie catch him, how does latter
hope to obtain work by heaving him over a fence ?
As for the effectiveness of a properly-constructed
and properly-looked-after fence, take the border-
fence between N.S.W. and Q. I have seen rabbits
in thousands on the N.S.W. side with the country
absolutely bare, while on the Q. side was plenty
of grass and not a rabbit to be seen.

At a recent tangi over the body of a Maori
child the proceedings extended over three weeks,
and interment was not thought of until the coffin
actually explo led. After the funeral one of the
bearers was taken to hospital suffering from ty-
phoid.

“ Yarrum ” : The philosophy of the iguana-
cim-snake business is simply that while the iguana
looks upon the snake as food, the snake objects to
that view of the case—hence the scrimmage.

“ W.S.D.” : On opening a newly-killed
iguana once, I was surprised to find the heart still
beating. I then cut the heart out, and watched it
pulsating regularly (not merely twitching) for
fully 2mm. Have since heard of the same pecu-
liarity from other bushmen. Dots the brain con-
trol muscular action ? [The brain does not control
the involuntary muscles, of which the heart is
one.—Ed. B.]
R.T.J.” : lie animals drinking salt
water (“ V.,” B. 12/5/’00). I have frequently seen
rabbits, and once a dingo (iq dry, saltbush coast-
country), drinking water trickling from the sand
after the tide had receded. Probably percolation
absorbs much of the salt from sea-water.
THE TIN-KETTLING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mickie Halligan had married on the sly,
And his neighbors were indignant at the slight.
Twas a month before the people of The Fly
Knew the news, and then they gathered in the night,
And they marched to little Mick’s
With their nail-kegs and their bricks.
At the racket sundry cattle died of fright.
Then came Halligan in anger to the door.
Mick’s dress was scant, the night was rather chill.
He dispelled the awful clamor with a roar
And a threat that he was armed and primed to kill.
“ Shut yer noise,” he said. “ Them jokes
Ain’t fer dacent married folks,
An’ ye’ll wake me little babbitt, so ye will 1 ”
B.S.

Kuppers Elberfeld Lager.
A travelling clergyman reached a Vic.
mining town just as the news arrived of the Re-
lief of Mafeking. Free drinks were the order of
the day, and every able male inhabitant was glori-
ously, patriotically drunk. The parson stood
alone —the only sober man in the street. This
strange fact excited the curiosity of one very full
digger, and he regarded him curiously. “Bill,”
he called to a bibulous individual close by,_ who
was trying, without much success, to maintain an
upright position— “ Bill, heresh a blanky bloke as
doesn’t know Mafekingsh relieved.”

“ Wiljar ” : Some people go often pain-
fully near being smashed up, eventually to die out
very easily. Know a man who
fell 30ft. through some scaffold-
ing, but escaped so well that
he would have continued work
if permitted. Another time he
fell into a liver, sank, and be-
coming entangled in the roots
of a tree was only brought back
to life with great difficulty.
Later on, a tree fell on him,
breaking his leg and smashing
his foot. Again, one dark
night he fell over a stump,
severely injuring his neck,
from which he endured tenable
agony for many weeks, recover-
ing at last to catch a cold and
die of dysentery in a few days.
“T.G.F.” : In the muddy
waters of the back country
creeks is found an eel which,
when caught, will strike with
its head, on which is a sharp
bone, a prick from which is
painful for an hour or more. This eel seldom
glows to a great size, and is excellent eating.
Can anyone snv why it is known as the “Louthen”
eel?
This looks like the champion snake-lie :
Dear Bu .letin, —“ R.H.L.” (L. S/5/’OO) found a snake
round h.s pick and shovel and another in his dish, on the
Wentworth. That’s only three snakes, after all. On the
adjacent Nicholson River there is a creek named Stutter-
ing Fred’s. My old mate and I paid a flying visit there,
and were so impressed both with the prospects and the
snakes that we decided to locate to dig, and, for the
snakes, my mate struck an original idea. He caught two
goannas, and starved them. Led them over on a string.
Snakes w \re pretty plentiful; I killed 73 going to the creek
for a billy of water, and 69 coming back. A frequent
remark about the “stripping” was (say), “3ft. of wash,
OLn. loam, and about Ift. of snakes.” YVe had lots of
trouble in preventing the goannas from swallowing their
prev ; they might have got lazy if too well fed. But one
night the biggest fellow got off the chain, went straight
for the tucker-box, and swallowed up everything, so he
was ignominiously booted off the claim. Then the other
one died of a broken heart and overwork, and the snakes
crowded us so bad that we had to leave, although making
250 z. per day each I —P.J.II.

More about rabbit-speed :
“ 0.L.” : To say (“ S.” B. 28/3 ’00) that a smart man can
run a rabbit down on a hot day is about as sensible as to
say that a cow in hobbles can run down a racehorse when
the ice is bearing. Also it takes a mighty smart dog to
run a rabbit down. As to rabbit-coursing with fox-
terriers, rabbits are the most timid animals on earth, and
when set down in a strange place, get absolutely stupid
from fear. A very high authority (Beilby) in the “ Dog
in Australasia” says : “ By no reasoning can a fox-terrier
be considered an equal in pace to the rabbit. . . . For
men to gather in a small enclosed area to hunt captured
rabbits, in many instances so paralysed with fear that
they quietly squat down to be worried, is incredible bar-
barity, and the few rabbits that do attempt to run are

Lamentable facts for the Cold-tea apos-
tolate ! Guinness of Dublin brewed last year
nearly 70,000,000 gallons of stout, using, roughly,
70,000 tons of malt, and paying £091,173 duty.
Bass came next, with about 57,000,000 gals. And
these are only two out of the countless brewing
establishments on this drinky planet.

handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

A Carter’s Catastrophe.
Mr. E. Cummings, who has been connected
with Messrs. Henfrey & Co. Aerated Water
Manfacturers, of 13 Castlereagli-st., Sydney as a
driver and collector for many years, has given our
reporter particulars of the results brought about
by an interesting though unfortunate experience.
“We drivers in large cities,” said he,“ have
long rounds and are exposed to all sorts of weather.
If we get wet through we cannot change our clothes
until we finish. As a result of a wetting three
years ago I contracted rheumatic fever, and I had
to give up work. A sharp pain first came in my
big toe, that apparently being the weakest
spot. Then it extended to my feet and
ankles, and two days later I could not raise
myself in bed. My wife had to put her
arm round me and hold me up while she
fed me. My feet and ankles swelled greatly, and
the pains were excessive round my heart, in the
legs, and in the left side. It was agony for me to
move and I could not let my feet touch the floor.
My doctor paid me every attention, but without
permanent benefit. Sciatica and lumbago came
on, so that I had a continuous gnawing ache in my
thighs and legs and in the muscles of my loins. I
then decided to use Dr. Williams’ pink pills. The
third dose benefited me, and shortly afterwards
the pain lessened a little ; four boxes cured me.
After my recovery I told the doctor that Dr.
Williams’ pink pills had cured me. His reply was
that he had heard of several cases in whicn they
had been efficacious. My cure is permanent for I
have had no return of my ailment. ’
The last remark is very characteristic of the
result obtained by the use of Dr. Williams’ pink
pills ; they do not produce a temporary result like
ordinary medicine, which acts merely on the
symptoms and effects of disease. Dr. Williams’
pink pills remove the cause of disease, and thus
give permanent cures in many different disorders.
Amongst the ailments they have cured are chronic
insomnia, neuralgia, dyspepsia, anaemia, effects of
influenza, heart palpitation, consumption, general
debility, ailments of ladies, and all skin and blood
disorders. Dr. Williams’ pink pills are sold by
the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place,
Sydney, and by all chemists and storekeepers, two
and nine per box, six boxes fifteen and three—But
mind you ask for Dr. Williams’. Mr. Cummings
resides at 128 Campbell-street, Surry Hills.

day and confessed he had been in gaol, but the
Salvation Army would not help him, because his
imprisonment was not recent enough.


CLAUSE 74.
Once upon a time a Lion, assisted by a Kangaroo, made war on a Boar. And when the war was well-nigh over and the Boar was at its last gasp, the Lion said to the Kangaroo, "You fight very well-very well indeed!" And the Kangaroo was beside
himself with joy, as he naturally expected some striking proof of the Lion’s favour. But the Lion continued, "As I previously , you fight very well, and, as a special mark of favour. I'm going to cut off some of your chews!"

Again the Private Railway!
Ln'Theal’s book, “The Portuguese in South
Africa,” issued in 1896, occurs the following p
sage:—
On the 14th Dec., 1833, the Portuguese Govt, granted a
concession for the construction of a railway a -
miles in length, from Lourenco Marques to Komati Poore
on the western boundary. The subsidy offered " as J ’
■tall it was only in March, 1887, that a c° m P a > r he
formed to carry out the work. In November, 1888, t
line was opened to a point which was believed to be
on the Portuguese boundary, though soon
was ascertained to be some distance short, and then,
oould not be completed within the stipulated time, t
Govt, took advantage of the opportunity, and on tne
of June confiscated the railway. Thi3 led to interferen
by Britain and the U.S. on behalf of the shareholders but
after much negotiation the Portuguese authorities
retained the line, and the amount of condensation to >
awarded to the company was referred for decision to
three Swiss lawyers.
The other day the cable told us that the three
Swiss lawyers had unanimously awarded to Great
Britain and America, as compensation for the
shareholders, £640,500 in all, to bear interest from
1889. The cable also said that the award was
two-thirds below the amount expected (amount
expected was £1,920,000)), and the leading papers
“had difficulty in repressing their indignation,
and the Americans talked about rejecting the
decision and recovering “their rights by other
means. However, the three Swiss lawyers are
going to give their reasons, according to a later
cable, and then the Yankees and the British may
have to listen to a lot of truths about themselves.
Australia and Maoriland have both, had bitter
experiences with private railway co. s, and yet
almost every co. that has tormented this portion
of the empire was a reasonable affair beside the
combination that laid its withering paw on
Delagoa Bay. When one reads the company s
history he begins to wonder if the cable has not
made a mistake and if the award, which is to
carry interest since 1889, is not meant for the
Portuguese Government as compensation for the
worry and humbug and breach of contract on the
part of the shareholders.
• • * *
Ever since the days when the Boers smashed
Mosklkkatzk and had time to consider their rela-
tionship with the outside world, they have
directed their eyes towards Delagoa Bay. in
President Burokrs’ time they actually had
railway material brought to the Bay by arrange-
ment with the Portuguese Govt, to connect their
country with the Port. But the Transvaal was
stone-broke then, and the material rusted away
and was stolen by the “ Dagos ” as old iron.
After the retrocession the idea again cropper. up,
and the Transvaal began to build railways and
informed the Portuguese Govt, that if it would
build a line from the Bay to the Transvaal frontier
at once the Transvaal would continue it on to Pre-
toria. This meant practically new life to
the Dago port, for gold-mining was beginning
to pay in the Transvaal, and there were
tremendous possibilities in the scheme. So
the poor Dagos struck the brilliant idea of
building the line by means of a subsidised co.
They granted a concession to Edward MacMurdo,
an American Colonel, and in return for building
the line he was to be allowed to work it for 99
years, and charge his own tariff, and to receive
a huge subsidy. Knowing what a hurry the
Transvaal was in, the Portuguese bound the con-
cessionaire to send an engineer to Lourenco
Marques within 40 days from the date of the sig-
nature of the agreement, to examine the line, for
which trial surveys had already been made by the
Portuguese Government. Also, he was to present
his completed plans for the line within 140 days
from the same date. The plans, in fact, had mostly
been already prepared by the Portuguese authori-
ties. But the Dago officials did not know the
ways of the syndicate. Edward MacMurdo
had as much idea of building the line as The
Bulletin has of irrigating Hades. He wanted to
make his golden fortune out of the business by one
stroke. He wanted to sell the concession. Un-
fortunately he could find no buyers at first, so he
had to makeapretence of doing something. When
bis engineer, Alpuovidge, went out to Delagoa
Bav it did not take the Portuguese long to dis-
cover that he was there merely as a matter of form.
When the 140 days expired in which the plans of
the whole line should have been prepared, he had
bare!v completed the plans for the first section of
ten kilometres or so. Even then the concession
bad lapsed by breach of contract, but the Portu-
guese extended the time and anxiously inquired if
they could render any assistance. Then the
engineer took a long, long rest.
• * • •
Meanwhile, far away in Europe, MacMurdo
was fooling about with delientures, forming com-
panies to buy the concession (he formed at least
four companies at different times), and buying the
concessions back from the companies, and jobbing
in shares, and indulging in all the curious, mys-
terious juggling by which the gold is poured
many times through the same hands and
diminishes in Quantity as it pours Mao-
Murdo had raised the wind on debentures
in London once, when lie suddenly found
that he could raise it a lot cheaper in Brus-
sels, and he broke faith with the London lenders
and’straightway commenced more juggling. All
the while the Transvaal was fuming and threaten-
ing to find another route to the sea, and the
Portuguese were lieginning to see through
the ways and methods of the co. The weary
Dago Government entreated and threatened by
turns. MacMurdo complained that Ins origi-
nal agreement to build the bridges of stone ai)il
iron and brick was the cause of the delay. J he
Govt, implored him in the name of the Virgin to
build them of timber or anything else, so long as
lie built them and got his trains running. I lien
MacMurdo wanted more concessions. He wanted
to carry the line round sharper comers and up
steeper grades and spend less on station accommo-
dation. The Government let him have his own
way. Then the co. wanted another year to com-
plete the line in, and said it might have the good
luck to get the works commenced before J une,
1836. It had been fooling ever since Dec., 1883,
and not yet made a start. About May 18th, 1886,
the co. discovered that it could not possibly start
work by June of that year, and without a blush it
proposed that the Govt, should start building the
line itself, with its own money, and later on, when
the co. had made arrangements for cheaper capital
in Brussels, it would take the works over if it felt
so inclined, at a price to lie agreed upon. Why
the Portuguese Government did not at once take
over the whole business is i nly to be explained by
its amazing reverence for British and American
capital. But, anyhow, the thickheaded Portuguese
actually did commence to build the company’s rail-
way with their own cash. They started the work in
fear and trembling, and found that nothing dread-
ful happened, and that every day the line crept
further inland. Then they began to regret that
they had fooled around with the boodlers. A
British engineer came along and told the Com-
mandant-General that his Portuguese Government
was composed of drivelling asses, as the
co. would bleed the country white in 99 years
if allowed to charge its own tariffs. _ lie
glinted out that even in England the British
ovt. didn’t allow the co.’s to charge their own
rates, but kept some sort of control over them,
and besides, there was always competition, whereas
the MacMurdo co. had stipulated that no com-
peting line was to lie built for 91) years. 1 lien
the Dagos made reflections u|>on the wisdom of
their fathers, and cursed the 14th of December,
1883. Just about this time it dawned on
MacMckdO and Co. that the Delagoa Bay line,
instead of being the “cat” they had all along
imagined, and a mere means of Stock Exchange
jugglery, was a really promising work, and
thev (iii March. 1887) flabbergasted the men of
Lisbon by taking over the line, and paying the
Govt, cash-down for the part it had built (though
it got the part very cheaply), and starting work.
• • • *
The concession expired on October 30th, 1887,
and the Hue was only half built. The last section
was not even commenced, and what was made was
in an altogether half-fledged state. None of it
was ballasted, and culverts and bridges on the
section the co. itself had built were only
the temporary structures with which to carry on
the work of construction. The co. asked for an-
other three months. It got it. '] l ic.-e Portuguese
were awful fools, they had no Dick Seddon among
them. Then tlieeo. wanted to open the half-made
line for traffic. The Govt.. consented, and the line
was declared open. Shortly after it was working,
floods came along and wrecked it in spots, washing
awav six of the half-made bridges, and bunged it
all up. The line mostly closed again. Repairs
went on at a snail’s pace. Meanwhile, the ex-
tended time was rapidly drawing to a close. The
co. said it couldn’t complete the line to the
border m the border wasn’t fixed, and never
had been quite certain. The Govt, fixed the
border at the pass of Incomati. This was about
five and a-half miles bevond the place which the
co. had hitherto regarded as its terminus, and the
co. said it must issue more debentures, and it
wanted an enormous compensation for making an-
other five and a-half miles, and it wanted the
Govt, to issue a decree that would clear up any
possible doubt that the co. could charge what it
pleased in the way of rates, and it started a scheme
for forcing the Transvaal Govt, to let it make all
its lines too. But the last demand, for com-
pensation fur finishing its own line, was too
much. The Portuguese worm turned. It swore
by the head of Camoens and Henry the Naviga-
tor that if the line was not finished right up to the
pass of Incomati in eight months, being the end
of the extended contract time, it would confiscate
the whole show as provided by the agreement.
The co. declared that eight months was not enough
time in which to make miles of railway.
Finally when the 24th of June arrived the co. was
only starting on the 5$ miles section and had
cleared about a mile of scrub, and the Portuguese
Govt, kept its win'd and took possession. 1' or once
it rose to Dick Seim ion's level. By July 20 the
Govt. Imd made arrangements to complete the
hue, which it has since finished and worked.
• • * •
For all that delay and those many breaches of
contract oil the co.'a part, the Govt, paid the co.
£28,000, and the Swiss tribunal has awarded
another* £612,500. with interest, and the co. is not
satisfied and is trying to work up its two Govts,
with their huge fleets to bounce the Dagos ty pay
two-thirds more. And somehow it doesn’t look a
fair thing. Over £040,000 seems a pretty high
tiriee Hi pay for 47 miles of mostly unballasted
line with wooden bridges many of which had
already fallen in, and absurd gradients, and
practically no rolling stock. It was close on
£I4,IKK) a mile, and the Transvaal lines, over
rather more difficult country, only cost £lO,OOO
a mile completed, with all rolling-stock. More-
over, the eminent engineer whom the Swiss
arbitration court sent out to overhaul the lino, de-
clared that the cash value of the 47 miles of ram-
shackle railway was only £225,000. The co. had
spent several times this amount in boodling and
office expenses, and commissions, and pickings,
ami stoals, but the legitimate outlay on the rail-
wav was only £225,000. And the co. has been
awarded over £640,000. And it is raving mad
liecause it didn’t get £l,ooo,ooo—ftbout nine times
the cash value of its property. Truly the daughter
of the horse-leech iau t a circumstance to the rail-
way syndicate.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Transvaal War : Boers v. Bourse.
News heading: “Mr. Chamberlain feels
the Australian pulse.” Yes, but Mr. Chamber-
lain has not yet listened to the normal Australian
heart.
In S.A. Leg. Council, a chamber of 24
members, the Liberals are now in a minority of
four, and, unless Federation brings an unexpected
change, S.A. Council will be as effective a barrier
to Liberal measures as of old.
The first N.S.W. Parliament under Re-
sponsible Govt, met six months after the pro-
clamation of the Constitution. The world moves
more quickly now, and the Federal Parliament
should get together well inside the term allotted
in the Bill.
When the N.S.W. Govt, begins to over-
haul the titles to the proposed-to-be-resumed area
beside Darling Harbor, Sydney, it will come on
some interesting news. And unless the facts are
smothered the people will come on some interest-
ing news also.
To settle a vexed question, somebody
should ask a prominent Vic. politician whether
the land lying between the Deddick and Snowy
Rivers and N.S.W. boundary was withdrawn
from selection whilst he was in power and had a
lien over certain cattle running thereabouts.
Late cables indicate that the Boers, realis-
ing that the utter destruction of the Johannesburg
mining plant and workings would be the only
way to actually strike the real authors of the war,
have not abandoned all idea of, in the last resort,
using dynamite where it will do most harm.
After all, it appears that the N.S.W.
Licensed Victuallers’ Association does not consist
of over 300 members, and that of these a large
proportion are spirit-merchants and brewers. Yet
this is a body which for years has awed poli-
ticians, and flaunted its power to make and
unmake Ministries !
Letter from a recently-imported Finn to
Cairns Advocate
But we may be “ law-abiding citizens” because the
Government of Queensland has lurked us with illusory
reports to come to this unlucky country where we found
more capitalistic anarchie than at home.
“ Lurked us with illusory reports ” is particularly
quaint and picturesque, and deserves to be remem-
bered . >
Next year’s census will probably upset
very badly the annual estimates of the population
of these provinces. Last census did
the same, and there is no reason to
believe that things have improved.
In Maoriland and Tasmania, where
all arrivals and departures are by
sea, the official estimates should be
accurate, for all such arrivals
can be recorded ; but in
the provinces with a long
land border, where popu-
lation goes to and fro
in droves without at-
tracting any special
notice, it is quite
different. There are
some curious features about
these estimates. For in-
stance, out of the 10 years,
1889-1898, the records
assert that in 9 years
N.S. Wales gained
largely by excess of
arrivals by sea,
while in 7 years it
lost heavily by ex-
cess of departures
overland. Unless
someone can show
a reason why people
should naturally come
to N.S.W. by steamer
and leave it on foot or
per train or bullock-dray,
this sheds a lot of doubt on the estimates.
An accommodating paper is Sydney Daily
Telegraph. In its leading article on Premier
Lyne’s knighthood he is described as being “ both
in public and private life sans pew and sans re-
pruche.” And yet there was a time ! Where now
are the snows of autumn—the virtues of the D.T.’s
discarded idol Reid?
Queensland Govt, exports the flower of
the province’s young men as Absent-Minded
Beggars, and imports Russians, Finns, Swedes and
others under the scheme of immigration. This
policy is, of course, highly approved by such cor-
porations as the Chillagoe Manes and Railway Co.
as said imports work for 4s. or ss. per day.
The natural question of a scoffing foreigner
on the night when every Australian city went
mad over the raising of the little siege of Mafeking
(defending force about 700; British force assisting
outside under Plumer about 1100 ; Boer attacking
army at first about 5000, afterwards about 2500):
“MonGott” or else “Meig Dieu, did you Aus-
tralian people never have a biy fight ?”
Household suffrage has lost one supporter
as a result of the S.A. Council elections. Four
sought re-election and two were defeated, but in
the Central district one Labor member was dis-
placed by another. In ’99, at a general referen-
dum, 50,000 electors asked for household suffrage.
The aggregate vote which returned opponents at
the recent Council election was only 16,000. So
the anomaly is seen of 50,000 being beaten by
16,000.
An English paper points out that the
existing Privy Council appeal is not to the Judicial
Committee, but to the Queen-in-Council, and the
distinction is more than verbal, for
there are cases in the books, involv-
ing political questions, when the
committee has not been exclusively
composed of lawyers ; and the Lord
President, not being a lawyer, has
occasionally been a member of
the board. In other words, what-
ever it may be in practice, in
principle the Privy Council as
a Court of Appeal is radic-
ally unsound, since the
Judicial Committee can
at any time be packed
by politicians to secure
a verdict based, not
on law and legal know-
ledge, but on political
bias or necessity.
‘ ‘ Hoodoo ”: Why
so much pother about
the Early Closing Act
driving people out of
shop-keeping ? Com-
merce and Law and
Banking—none of them
wealth-producing fac-
tors—have about rid-
den this country to a
standstill, and any legisla-
tion which drives the people out of these
employments into farming, squatting, mining,
manufacturing or other wealth-producing occupa-
tions is a blessing in disguise. For years the
country has been bossed—entirely in their own
interests—by hucksters, big and little, lawyers and
bankers. Let the commercial progress of the
country have a rest, and let us see if the agricul-
tural, mining, manufacturing, and general wealth-
producing interests cannot be raised to the same
status as the commercial.

Kingston is still determined not to stand
for the Federal Parliament till he has secured
reform of S.A. Council franchise. The House of
Lords he looks upon as quite a democratic body
compared to the local upper chamber. He appeals
to 150,000 Assembly electors who will vote at
Federal contests to boycott every candidate who
refuses to help them in liberalising their own
suffrage.

A couple of thousand pounds was raised
among the N.S.W. State school-teachers for the
so-called Patriotic Fund. “A striking proof oi
what can be done by united effort,” remarks a
Sydney daily. At least fifty teachers have written
The Bulletin denouncing the departmental cir-
cular commending the fund to their notice as dis-
guised blackmail. Pay or oe secretly black-
listed ” —is what it conveyed to them.
Despite the rumpus lateiy raised con-
cerning tno appointment oi two mere boys,
sprigs ox Queensland nobility, as lieutenants
in the fourth contingent, the infants con-
cerned actually went as officers after all. In the
face, however, of certain recent appointments in
Bananaland civil service, this affair was only a
flea-bite. As to the Queensland public—it oan
stand and does stand any sort of insult.
At Bunbury (W.A.) two men were dis-
cussing whether John Forrest was Federal or
anti-Federal. The dispute raged over eight beers
without getting any forrarder, and at last one
arguer said: “No use quarrelling. Let’s toss
up ! ” So a penny was sent spinning in the air.
It struck a chair during its descent, rolled across
the floor, fell into a crack, and—stood up on edge !
Even so small a thing as a penny wouldn’t commit
itself about Bigjohn’s politics.
Of the eight candidates who succeeded at
the S.A. Council elections, May 19, Bice, Gordon,
and Kirkpatrick are on the side of electoral reform,
and Duncan, Lucas, Pascoe, Stirling, and Vardon
against it. Bice, Duncan, Pascoe, Stirling, and
Yardon are S.A. natives, Lucas and Kirkpatrick
are Irishmen, and Gordon is Scotch. Kirkpatrick
and Vardon, who are colleagues in the Central
district, are both printers, and Kirk once worked
as a journeyman for his Tory colleague.
Chamberlain is of opinion that the Wes-
tralian goldfields don’t require Separation because
the new electoral law gives them a little more
representation than they used to have (it provides
that there shall be about one-tenth as much repre-
sentation per head on the goldfields as in the
squatting districts). Therefore the goldfielders
will have to think of ways and means for convinc-
ing Joseph of his error, and if there is a Cromwell
among them this is the time to trot that Cromwell
out.
R. D. Meagher, M.L.A., referring to his
heads of income before the Full Court, the other
day, omitted all mention of his Parliamentary £6
a-week. Legally he was right. Through the statute-
fiction that the £3OO a-year is a recoup of expenses
incurred,, a N. member who earns nothing
otherwise can actually testify in a court of law
that he has no income whatever. The statute
would not have passed when it did only that anti-
payment members were placated by the specious
wording of the Bill.
Let the community not hastily and hys-
terically erect big war-memorials of any sort; let
it sit down and wait. In six months after peace
comes the glitter will have vanished from the
alleged “glories” of the Transvaal war; in two
years the memory of the thing will be covered
with fetid slime. The Jingo game will, of course,
be to rush the erection of a VVemher-Beit dead-
house. Let us not hastily proceed to commemor-
ate a sordid crime the recollection of which will
stink down all along the ages.
Rumored in Maoriland that a great pub-
lic works policy, and another great boom and
burst, involving an expenditure of £4,000,000 of
loan money, will soon be announced. Maoriland
c °uld raise £2,500,000 of this as a permanent loan
without interest merely by making the issue of
bank notes a State monopoly, and by adopting the
Canadian system of requiring the hanks to keep
40 per cent, of their cash reserves in State notes.
±>ut the British loan leprosy is too deeply rooted
m the bones of this unhappy country.
The letter of Symon, Q.C., to the Age re
its attitude towards enemies of the Commonwealth
-Bill, is about the roughest kicking of an editor
that a great daily autocrat in Melb. ever permitted'
to happen in his own columns. Evidently David
oyme thought his long footnote an adequate anti-
dote, but discovering later it was nothing of the
kind, he returned to the charge, and called Symon
C.C., the animal that was “the crapulous creation
or Dean Swift’s nasty imagination.” Which implies
great tenderness in the Symean withers.

For the first time in S.A. history, saving
a small break, the Legislature is without a King-
ston. George Strickland Kingston, the sturdy
father of the present ex-Premier, was the first
Speaker of S.A. Assembly. Away back in ’45
through his efforts the province escaped being
made a dumping-ground for convicts, and he lea
the opposition to state-aid to religion a little later.
It is a coincidence that the father strongly, but
ineffectually, opposed the property-qualification
tor the Council which the son is now, 40 years
after, attempting to remove.
The elections in several S.A. Assembly
divisions and in the N.E. Council district are con-
trolled by the German vote, and in many cases
the voters can neither speak nor read English,
lhey take their politics in that, and most other
cases, from the Adelaide German newspaper,
winch is hopelessly Tory. Despite the patriotic
fervor on the subject of the Transvaal war which
has recently swallowed up much of S.A.’s common
sense, it is still a fact that while thousands of
naturalised aliens are enfranchised for the Leg.
Council, the vote is refused to tens of thousands
of natural-born Australians. Out of 152,000 adults
province and on the Assembly roll only
4o,0(M) have the Council suffrage.
Manchester Guardian correspondent—
to sample opinions expressed all over Britain, and
never voiced in the Australian dailies :
It is as certain as anything in this world can be that
Conner), Bright and Gladstone— these great ones of the
past would have thrown all the weight of their influence
against this iniquitous financiers’ war No
true Radical can observe the spectacle of these two tiny
Republics slowly gasping out their life’s blood in the iron
grip of the most powerful monarchy in the world without
feeling strongly moved, and when we know that the
power of England has been set in motion by capitalists
with a view to oust white labor in favor of compulsory
native labor our indignation is increased. When we con-
sider that wages form 55 per cent, of the cost of working
the mines, and that wages in the Transvaal are higher,
with shorter hours of labor, than in Kimberley, we see a
strong motive for the war on the part of the capitalists.

CONFESSIONAL.
(F OR THE BULLETIN.]
God of the Jingo, brass and gold—
Lord of file ravening millionaire—
Beneath whose hateful sway we hold \
Honor and Faith as light as air—
Mammon and Mars, he with us yet,
Lest we forget—lest we forget.
ne ever-present wranglings cease ;
The conflicts of the Market go ;
For, in the name of pure-eyed Peace,
We turn to rend a weaker foe.
Mammon and Mars, be with us yet,
Lest we forget—lest we forget.
We go to mend the bruisfed reed,
We go to the smoking flax,
To succor brethren sore at need,
To liberate the groaning Blacks.
Father of Lies, be with us yet,
Lest we forget—lest we forget.
We crush a budding nation’s life,
We fill a people’s heart with pain,
We kindle unrelenting strife—
We win a Goldfield for our gain.
Mammon and Mars, be with us yet,
Lest we forget—lest we forget.
We will avenge our brother’s ill,
We will become the orphan’s shield,
Lest we forget Majuba Hill,
Lest we forget the Monthly Yield.
For canting phrase and boastful words
Are incense to our dreadful Lords.
TUR BUICH[?]
