SYDNEY.
The Sydney Wharf Resumptions.
The Lyne Government’s great project for the
resumption of the wharves and wharf-frontages of
Darling Harbour, Sydney, has many good points
about it—also it has a large number of noticeable
deficiencies. The proposed resumption doesn’t
extend far enough back to give any opportunity
for straightening and widening the hideous tangle
of slum streets and slum lanes and blind alleys
which lie between George-street and the water-
side. That is one very bad defect to begin with,
for a great deal of the f uture value of the property
will depend on the ability of the Government to
afford some sane means of access to it. Also it
doesn’t go far enough back to make provision for
railway extension to Dawes Point. The scheme
is an isolated patch on a dilapidated old garment,
and it doesn’t appear to be associated with any
plan for the repair and reconstruction of Sydney
m general. Nothing definite has been heard yet
of any intention to base the resumption on the
existing municipal valuations the valuations
which the owners all accepted as being quite high
enough, or else a great deal too high, when it was
a quastion of paying taxes on them —with a fair
percentage added for “disturbance.” Apparently
a tribunal of some kind is to be appointed, as
usual, to decide values, irrespective of the values
which have been decided already; and it will be
guided by “experts” as usual; and the expert
who swore quite recently that a property was
worth £10,300 when it was a question of paying
land-tax or probate duty on it will turn up as
usual and swear that it is worth £35,000, and
be listened to quite gravely as a reliable
witness. And the country is still waiting to hear
that there will be some clause in the Resumption
Act, providing that, as soon as the newly-acquired
property begins to pay interest on its cost and
leave a surplus, that surplus will bo applied to pay
off the debt incurred in the resumption, instead of
being dumped into the goneral revenue while
the debt stands for ever. The Bulletin is
waiting in a very loud voice to hoar something on
this latter point particularly.
♦
About Ben Harkus. Writes a N.S.W.
trooper from the Transvaal: —
It woa very sad about Ben Harkus, poor chap. He gave
way to his trouble too much. I reckon myself, public
opinion drove him here, and public opinion killed him,
and nobody will ever make me believe that it was
cowardice that took him home in the first place. As soon
as we got word of his death, we got together a firing
party, and started for town to perform our last sad duty,
sending on a fatigue party ahead to dig the grave. It
was late before we got him buried, along with three
others, also victims to the fever. It was a most im-
pressive sight, four open graves, the tall elderly form of
the pastor clearly defined in the candle light, and his
steady solemn voice as he read the burial service.
When one man's name is idenlifiod with a
great public question another man with a similar
name breaking into the question should by some
means endeavor to protect the public from a
natural mistake. A letter of Mr. G. B. Barton’s
in the London press, adversely criticising certain
principles of the Commonwealth Bill, points this
remark. Fifty per cent, of the readers of the
letter took it to be from the pen of the writer’s
brother, Mr. Edmund Barton, and for a time
could not be made to understand the delegate’s
apparently contradictory positions. By the way,
many will regret to hear that Mr. G. B. Barton
has been d rostrated by a serious attack of
paralysis.
The Cremorne land over which North
Sydney and Mosman Bay Borough Councils rend
one another, arid which the Lyne Govt, will need
to handle very warily in the public interests, was
in the first instance alienated to the father of the
late politician, ex-Premier and Minister for Lands
John Robertson. Robertson sen. struck Sydney
in 1820 with a wife and six children, living there
for over a dozen years and speculating freely in
inland and water-frontage properties. He after-
wards took to farming on the Hunter, where young
Jack picked up the elements of the land-knowledge
which made him in later years a power in N.S.W.
Parliament. North Shore Milson, like Robertson,
coveted water frontages, and in 1833 the latter
bought Cremorne from the former for £4OOO.
A N.S.W. Minister, whose magnificence
since he reached “ Hoffis ” is almost too great for
this poor country to stand, lately left Sydney for
the way-back to lay the foundation of a small post-
office. He took a stone with him ; but just before
he started the awful fact broke in upon him that
in the rough and tumble of a long journey the
stone might get broken, or stolen, or mislaid, and
so he wired instructions to have a duplicate
instantly prepared on the spot; he wasn’t going to
lose that chance for the sake of a £5 note —of some-
body else’s money. But when the stone arrived
safe and sound the question arose what should be
done with the duplicate. Just at this point some
obsequious person remembered that there wris a
half-finished school-building in the district. “Ha,
ha ! ” said the Great Man, “ I’ll give the stone to
the Education Department, and I’ll lay it at the
school! ” So he and some public officials went
from the post-office to the half-built school, and
there the Important Person breathed a speech
about Himself and education in the buzzing ears
of the reporters.
Artist John Longstaff has been commis-
sioned by Premier M‘Lean to design a medal to
be given to the Vie. Boldiers who have served in
S’ Africa.
Stipendiary Magistrate Delohery only re-
ceived the appointment to the vacancy on N.S.W.
Public Service Board after a keen struggle with
Mr. F. Bridges, Chief Inspector of the Public
Instruction Department. Mr. Bridges has now
been appointed as a temporary member of the
Board for one month during the absence of Mr.
G. A. Wilson, the only non-civil servant on the
Commission. Other public servants on it have
been Govt. Statist Coghlan, Collector of Customs
Powell, and Under-Secretary for Lands Houston.
A curious feature about this last appointment is
that for four weeks Mr. Bridges will sit in judg-
ment on the recommendations of the Under-
secretary for Public Instruction who for the rest
of the year is his superior officer.
As Edmund Barton holds no seat in
N.S. W. Parliament he will not, on his return, be.
able to give effective aid to the Governor-General
in arranging the first preliminary Cabinet. To
give him the locus standi his federal position en-
titles him to in this matter some members talk of
again making room for him in the Assembly by
resignation, but others more fittingly propose
that Lyne should appoint him Vice-President of
the Leg. Council. In that capacity he would have
a seat in the Cabinet and could, without any charge
of irresponsibility, lend valuable assistance to tjie
Governor-General. Premier Lyne, despite gossip
to the contrary, cares little about being first
Federal Premier, especially as the first Cabinet
business will be almost wholly an arrangement of
details.
Tlie long and bathetic cablegram' from
“ C. Arthur Pearson,” sent to Melb. Akgus and
published by other Australian dailies, concerning
“ a worthy memorial in London to those who have
fallen in the S’African war,” is wholly and solely
the cold-blooded handbill of a cheeky Cockney
advertiser. “C. Arthur Pearson” was erst-
while the Man Friday of one Newnes, who,
beginning public life as boss of that eminent and
elegantly-named thief-paper, Tit-Bits, engineered
himself into a baronetcy. Pearson has been
running a scrap-paper of his own—and so success-
fully that he has been able to start a daily ; which
he now seeks to advertise. The first time Aus-
tralia hears of Pearson’s daily paper it is in con-
nection with his publication of a since-contradicted
yarn to the effect that Uncle Kruger had “humbly
sued for peace.”

Where is the President of the Legislative Council now?
Hitherto I thought it was only the dogs of Constantinople
that fed on stolen garbage.
Let me assure the President of the Council, who, in his ■
own person furnishes one good reason for its abolition—
that my mental health is excellent. Even that enquiry
he either borrowed or purloined—your President is such
“ a barren rascal.” Yet it was complimentary to attribute
it to my “powerful intellect.” His friends need have no
solicitude on that account. Their fears arise from quite
another cause. They find that a weak intellect and a dull
moral sense are the most frequent sources of aberration—
sometimes, indeed, of imbecility.
“ Tfingata ” to The Bulletin from
Dunedin: —
The recent annual meeting of the M.L. Women’s
National Council, at Dunedin, ended in an unexpected
flare-up. So long as the ladies confined themselves
to “ The Relation of the Sexes,” “ The Servant Girl Pro-
blem,” and such, the local Jingoistic type-liars had
nothing but praise for the “ combined intelligence of the
women of Maoriland ” —even when one delegate went so
far as to state “ that any man who poisoned the atmosphere
with tobacco-smoke was as guilty as the Boer who
poisoned the wells for the British soldiers.” But what a
change when one daring female person announced that
she hoped for the success, in this war, of those defending
their country against the greed of unscrupulous mono-
polists. Then did the bloodthirsty pen-and-ink warriors
arise in their wrath ; the Mayor in a lofty letter with-
drew his “ patronage ” from the Women’s Council’s final
conversazione, and even the local “Melba” got a cheap
“ ad.” by refusing to sing for her unpatriotic sister. Then
the poor Women's Council let down its back-hair and
went sadly into committee to recast its ideas of
“ Humanity " to suit the times.
The appointment by the Lyne Govt, of
Mr. A. A. Atkinson, as Chief-Inspector of N.S.W.
Coal Mines for another three years, proves once
again that appointments objected to by members
when in Opposition may be considered superla-
tively proper when the Opposition becomes the
Govt. The Opposition, three years or so ago,
raised a Parliamentary row because Sydney Smith,
in seeking a Chief-Inspector, inserted advts. to
that end in London papers. Mr. Atkinson was
picked out of 93 applicants, many of whom were
residents of Australia, and was described as being
recommended by the Agent-General and Sir Wil-
liam Lewis, Sir William Lewis is, by the way, a
director of Sydney Harbor Collieries Co., and the
greatest living enemy of the claims for higher
wages of the Welsh miners. The sponsor was not
.above criticism, but so well apparently has Mr.
Atkinson behaved that the Lyne Govt, now re-
appoint him.

CHEAP AND FASHIONABLE TAILORING.
CG. HATTE, of King-street, Newtown (near Sydney,
• N.S.W.), has recently made great additions and im-
provements to his Tailoring Department, which is now one
of the features of his extensive business. He receives regu-
lar shipments, from his Home buyers, of all the newest and
most fashionable goods, and has one of the smartest cutters
in the colony Everything is made to measure on the pre-
mises; a perfect fit is guaranteed, and patrons may rely on
every satisfaction being given. Self-measurement forms
and'patterns sentfree by post. Hatte’s suits of Indigoserge,
all-wool tweeds, and sergerettes (with extra pair of trousers
given in) for 42/- are the marvel of tailoring and the talk of
Australasia. For 50/- Hatte makes a suit of tweed or serge
fit for a prince. Inspect or send for patterns of his new
woollens, comprising vicunas, West of England, Scotch and
Blarney tweeds, at 57/6. and 65/- ; the great value offered.
Hatte makes a smart Chesterfield overcoat from meltons,
vicunas, and Venetians from 45/-, and the new paddock
coat, with full back, side vents, crescent pockets, silk-faced
and velvet collar for 55/-. Hatte’s speciality is in dress-
suits, starting at £3 3s, made of purple dye elastic coating
with silk-facing. For excellence of material, perfect fit,
latest fashion, and superior workmanship, Hatte is un-
equalled, whilst his charges are at least 30 per cent, under
those of ordinary city tailors.
Government Notices.
PUBLIC SERVICE BOARD,
50 Young-street, Sydney.
18th May, 1900.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of Candidates (male)
for employment as “ POSTAL and TELEGRAPH
ASSISTANT” in the Postal and Electric Telegraph
Department will be held in SYDNEY, and at VARIOUS
COUNTRY CENTRES which will be fixed as far as
possible to suit the convenience of candidates, on the
2nd JULY NEXT. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made is 100. Candidates must not be less
than 16, nor more than 19 years of age on the Ist day of
examination. They must be of good character, and in
sound health. Persons already employed in the General
Division of the Public Service will he admitted to the
examination, provided their age does not exceed 39 years.
The subjects of examination will be as follows : —(1) Hand-
writing, (2) English, (a) Dictation, (b) Composition,
(3) Arithmetic, (4) Geography. The successful candidates
will be appointed on probation as vacancies occur, and be
paid at the rate of £39 per annum for the first year, and
£52 for the second year. On completion of the second
year of service they will be examined for permanent
appointment, on passing which they will be entitled to
receive £BO per annum, with periodical increments under
the terms of the Regulations, copies of which can be
obtained at this office. In future, vacancies for Telegraph
Messengers at Branch, Suburban, and Country Offices
will be filled as far as practicable by candidates who pass
this examination, on the understanding that they deliver
telegrams, letters, etc., and assist in other postal and
telegraph duties as required, and that they be styled
Postal and Telegraph Assistants, and shall not be required
to wear uniforms. Application to attend the examina-
tion must be made on a form for the purpose, which can
be obtained at the office of the Board, or from Clerks of
Petty Sessions in country districts, and must reach the
undersigned not later than the 11th June next. Candi-
dates will be required to pay an examination fee of 55.,
which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty Sessions or
the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to the
Secretarv of the Board, with the form of application.
Persons already employed in the Public Sen ice will not
be required to pay any examination fee.
By order of the Board.
9a96j J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

Cloth
Writes a lady to The Bulletin
Sydney postal authorities rarely use their common
sense except when not required. A lady recently acci-
dentally dropped a parcelled-up pair of gloves into the
letter-receiver of a local P.O. Naturally, she went right
in and asked for them. “They cannot be returned to
you, madam,” replied the clerk ; “ they have been
posted in the ordinary way, and must now go straight on
to the dead-letter office.” The lady also went straight o n
to the dead-letter office, but did not receive her gloves.
The official had no option ; as the placing of the
parcel in the receiver made it, pro tern., the pro-
perty (legally) of the Postmaster-General.

THE BOARD AND THE VELVET.
“A Thkone,” said Napoleon, “ is a board covered
with velvet.”
Strip the velvet from the throne, and you have
nothing left but bare, vulgar boards ; replace the
velvet and you have the most coveted symbol ot
human power and glory. How easy the transition,
how vast the difference !
There is no operation in chemistry more sharp
and sudden than that in human life whereby ex-
tremes of feeling follow each other— tears rarefy-
ing into smiles, and smiles condensing into tears.
Is happiness, or is power, so poor a thing, then,
that it drops into its antithesis at a touch ? at a
breath ? Let us not be too hasty with our answer,
as we may be wrong. The great French Emperor
was a cynical fellow, and right well he loved a
throne, even though it was only an upholstered
board.
And we all love life and its blessings even
though they are uncertain and shaky.
Hence, when we hear a man say, “I had no
pleasure in life, ami did not care what became of
me,’ we are interested to know the reason why.
The person from whom we quote these words
explains himself: —
■‘For over two years,” he tells us, “I suffered
from loss of appetite, sleeplessness, and nervous-
ness. Prior to May, 1894, I had always been
strong and hearty. At this time I began to feel
that something had come over me—l felt so low
and weak. After eating my face would flush, and
the food gave me great pain across my chest and
at the left side. 1 had a cutting pain around the
heart, and bad attacks of palpitation.”
1 beg to interrupt our good friend a moment at
this point. The burning of a barn or a hayrick
may make a bigger blaze than the burning of the
cottage we live in. But the latter alarms and ex-
cites us most because we do live in it. On the
same principle a very painful ailment of the hand
or foot may cause little or no mental anxiety,
while a disturbance of the heart’s action does, for
the heart is one of the three houses which life
resides in, the other two being the brain and the
lungs. Yet, as generally happens in so-called
heart troubles, the worry was needless, as we shall
presently see.
“For weeks together,” continues the narrator,
“I got no proper sleep, and, in truth, so bad was
this condition that / dreaded going to bed. My
nerves were thoroughly unstrung, and affected the
left side of my face, which was quite drawn. I
suffered martyrdom with facial neuralgia.
“ As time went on I grew to be so low and
miserable that i had no pleasure in life, and did
not care what became of me. I consulted a doctor
but none of his medicines helped me. Better and
worse, I continued to suffer, until a friend told
me about Mother Soigel’s Curative Syrup, and
persuaded me to try it. I got a bottle from Mr.
Pulliam, Crocer, Spring Road, and after taking it
a short time I felt it was doing me good, ( slept
well, and had less distress after meals. This en-
couraged me to persevere with it, and gradually I
got stronger, and the nerve pains wore away. I
now enjoy good health , and have recommended this
medicine to many of my customers. You can
publish this statement as you like. (Signed)
Harry Wenden, Hairdresser, 171, Spring Road,
St. John’s, Ipswich, July 17th., 1890.”
Mr. Wenden s explanation of his loss of life’s
pleasure is commonplace after all. And yet how
much more important than if it were unique or ex-
ceptional ; because the commonplace is the uni-
versal. it is disease, my gentle reader, that tears
the velvet from thrones, that robs the cottager of
his sleep, that makes the baby cry in its cradle,
that strips the strong man of his vigour, that wipes
the bloom from the cheeks of fair women, that
hurries humanity to the churchyard with bowed
heads and bleeding feet. And the most pitiless
ogre of all diseases is the one from which Mr.
Wenden suffered, and which Mother Seigel’s
Syrup cures—indigestion, dyspepsia. Even
without the velvet, Health is the best of thrones,
and this great remedy helps to keep you seated
safely and happily upon it.
His Honor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Me. Julian Fitzboom was roused in his tent-bunk
by the shouts of Selector Small. Fitzboom was
sleepy and disinclined to respond, but, the yells
becoming savage, he pulled on some clothes and
walked across the creek. A man was stretched on
his back in front of the hut, and Selector Small
was sitting on the man’s chest.
“ Caught the beggar at last, Fitz; found him
humping off one of the killing wethers. D’yer
know him ! ”
“Why, it’s Joe Moran. How’d you get your-
self in this mess, Joe?”
Joe groaned and spat some earth off his teeth.
“Now, Fitz, I want you to look after this
gentleman while I go for the traps. Can’t do it
under four hours; but the fire’s alight, and the
billy’s hot; make yourself comfortable. And, one
thing : give me your word of honor you won’t let
this chap escape—l’d rather lose fifty quid. This
is the third time he’s been at it, and the game’s
about played-out. It would be a real sin to let him
off.”
“Honor bright, Small, I’ll keep him safe; you
can trust my word for it.”
“That’s good enough for me, old feller; so
here’s off.”
Save for the spitting of the gum fire and the
quick ticking of the clock, there was silence in the
hut. Fitzboom sat across the door, smoking ; Joe
at the table, thinking. Presently the latter spoke
out his thoughts with a jerk : —“ Mr. Julian, I’ve
a young wife at home.”
“ So 1 believe, Joe, and I’m sorry for it.”
“ And I want to see her afore the traps come —
I want to square things—l want for God
A’mighty’s sake, Mr. Julian, let me out, and I
swear I’ll give myself up in the morning—Mr.
Julian ”
“1 can’t do it, Joe; I’ve given my word to
Small.”
“ Damn Small! What’s Small’s troubles to me
or my missus? I’d have let you go if I’d sworn to
Small on the Bible.”
“ Your ideas of honor and mine run on different
lines, Joe.”
“Put yourself in my place, Mr. Julian, with a
young wife at home sittin’ up and expectin’ yer.
My God ! what’ll she think—what’ll she dp if they
•lag me i Let me out—come with me—tie me up
and drag me, but let me go home this once.”
“I tell you, Joe, I can’t do it. I’ve given my
word to Small. My honor is at stake. I’ll go first
thing in the morning and break it to your wife,
and do what I can to oomfort her. Depend upon
it, I’ll smooth things over for you. It would
ordy make you feel worse to see tier. Far better
face the music first —I know it’s hard ”
“ You don’t know—you can’t know what I feel
—what I mean. I tell yer things have to be
squared. Let mo out, Mr. Julian, and 111 pray
for yer—we’ll both pray for yer—all our lives.
What chance would I have agin them traps if you
did let me go ? I’d be snapped anyhows. What’s
the difference to you? What d’yer want to keep
me for? Have I done uou any harm ? What the
h ” . . _ T ,
“ Come; come, Joe, you re getting excited. Ive
given my word of honor to Small. What would
he say, when he returned, to find you gone ? _ I’ve
told you I’ll comfort your wife as far as lies in my
power—more I can’t say. Be a man and face the
trouble. Be” .
“ Lot me out, Mr. Julian; that’s the biggest
comfort you can do my wife. Just let me go
tell Small anything. That I rushed you—smashed
the door through—anything.”
“ I won’t tell a lie for anyone’s sake.”
“ For my wife’s sake ? ”
“No.”
“Oh, God—God—God !”
And so on, all the weary night.
• * * *
Verdict—guilty. Sentence—two years. But
Joe’s wife was comforted, according to the promise
of Fitzboom.
11l news not only flies fast, but also penetrates
far. It percolated even into Joe’s gaol-yard.
What Joe heard was worse than the Judge’s ser-
mon. For his wife had been “ comforted.” You
see, Fitzboom was a man of his word; his
“ honor ” had been at stake !
C. H. READ.
Rev. Barnabas Cope’s ritualism rapidly
endeared him to the hearts of all Gunyahvilie, but
now he has fallen below zero. Visiting a
matron who was impai-ting maternal nourishment
to her infant, he felt himself at a great loss for
small talk, and, seizing the incident before his
eyes as a possible escape to his dreadful tongue-
tiedness, thus ruined all chance of ever becoming
popular: “Is —is madam—that sweet child
yo’uah youngest ? ”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
First day of S.T.C. Birthday meet at Randwick
was bleak, wet and sloppy ; attendance poor. Betting
made Claymore look a “ cert ” for the Hurdles ; he won
by 10 lengths. La Hogue (outsider) downed Battlefield
(hot favorite) in Amateur Stakes. Taipo and Crusado
were first and second favorites in the “Steeple” and
finished that way, with Taipo a furlong in front (the rest
of the field refused the second hurdle). For Turf Club
Handicap 14 ran ; the uncertain Alix (8 to 1 against) got
home first, 3 lengths in front of Hope. Bar La Hogue,
which was fairly well backed, Ikej 7 had a good time.
Dandy's triumph in Sydney Royal Stakes,
Queen’s Birthday, was a great effort. Twenty-three lined
up. Flying Handicap winner (Bob) at Tattersall’s meet-
ing Saturday before was backed down to fours ; then
came Bulletin’s tip, Oast Iron (sevens) and Dandy
(eights). Half the journey over, Cast Iron was lengths
away from the field and. entering the straight, looked
like finishing solus ; 350yds. from the judge, Cast Iron led
by six lengths, and his backers regarded the race as won
Dandy now showed out: laying his ears back, he over-
hauled the leader yard by yard and o night and beat him
by half-a-neck in the last couple of strides. Rarely
has such a finish been seen. In the final ljfur.
Dandy placed 10 lengths between himself and the balance
of the runners. Rider (Barden) was a mass of mud from
neck to heels. Race was the slowest (1.19.1) Royal Stakes
on record, but conditions were strongly opposed to fast
time. Dandy had 8.10 up, and is by Martini-Henry—
Vain Glory. Same stable, Kelso’s (it had Duke of Kent
fourth), supplied two of the first four on this occasion,
and won with Akarini in ’96 and Caledonia last year.
From a weight-carrying point of view, Dandy’s achieve-
ment is the second-best on record, for, barring Sedition’s
feat with 9.3 in the saddle, in ’B9, latest winner humped
the biggest burden over the journey—6fur.
* * * *
“Gun.” Mayn’t be generally known that up to
the last half-hour Dandy (winner of Royal Stakes at S.T.C.
meeting) was a doubtful starter, and that the night before
Wotton, owner of Ace of Diamonds, who won the Birthday
Cup, had made up his mind to scratch that nag if the rain
continued. The expression “they run in all shapes”
applies more to Ace of D. than to any other equine racing.
He is all angles even now, though carrying more beef than
for a long time. Ilis gallop on Saturday suggests some-
thing a heap better when it’s wanted and he’s fit.
*•» * .
Minister Tom Hassall had bad luck in both the
’chases at Randwick with his recently-purchased Crusado.
On first day the horse was interfered with by one of the
baulkers, which sp >ilt his chance, and on second day he
lost his rider in the lake, which unlooked-for obstacle
proved formidable to every horse in the race.
Trainers as a rule dislike showing their hand
over a maiden at a short price, but J. Burton proved the
exception at Randwick last week, when he won with
Claymore, who started in both hurdle-races at a very
short price, and won easily in each case. Strange to say,
Claymore’s greatest danger in both events lay in jump-
ing through a broken hurdle at the last obstacle.
• • * •
N.S.W. Postmaster-General Crick’s threatened
bill to “clip the wings” of the A.J.O. is heard of. Word
comes from Maitland, that Gillies, M.P., is to introduce it
next session, and that it is to “ define the rights of the
people to the use and occupation of all lands dedicated, or
leased to, or otherwise occupied by companies, clubs’ or
associations for horse-racing purposes, to provide for the
estanlishment of a representative tribunal to control all
matters affecting horse-racing; to repeal in part the
A.J.O. Act, and for other purposes.” Writer thinks it has
just as much show of passing as had Crick’s measure
(upon which it is based) some years ago. The A.J.O.
isn’t doing too badly for those who want to have things
as they should be—howls about ill-treated owners and
Star-Chambered jockeys notwithstanding. The chief de-
fects of the club arise out of the fact that it is conducted
on altogether too haw-haw a basis.
* • * •
Though unlike his brother-in-law, “ Bill ” Long
the late W. B. Dailey was not prominent on the turf he
dabbled in racing in dilettante fashion. In the early days
of Sydney Tattersall’s he used to figure as steward at the
club’s meetings, and occasionally had a horse or two in
training, but never seemed able to get hold of a good one.
* * * •
“H.S.” : As an old “cross-country” man, I
commend the recent alterations in the Randwick (Syd-
ney) “ steeple ’’-course, which now not onlv furnishes a
better test of stamina and all-round cleverness in the
horse, but is, from a spectacular point of view, much im-
proved. Let us hope that the club, having provided
such an excellent “ convincing ground,” will now see its
way to making the steeple prizes worth competing for by
the best horses in Australasia. Then we shall have the
cracks from Maoriland, South Australia, and Victoria tak-
ing a “ fly ” at Randwick—with the certain result of a big
increase in the club’s revenue.
• • » *
In a recent Sydney police-court oase an oyster-
merchant said that the profit to retailers was lOd. per
plate, the actual cost being about 2d. Yet the charge in
the paddock at Randwick racecourse for a plate of oysters
is Is. 6d.

Q.T.C. Autumn gathering at Eagle Farm ended
Saturday with fine weather. Ikey had a good time. Tote
held £4919 ; total for three days, £15,488 10s. Velocity
(at 10 to 1) annexed the New Handicap (6 fur.), and 67 of
1060 tickets drew £6 18s. each. Heavy-weight Handicap
(7 fur.) went to Gadfly—7 to 1 agst. Of 789 tickets 103
picked it and got £3 7s. each. Boreas 11. (5 to l)was first
fa' orite for Moreton Handicap (1J miles) and landed the
money easily from Longford. Machine showed 1052 with
261 on the right pea—they got £2 10s. each. Leger div
went 11s. better. Time was 2.17J —3Jsec. slower than
Dundonald’s win last year. Second Novice Handicap (14
miles) fell to Nor’-West (6 to 1). Of 888, 159 found the
winner and received 295. each. Gozo gelding Ohio was
first home in the Royal Stakes (1 mile and a distance);
he started at sevens and paid £3 15s. to each of 123 of 1063
who played up their half-sovs. Lauri’s number showed at
the finish of Epsom Mile. Books offered sevens ; tote
paid £5 -for every 10s. to 131 punters out of 1504 invest
ments.

At the Q.T.C. meeting, Sir Jean, who won the
High Weight Handicap, paid £52 10s., and the Brisbane
Cup winner—the well-performed Boreas ll.—panned out
£l9 Bs.
* * * *
Adelaide R.C. meeting opened at Victoria Park
on Saturday. Though heavy rain had fallen before,
weather was almost like that in Sydney. Tote showed
£12,149 10s. as compared with £13,241 same day last year.
Fleet Admiral scored at last by annexing the City
Handicap with three-quarters to spare from Howlong and
Harpist as named. Machine held £3261 10s., and div.
was £5. Winner of Adelaide Stakes (Ariadne) by
Thunderbolt—Calirrhoe, paid the biggest amount. £24
19s ; £205 15s. went on.
* * * *
That respected sportsman, W. R. Wilson,
ownqr of the famous St. Alban’s racing-stables, and erst-
while big Broken Hill speculator, died on Sunday night,
after a lingering illness. W. R. made his pile at the
“ Hill ” when piles were made there over and over again,
and put a lot of the money amassed into the breeding
establishment mentioned. His nags won races innumer-
able, but none of them got home in Melbourne Cup,
which was the dead sportsman’s greatest ambition. Mr!
Wilson did much for racing in Australia by ever running
straight and importing new blood ; he brought out aristo-
cratic mares, and was also responsible for the advent of
Bill of Portland and Eiridspord here. At the same time
he had Robinson Crusoe, Trenton, and Carnage in
active service. Some other nags owned by Mr. Wilson at
different times were such big and blue-blooded performers
as Strathmore, Wallace, Elusive, Aurum, Bobadil, Havoc,
La Carabine, and La Tosca. And almost at the same
moment is reported the demise of the plucky and popular
Donald Wallace, whilom owner of Australia’s equine god.
Carbine. H °
• • • •
Trenton’s list for 1900 filled some time ago in
England at 100 guineas a mare. Abercorn (strange to
say), Aurum and Patron are doing none too well at half
that figure. Some authorities think Abercorn was a
better performer than Carbine. What makes the matter
puzzling is that each went off in his fourth season. When
Abercorn was four Carbine was three years old,’ when
Carbine was four Abercorn was five. Still, Abercorn
hasn t done anything equal to Carbine’s Melbourne Cup,
nor his Cup—a still better performance.
** I*. A.” : Phil Hey wood refused to take Carbine
at £3OOO and then gave £3050 for the gigantic Tradition,
who never won a race after. Carbine’s subsequent suc-
cesses are a matter of history.
* * * *
M.L. Govt. proposes giving a practical applica-
tion to the much-mouthed, but little-believed-in theory
that “ horse-racing improves the breed of horses” by set-
ting aside a portion of the totalisator tax to really encour-
age horse-breeding of the right sort.
* * « *
Monday’s Vancouver mail brought bad news for
Australian followers of pugilism. Champion middle-
weight of Kangarooland, Tim Murphy, who went to Frisco
months ago with a flourish, succumbed to Californian
Neill (a comer of first quality) April 27. Battle is said to
have been the fiercest ever seen in the State. Platform
was “hemmed in by howling, swaying thousands.” Fight
lasted 11 rounds. Murphy’s grit and savage punching
were outgeneralled by the Yank who raised big welts over
the Australian s heart. Murphy’s body-blows, when they
did land, shivered the ’Frisco champion from stem to
stern. In the seventh round Tim went to his corner
stooping ;it was the beginning of the end. Bights and
lefts from Australia fairly whizzed in the ninth term,
but Neill blocked or ducked and a stiff right-hander
ultimately felled the boy from these parts. Murphy came
out for the “ last round of this memorable fight ” with gashed
face glaring eyes ; he fought in desperation. A fearful right
on the point sent Tim prone. Game as a pebble he rose
and rose thrice more after being bashed to the floor, and
was a fifth time struggling up when the fatal “ out ” came.
Americans have gone crazy on Murphy’s grit; he got a
terrible heap of stoush, and gave some, too. Crowd was
the largest seen at a fight in ’Frisco for many a day. Two
of Tim s seconds were old Melbourneites—Jimmy Anthony
and Tommy Tracey.
* * » •
. "‘Old Timer” : Saw a tough struggle, Saturday
night last, at Golden Gate Club (Sydnev), between two
developments of the novice-tournevs—Watts and Turner.
It was slog all the way in a 20-round draw. Both were in
prime trim. Turner forced the game, and Watts res-
ponded as if ’twas life to him. Turner fouled frequently,
but fouls don t weigh much with latter-day referees, some
of whom have an interest in the “ gate ” and want to give
the crowd plenty stoush for its money. It would, any-
how, have to be a strong foul or persistent fouling for a
pug battle to be lost that way these times. Haven’t for
many a day seen men ladle it out so hot and thick as
Watts and Turner did. They were strong all the time
and finished strong, though with sundry abrasions, dis-
colorations and fleshy mounds that weren’t noticeable at
the outset. The crowd looking on went crazy, applauding
and yellmg from end to end, bar the minute spells. Said
that same two are shortly going to have another cut at
each other; if so, writer advises all lovers of Fierce Battle
to be there at any cost.
* • • •
Australian pug. Jim Richardson, who was “of
Honolulu for some time and is now “of Newcastle-on-
ryne according to ’Ome papers, scored his first English
win about mid-April by sending an old country pug, one
Cock Robin,” to sleep in four rounds. Richardson
promised well years ago. Ere leaving Svdnev he beat
two good uns in Billy Hefferman and Tom Partridge, and
couldn’t get any more matches on though challenges were
thrown out to all comers at the 11.4 limit. Jim went to
o Africa but wasted time and money there fruitlessly, as
no one would take him on. In England Richardson
fought two or three times previous to his latest match
but always played the part of under-dog, which seemed
strange in face of what he did here.

“Old Timer”: Visited Brighton Hotel, Lady
Robinson s Beach, Sunday last, to see Peter Jackson, and
marvelled at the improvement evidenced. Peter looks
good for another big fight or two ; but, all things con-
sidered, that’s too much to expect. He was lately taken
in hand by a leading doctor, has put on about 121 b.
during the past month, and seems like keeping at it.
Jackson may in time fight one or two limited-round bouts
with any good man who might happen Australiawards,
but his mind is made up to go in for teaching. Two or
three privately-formed classes are already waiting
anxiously till Peter feels up to it. The sciatica has almost
wholly disappeared and the effects of that attack of pneu-
monia, which left life hanging by a hair in the hospital at
Victoria (B.O.) for three months, are fast disappearing
Writer was amused at that picture in a Melb. sporting
paper giving Peter a corpse-like expression in the face.
At his worst the big black never looked so bad as that
and .probably wouldn’t even if he did “ peir out ”
• • * •
The elongated dude-black, Peter Felix, and
Bill Doherty are to have another go, in Sydney for
Australian pug supremacy. A £lOO a-side match was
made on Monday, to eventuate inside seven weeks.
Petah” won’t take very kindly to doffing that long
frock-coat, high hat and aggressive button-hole, which
have been so much in evidence in Bourke-st., Melb for
such a long time past. He’s not too far gone on’the
game either, though clever enough to beat the best
available now, which isn’t saying overmuch for the
champion. On their previous go, writer doesn’t see how
Doherty figures, out his chance of winning as being good,
but the money is up, and money talks.
• • • *
Mailed that that very peculiar pug.-develop-
ment of N.S.W., Tom Lees, and whilom Maitland
(N.S.W.) blacksmith Paddy Slavin indulged in a wild and
woolly struggle at Dawson City (Klondyke) about last
mid-winter. Admission was 25d01. per head, and the
huge barn-like structure was packed with fur-clad gold-
hunters. Slavin was top dog from the jump, but. Lees
shaped full of grit. Half-breeds among the motley throng
were so excited that the Supt. of Police stopped the fight!
Stakes were big and Slavin got them. By-and-by the
police batons had to be brought into requisition, and a
lively half-hour resulted. Latest is that Paddy runs a
big sawmill.
• * * *
“Leash”: National Coursing Ass. of N.S.W.
absolutely excelled itself with opening meeting (last week)
for the season. Hares at headquarters (Rooty Hill) were
reported dying unaccountably weeks ago. Investigation
demonstrated that absolute starvation was the sole cause
Any properly-managed body would have postponed opera-
tions, to give the animals a chance of pulling round * not
80 the N.O. Ass. of N.S.W. It sent up some oats (which
subsequent happenings indicated never reached their
destination), and went on with the show. Result: hares
hardly able to crawl, much less run—you could have
knocked them over with a stick. A little terrier
took to one, and collared it and killed it as if puss
had been a rat. Forty-five (comprising all fit to move. ap-
parently,) of the stock were caught quickly, and settled
one after the other till the thing became monotonous.
The ruling body of N.S.W. Coursing then decided to shut
down despite the fact that two days had been advertised
and the classic events (Oaks and Derby) with 7 and 8
respectively still in, and the Birthday Stakes with 8 left
were unfinished. Nominators of hounds that had not been
beaten got their own subscriptions whacked among them
less the usual 10 per cent, deduction. Worst feature of
the whole thing is, that Belmore Coursing Park and
hares could have been had on reasonable terms. The
outcome is that the honor of winning three of the bit?
events of the year is divided among 23 dogs ! The writer
had something to say about the lack of management or
knowledge of the game evidenced by those running the
N. S. Wales N.C.A. Last week’s happening emphasises
the view then taken.
An attempt is being made to resuscitate the Sir
Joseph Banks (Botany, Sydney) footracing handicaps on a
scale somewhat less ambitious than when the late Frank
Smith was promotor. The smaller prize-money will make
little difference, for the game has sunk so low; that a
tenner will produce more genuine triers than 20 times the
amount would have done ten years ago.

T. Hayward, England’s best batsman, has
started well by making 120 not out against London
County (Grace’s team). Abel made 65 in same match.
For London, A. E. Trott took 7 wickets for 122 in a score
of 294. Grace made 15 and 1, and his side was beaten by
an innings and 34 runs. Richardson failed to take a
wicket in either innings, but Lockwood got 5 for 8.

Clem. Kill has blossomed out as a broker on
Adelaide Stock Exchange.
* • • *
The Vic. Football League made a “patrioti-
cism ” of diplomacy—not to say necessity—on May 19th.
All the club-grounds in Melbourne were unfit for play
owing to the recent soaking rains, so as soon as the cable
news arrived and the mind of the multitude was palpably
“ off the ball,” the League issued its mandate : “ All
matches postponed in honor of Relief of Mafeking.”
• • • •
In the opening match of the football season at
Waihi (M.L.) recently, a half-back had to be ambulanced
early in the game ; thereafter every shin was a legitimate
target. The referee was ornamental and quite harmless,
and blood fairly streamed when hellowing barrackers
took the field, and were repulsed with great loss, some
getting a bad flatironing as a reward for their inter-
ference. The ball meanwhile disappeared, but was
eventually recaptured from a bull-terrier. The affair was
a mixture of Killaloe and Waterloo —and provided a rare
afternoon’s sport.
• * • •
Auckland (M.L.) Rugby Union is creating a
season-fund for disabled players, in preference to handing
over a lump-sum to an insurance co. Knocked-out battlers
will receive £2 a week. _ The Union is also drafting, for
submission to N.S.W. Union, a scheme providing that in
future each province shall finance its own touring teams.

The unconscious humor of the “ comp.” In the
now-defunct Melb. Telegraph, a football report, which
should have read, “ The captain of the visitors, an old
Bendigonian, having won the toss,” &c., was cheerfully
altered to, “ The captain of the visitors, an old beery
onion,” &c.

The N.S.W. rowing centre appears to have
been transferred from the Parramatta to Coraki, where a
successful regatta was brought off last week. The Beaten
Stakes handicap was won by Messenger, 21 secs., who beat
Tressider (2sec.), Pearce (ssec.), Bishop and Kemp (I7sec.),
and Towns (20sec.). Messenger comes of an old racing
stock. His grandfather, in 1853, beat Candlish over the
Thames champion course ; and in ’67 was defeated over
same course by Harry Kelley for the world’s champion-
ship.

A number of newspapers have never ceased
to howl against Lord Methuen, and Syd. D.T. has
published an interview between its S.A. corres-
pondent and “a gentleman who was in an
excellent position to form an accurate judgment,”
in which it is stated that Methuen was “a brave
blunderer.” Now, when Methuen started for
Kimberley his orders were to relieve that city,
which was said to be hard pressed. He had 12,000
men, including artillery, and about one regiment
of cavalry ; as he had no transport to enable him
to make flanking movements he was virtually
chained to the railway. He won three battles in
one week and then found himself up against
Magersfontein, to outflank which Roberts used
60,000 men and killed 10,000 horses. Methuen’s
force had swelled to 18,000, but he was still abso-
lutely without mounted infantry, and had only a
handful of cavalry and was still chained to the
railway. The only attack he could make with the
force at his disposal was a more or less frontal one,
and in the Boer paper, The Volksstkm, it was
admitted that but for the sudden panic of the
Highlanders, the Boers would have abandoned the
position under cover of night as their right wing
(looking south) was utterly crushed, the trenches
filled with slaughter from an enfilading fire.
Suddenly it was noticed that the British were re-
tiring owing to their failure to carry the left wing,
and the Boers, finding the fire die away, held on.
The South African Review, one of Rhodes’
papers, among other denunciations of Methuen
prints this:
That Lord Methuen succeeded in effecting the removal
of a war correspondent who was too outspoken in his
criticisms of Boer treachery and inhumanity in the field.
Looks as though Methuen’s crime was that he
sacrificed the atrocity-mongers and not that he
blundered against the Boers.

“T.D.”: What bucketsful of sentiment were wasted after
all upon the leaden dullness which was to creep over the
Melb. playhouses, the ghostly memories which were to
haunt the Princess’s and so on ! It’s only six months
since Williamson had a big and tearful leave-taking and
the Broughs bade us good-bye for two long years, yet
Williamson is on the job again with his Alex., the
Broughs are working back to us, and the Princess’s is
going strong for remainder of the year at least.
Dear Bulletin. — Re the recent statement in your
columns that Steffani was no longer Florence Schmidt’s
instructor. It seems from a letter lately to hand from
Steffani that the lady is still faithful to her yrimo
maestro. Steffani says, by the way, that there are about
15,000 American women in Paris studying singing. Un-
fortunate audiences of the future !—J.A.O.

wonderful wet-night hat blocked my view of stage and I
was savage till a lady behind me (she too was blocked)
loudly described said hat as “ a triumphant combination
of rigid economy and reckless bad taste.” After that, I
felt Good!
Fitzgerald Bros’, new co. includes the Dunbar trio of
trapeze artists (from Barnum’s), the Jandaschevskys, a
new troupe of character dancers, clowns, acrobats and
pantomimists ; Rhodasis, a lady juggler, said to be a top-
notcher; and amongst others, our former favorite,
Adelina Antonia, of the fascinating smile and inimitable
pirouette, who has fallen from the roofs of most of the
great buildings of Europe since she last fell into a net in
Australia. The new co. is said to be a beauty show, as
some 12 new ladies will don tights under the monster
canvas. Tom Fitzgerald remains in Europe some time
longer.

A “PRINCESS” GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So disdainful an air—’twas in vain,
Such sweet scorn but suggested the stage—
Yet a face that again and again
Thrusts its beauty ’twixt me and the page—
So bewitchingly graceful and fresh,
Though Hell’s pangs I might fail to escape—
I could worship a god robed in Jlesh
Who assumed such an exquisite shape !
From those eloquent eyes—What flashed out
In an indolent glance as she passed i
Did they hint of the boisterous shout
Accorded the curtain up-cast V
Of a maddening smile darted fleet
At the stalls in high-collared array ?
Of a note per an usher discreet
Breathing love that would brook no delay ?-
Of a supper with laughter beset,
Where a dainty pink ear might be told
That a white wrist would gleam whiter yet
Were its ivory blazoned with gold ?
Of a whirl and an eddy of dust
As a dog-cart spins noisily past ?
Of a hans—here a northerly gust
Broke the spell that was over me cast.

The Snowy & Thredbo Rivers Gold Dredging Co.

DIRECTORS : 0. H. KERRY, Esq., 010 George-street, Sydney ; T. F. WALLER, Esq., Poynton, Glenmore-road,

C. PERKINS, Representative for N.S.W.).
DESCRIPTION OF PROPERTY.
The dredging area held by this company consists of 100 acres of river beds at the junction of the Snowy and
Thredbo Rivers, and is distant from Oooma about 40 miles, lying at the foot of the spurs flanking the eastern slopes
of the Snowy Mountains. The whole of this portion of the mountains is a well-known auriferous field, and gold may
be got in almost every small gully and stream. The principal drainage channel of the watershed is the Snowy River,
which receives in its course the waters of the Gungariine, Thredbo, and Eucumbene Rivers. The former, emptying
into the Snowy a few miles above this company’s lease, is we'.l-known to carry good gold, and parties of miners have
for the past 20 years been successfully working the alluvial deposits occurring on its banks.
The Thredbo has given its name to a very rich alluvial goldfield, the principal working centre of which was a few
miles above its junction with the Snowy. In importance the Thredbo field in the early days ranked second only to
the famous Kiandra.
The Eucumbene drains all the gold-bearing country intervening between its junction with the Snowy River and
the Kiandra diggings.
The Company’s area occupies an exceptional position at the head of the Jindabyne Valley, and is the first spot
along the main Snowy River where any important river drifts occur. Prospects and boring operations recently finished
prove the wash to be exceptionally rich, and the gold of a nature easily saved.
In addition to the 100 acres now applied for arrangements have been made on a satisfactory basis with the owners
of properties adjoining the river banks for the right to work a further area of about 60 acres, or so much of their land
as may be proved dreageable.
Reports by Mr. A. C. Perkins, representing the well-known dredging firm, Messrs. F. W. Payne and Co., of New
Zealand, embracing results of boring operations, together with report by Mr. W. S. M'Allister, Manager Bogong
Mines, may be seen at the Company’s office.
The lease of the land applied for on behalf of the Company has BEEN ISSUED BY THE MINES DEPARTMENT.
Tenders have been called for the construction of a suitable dredging plant, having a working capacity of from 80
to 100 cubic yards per hour.
EXTRACTS FROM MR. PERKINS’S REPORT.
Sydney, Bth March, 1900.
Since last reporting on your Claim on the Snowy River, under date 11th September, 1899, I have at your request
thoroughly prospected and tested the property by means of extensive boring operations, and am very pleased to in-
form you that the excellent opinion at first formed by me has been fully confirmed.
Holes Sunk.—Thirty holes have been put down by means of boring and sinking at various points along each side
of the river, and on some of the small islands in the centre of the stream.
Bottom.—The bottom is of soft decomposed granite, and will be very good to dredge on.
Prospects.—The best prospect obtained gave Idwt. l'CCGgr. from a hole 12ft. deep and 3in. diameter; a small
nugget weighing 23‘116gr. was obtained from this. The gold obtained from the whole of the dirt lifted from surface
down is equal to 15dwt. lgr. per cubic yard. The average, exclusive of the nugget above referred to, is 6dwt. 13'5gr.
per cubic yard.
These prospects refer exclusively to the gold obtained from the bores put down on the dredgeable portion of your
lease. They were fairly uniform with the exception of No. 19, from which the nugget was obtained, and are from
various points well apart, extending over a distance of a mile along the river. I consider them to be a representative
prospect as far as could be obtained by this method, and they indicate that the deposits are very rich.
Gold.—The sample of gold is very good, and of a class that is easily saved, and worth £4 per ounce. In my pre-
vious report I stated that the gold was chiefly fine. This referred to the prospect then obtained from surface, but the
boring operations have proved that the bottom wash carries a very rough sample of gold, one piece obtained being
just on a dwt.
In conclusion I have to congratulate you upon possessing what appears to be an excellent claim, the prospects of
which compare most favorably with the best N.Z. properties, which are now returning large dividends to their share-
holders, and I feel confident that yours will do the same.
Yours faithfully,
A. C. PERKINS,
Late Manager Unity and Earnscleugh G. D.,
Molyneaux, N.Z.

Melba has, for the benefit of her Australian admirers,
denied that she was born in Scotland. But she appa-
rently did not do so in Scotland, when feted by the pro-
vost and corporation of her alleged birthplace.
Said that young Rebottaro got a whack in the eye from
a drunken Melbourne Jingo, t’other Saturday, on account
of his un-English parentage, or because he refused to load
himself up with British beer at the patriot’s expense, or
for some equally “ Mafekinglike ” cause. A pretty story,
if true. Rebottaro, earlier in the day, had assisted to
make things festive by singing Jingo songs at an im-
promptu concert in front of Glen’s music-shop.
With the conversion of Melbourne Alexandra into Her
Majesty’s came an entente cordiale between the dress-circle
and the stage. At the cheerless Aleck it was as difficult
to see much of the action of a piece from a side seat in the
d.c. as to hear much of the dialogue from a centre seat in
the back row. At Her Majesty’s, the circle people can
hear every word that’s uttered and see things they would
rather leave unnoticed. They get the show thrown at
them, so to speak.
“ Joe Rocks ” : Nobody hitherto seems to have noticed
that when Brough stands or walks on the stage his soles
have an affinity for each other. Must be a mere man-
nerism, because in “ Tanqueray ” or other heavy dramas
the peculiarity is hut slightly noticeable, whereas in
comedies such as “Tyranny of Tears” it comes out
stronger, and in a farce of the “ Jane ” order (Lord Bap-
child) said affinity not only affects the feet, but extends
right up to the knees, and reminds one of the time-
honored gait of the pantomime clown. The device,
however, is effective, and goes to show that, in addition
to gesture, face-play, &c., an actor should study foot-
play.
The London Opera season opened on May 14th, with
“ Romeo and Juliette,” and Melba to the fore. Last week
(on Queen’s Birthday night), Lalla Miranda came out as
the prima-donna who falls victim to the wicked duke in
“ Itigoletto.” The cable reports a success of more or less
magnitude for the bright little Melbourne songstress, and
it is certain that her first appearance was looked forward
to as an event. The Daily Mail, in mentioning the engage-
ment of Lalla Miranda, said “whom the Brussels people
declare to have the most beautiful voice heard at La
Monnaie since Mme. Melba first appeared there.” Another
20 years and 50 per cent, of the world’s finest voices will
be Australian. Already, this is pretty well the hub of the
universe for comic opera, chorus singing—and we don’t
know it. Courtice Pounds, who had been a cathedral
chorister before he grew into a popular English tenor,
used to fairly snort at the country which could let the
Firm’s chorus go un-encored after some of its best efforts.
Charles Arnold must feel rather sick of the S’African
war. It cut short his Transvaal tour and sent the co. fly-
ing back to Capetown, where, with Australian business in
view, Arnold raised a £lOOO “ fruit fund” for the benefit
of the Contingents. And now that he is in Melbourne,
and the local Stock Exchange is spending money on bursts
of “ patriotic ” fury at the theatres, the roaring brokers
have forgotten to patriotnise Arnold’s show.
Melb. Age critic takes the Gilbertian humor quite seri-
ously. Talking of Dorothy Vane as the bumboat-womau
in “ Pinafore,” he complained that her hideous make-up
“ destroyed all the poetry of the character.” Evidently
he looks for lingering suggestions of sweet simplicity in
“ Little Buttercup,” although the author's satire is
broad enough. The lady for whom Gilbert wrote
Little Buttercup was chosen for her calm, prosaic
style. She had previously appeared (under Gilbert’s
direction) as the respectable pew-opener in “The Sor-
cerer,” where she folded her mittened hands together and
sang—“ I’m no saucy minx, and giddy—but a clean and
ti-i-dy widdy.”
Some of the casual jokes in “ What Happened to Jones ”
are fresh and human. For instance, the lunatic who
escapes from the asylum in a Red Indian make-up is
humorously sane on one point. When he strikes another
fool roaming about in a blanket he proposes that they
shall start a joint wigwam and furnish it with squaws.
“ Old squaw for you—young squaw for me,” says the
stray crank, but as the other fellow is merely bent on
finding his lost clothes the amorous one yells, “ Both
squaws for me,” and departs hurriedly, followed by
encouraging laughter from the charmers in front.
They had to provide a new screen in “ The School for
Scandal ” at Sydney Her Majesty's to hide the generous
form of Nance O’Neil; that which concealed the rather
emaciated Brown-Potter was far too small.
Olga Nethersole was introduced to the stage by Arthur
Dacre and Amy Roselle, whose violent deaths in Sydney
are still remembered. Writer remembers her as a nervous
little theatrical debutante in the English provinces. She
had been a governess, engaged first to look after the
Dacres’ only child, which died in infancy.
The Dancing G’Earl of Yarmouth was among the little
lot of inferior mummers who went to S’africa as Imperial
Yeomen. Two or three of the others are stage-struck
lords like himself, but the absurd Yarmouth is remarkable
for having made a lucky escape from the army before he
set out to play the goat in Australia. Five years ago he
held a lieutenancy in the Black Watch. Had he stuck to
his regiment his young Nibs might have danced to the
deadly ballet-music of Magersfontein.
Australia gave liberal latitude to Alice Leamar in days
of yore, so it ought to deal kindly with Marie Lloyd
should she come along on top of the “ wave of Imperial
sentiment.” Marie is a beloved English institution of the
red-light and blue type, with the accent on the blue.
Her favorite ballad, “ Everybody wondered how he knew,”
opens up a question as to why a casual friend of the family
regarded the dimple on baby’s knee as an interesting
proof of its maternity. Also one of Marie’s latest is an
announcement that her sister has got khaki twins.
Which ought to appeal to a good many thoughtful Aus-
tralian damsels just at present.

“Melb.”: The late Gilbert J. Smith was not identical
with Gilbert Probyn Smith, who is still quite alive.
Willie Nott, one of the very few organists who have
made a name for themselves in Melbourne, died of con-
sumption last week. With good health and good luck
Nott might have achieved brilliant success in his profes-
sion. A clever portrait of the tall, thin, round-shouldered
young man appeared on Alf. Vincent’s double-page of Mel-
bourne Block characters, published in Tub Bulletin last
December.
Said that Bernard Espinasse, or someone else, is slinging
together a bowdleriseil edition of “Sapho” for Williamson
in which Nance O’Neil is to take the part of the giddy
l’arisienne. Well, the most suggestive scene in “Sapho”
is where Gaussin, in kilts, carries Sapho upstairs to his
bedroom, and this writer wishes Kingston or whoever
takes the part (in kilts) joy of the contract of heaving the
colossal Nance up to the second floor. In fact, unless
some invisible machinery can be devised to hoist Nance,
that scene must be cut out.
Beerbohm Tree has produced, or is about to produce,
a somewhat new version of “ Rip Van Winkle” with him-
self in the name part. It will be more of a temperance
sermon than the old, original drama. Joe Jefferson’s Rip
was a joyous boozer who drew no attention to the fact
that the accursed drink had wrought his downfall, but
the new Dutchman will suffer a bad attack of blue-devils
when he gets to the haunted mountain, and a bottle imp
will appear before him in tangible shape.
Mr. and Mrs. Patrick Oampbell married when he was 19
and she was 17, and there are two children, boy and girl.
Oampbell was away from England seeking bis fortune
when his wife sought hers by going on the stage, at the
Ad&lphi in 1890, after shining as an amateur.
“ Cecil ” : lie “ P.’s in B. 5/5/’OO. Coming down river
in Borneo in the early 90’s we lay for the night alongside
a Malay trader’s dug-out going up, and were kept awake
for hours by an awful tune played on an awfuller accor-
deon. Next night the serenade was repeated by another
party on an execrable violin, and this continued for a
week, so that the air was fixed for ever in my mind.
Reaching headquarters and attending an “at home” at
Government House I heard the same air played by the
constabulary band, and learned that it was called “ Ta-
ra-ra-boom de ay ! ”
The most remarkable trial that has taken place in con-
nection with the dramatic profession in Australia was the
one in which Walter Montgomery was arraigned before
the Duke of Edinburgh for murdering Shakespeare. The
case was heard in camera in Sydney, counsel for and
against the prisoner being B. C. Aspinall (Victorian bar-
rister of renown) and W. B. Dailey. Those who witnessed
the forensic exhibition say that the display of witty elo-
quence—and that Dailey had rather the best of it—was
matchless.
Curious to remember that Edwin Booth, who after-
wards gained so much fame in America and England as a
tragedian, visited the Australian in ’54, and left no mark
whatever. He appeared in Victoria, being then a young
man of 22 or 23, as a stock actor in Laura Keane’s co.
Melbourne was suffering from a period of commercial
depression, and the co. quickly took its departure.
Dot Boucicault’s versatility as an actor was pretty well
known and appreciated, but not to its fullest extent.
During Fanny liobina’s Melb. “ Jack Sheppard ” season
Dot went on one night for Jack instead of the shapely
Fanny. His make-up and performance were so admirable
that no one in front guessed the change in the cast, and
even the Melb. dailies failed to make a par out of the
incident.
George Musgrove is said to have engaged a very fetching
operatic artist in Madame Caro Roma for his next Aus-
tralian campaign.
Dear Bulletin, —I am a man of genius, and have written
a play. As lam woefully weak in chronology and topo-
graphy, I was glad to see by your note to “ Scriptus ” par.
in Poverty Point of May 5, that it won’t matter a hang.
I was dreadfully in fear of those wretches, the critics,
who think of nothing but showing their own stupendous
learning. Now I shall no longer be troubled with my
besetting ailment—inability to sleep in the daytime. On
looking over my M.S. I find that I have inadvertently
made Bathurst the principal seaport of N.S.W., and repre-
sented the landing of Capt. Cook as being contem-
poraneous with the knighting of the rats’ friend, Mayor
Harris —but it is too much trouble to alter it. When my
audiences get into what critics call the “soulful flap-
doodle ” of the third act, they will neither have time nor
inclination to think of such trivialities. I always felt that
I was a genius : now my topographical and chronological
resemblance to Shakespeare proves it. On behalf of the
divine William and myself I return thanks for your very
sensible note.—J. Cock-Shore.
Charles Mathews used to say that the worst set-back he
ever got was when playing in a favorite farce. In the
midst of the most laughter-moving scene, a languid swell
rose in the stalls and began leisurely to put on his over-
coat. “ You need not go yet, sir,” said the somewhat-
nettled comedian—" there’s more to come.” “ Ya-a-s,”
drawled the exquisite, “ that’s why I’m going.” And out
he sauntered without moving a muscle of his face.
The late “ Billy ” Hoskins and the late J. J. Kennedy
were once playing the twin Dromios in- the “ Comedy of
Errors,” in M.L. Hoskins noticed Kennedy lining his face
very heavily, and said to him : “ Here, what are you up
to ; I ain’t so wrinkled as all that? ” .Wrinkles?” said
Kenned^ - : “ No, guv’nor, these are dimples.”
A London paper takes the trouble to state that
Manager Benson is not the son of the demised Archbishop
of Canterbury. Otherwise there might be the more
solemn horror over the Caliban he lately perpetrated in
Shakespeare’s “ Tempest ”at the Lyceum. Tell it not in
Gath, or to Geo. Rignold, but Benson’s acrobatic Caliban
turns somersaults, climbs a tree, and hangs from a
branch ’lsshis toes.

Ex-Judge Forbes, formerly of Vic. County
Court, who died the other day, in the neighbor-
hood of the nineties, was an impressive figure when
on the bench, SO or 40 years ago. Forbes, even on
circuit in the rough old digging days, used always
to wear an elaborate Windsor Court suit, but he
was not so punctilious in certain other respects.
He retired when some of the present-day jurists
worn playing marbles.
A medico who does the post-mortems at a
Vic. lunatic-asylum was engaged on two defuncts
one afternoon, and had left the key of the morgue
in the door on the outside. The job took a long
time, and as it was getting dark one of the
“tame” patients, supposing that the doctor had
left, locked the door and took the key away. The
doc. hearing the key turned, called out,‘but the
lunatio, being deaf, heard him not. There was
but one chance of being liberated that night. At
about 6.30 the engine-driver of the pumping-house
on the creek would pass by on his way home, and
his attention might be attracted. The doctor got
up to the small window near the roof, watched
till the E. -D. came along, and then made all the
noise he could, but in vain—he was apparently
not heard, and seasoned as he was he didn’t relish
the prospect of putting in a frosty night in com-
pany with two corpses. But presently he be-
thought him of the instrument—a sort of chisel—
which he used for burglariously breaking into the
skulls of his subjects, and with this he succeeded,
after much trouble, in picking the lock and releas-
ing himself from his gruesome company.

WEAK, NERVOUS, AND IN A VERY LOW STATE,
Through a Great Deal of Suffering.
EAR, TOOTH .A-ZtsTID
An Unstrung Nervous System Thoroughly Renovated.
The Case of
MR. WILLIAM HARRISON.
(From the '■* Newtown Independent”)
Maria Street lies in a thickly populated
part of Newtown, and it was here that our
reporter sought for No 138, the residence of
Mr Hairison. The house is readily found,
being the nicest and most attractive in the
street. Mr Harrison, who had just returned
from work, greeted the reporter with a
cheery welcome.
“I wish to ask you, Mr. Harrison,”
inquired our reporter, “ the truth of the
statement that you have been through a
great deal of suffering, and finally found a
sure and perfect relief
“ Yes ; that is quite true, and I am never
likely to forget the time f had. I will tell
you how it started I had had a long spell
of very heavy work, for I may tell you I am
in the building trade, and at best my occu-
pation is very laborious. I got weak,
nervous, and in a very low state of health.
I believe my system was thoroughly run
down, and for nine weeks I had a terrible
time. ”
“ What were your symptoms, Mr Harri-
son ? ”
•‘My great trouble was neuralgia. The
pains would come up my spine and settle in
my head and face, mostly on one side of the
face, giving me the earache and excruciating
pains in the jaws At times the neuralgia
would leave one side of the face and settle
in the other. The pains came on every
night time with distressing regularity, and
lasted till about three o’clock in the morn-
ing. It was a perfect misery to go to bed,
and I was forced to get up and walk the
floor for hours. Sometimes, to get a little
relief, I held brandy in my mouth to deaden
the pain. I became a martyr to both tooth
and faceache. The pains spread all round
my gums, and to this was joined headache
and frequent giddiness. After some time
my legs became affected, and trembled so
I dreaded they would not carry me and that
I should fall down. This convinced me
that my nervous system was thoroughly
unstrung. I would give sudden starts
in my sleep, and wake up with a jerk
for no reason that I could tell. After eating
there was a feeling of wind gathered across
my chest, and it hurt me to stoop or bend.
My back pained me all down the spine, and
I had to have it rubbed to get a little ease ;
and I got that way at last I felt pains from
head to foot; particularly, I noticed that
my distressing symptoms were aggravated
by the hot weather.”
“ Did you leave off work, Mr. Harrison ? ”
inquired the reporter.
“No ; bad as I was, I stuck to my job,
and kept battling through. In fact, I was
that way that work, as long as I could
struggle with it, was the only relief I had
“ Did you seek medical advice ?”
“ Yes ; I went to a doctor, and he gave
me some powders, but they did me no good
whatever "
“ Yeb you got cured, I believe?”
“ Yes ; Clements Tonic cured me ; there
is no mistake about that. I noticed a
decided improvement after the third or
fourth dose, and after I had continued taking
Clements Tonic I got steadily better ”
“ What induced you to take Clements
Tonic ? ”
“ We were living in Hugo Street at that
time, and I remember my wife picked up a
little book of cures effected, and she read
what Clements Tonic had done for
other sufferers And as some of these persons
mentioned were local people that we knew
of, my wife gained confidence that what was
said was true. And it was my wife per-
suaded metogetClementsTonic and take it ”
“ Was your recovery complete? "
“ I got all right again, and as sound as the
bank ; all my troubles went away. The
trembling in the limbs, the nervousness,
headache, and, what relieved me the most of
all, the terrible neuralgic pains and toothache,
disappeared, and I was able to getmy night’s
rest again Remember, I lost my proper rest
for nine weeks, always having to get up
early the next morning, sleep or no sleep,and
I am certain that had my troubles not been
stopped in time by Clements Tonic I should
have got into a deplorable, helpless con-
dition.”
“Will you allow me to make your re-
covery public?”
“ b»y all means. You are quite welcome
to publish what I have said about- my sick-
ness, and what Clements Tonic lias done for
me. It was by reading another man's ease
that I was fortunately induced to try
Clements Tonic, and I shall be glad if what
I say may benefit someone else ”

An old bachelor scribe tells The Bulletin
about Happy Homes :
Wherever you see a Happy Home, the owner has a share
in one of the swindles of the World. Oh, the embezzlers
do make such lovely homes ! What nice wives they have
The typical Mrs. Embezzler is a fairy creature, with straw
hair. Her diamond rings are many-convenient to bolt
with. Who can play the piano hl*e her? Of course, , 1
don’t include the home of the rich author, of whom the
publishers say, “ That’s where our money goes to. Ue
mostly quarrels with his wife. The pensioner has one of
the sweetest of Ker-ristian homes. Over the mantelpiece
is, “Cast all your care upon the Government, because it
carethfor you.” The sweater has abeautiful home, wife and
children. He wouldn’t sweat but for them. He is not a
beast by nature. There is no inherent charm in sweating.
And the usurer! He also is a delightful, gentle, loving
house-father—never even lets his family see him sharpen
his teeth before he goes out to prey like a wolf from a
Thuringian forest. I went (when indebted to my host at
80 per cent, per an.) to the house of such a one I dared
not refuse the invitation. The most luxurious chair I
ever sat in, the choicest and biggest of Havanas, warm
and velvety Bordeaux (out of a magnum), and—l am now
58 and place these things last—the brightest eves and
kindest that ever shone even among the beautiful Women
of Israel. And it was all the expressed and coined juices
of men who conformed not to the Mosaic dietary . While
I smoked that ever-memorable Manuel Garcia Alonso, you
could hear the compound-interest mounting up all the
time ; the heavy silk curtains were red. with the blood Of
the Christian, and the lamps burned bright with the tat
of the Gentile.
“ Anti-Alien ” writes to The Bulletin :
“ The plague has been insidiously advancing
amongst the Sydney Chinese, and the Board of
Health is awakening to the terrible consequences
of a further spread of the malady through the
noisome dens of the Mongolian. If Sydney has
i to suffer an epidemic attack of plague this is the
. source of the greatest danger. Eight cases, seven
! fatal, have already been reported, and with their
i native secretiveness severalmighthave been hidden
I by the callous Chow in the interests of his vege-
table trade. The scouring-out of Sussex and Kent
Streets is nothing to what is. needed in the msam-
i tary uncivilised habitations of almost all the
i suburban vegetable-hawkers. [ln fairness to the
Chinese, it should be stated that overcrowding
I rather than dirt is the main provable charge
against them in this connection. Ihe average
number of plague cases amongst the Chinese is
not greater than amongst any other nationality,
but the death-rate is much higher.— Ed. B.J
According to Sydney D.T.
But in that case how is it that the horses and
cows that ate the infected fodder haven’t suffered
anything therefrom, while the humans who didn t
eat it, or come into particularly close contact with
it, got plague. But President Thompson has his
hypothesis ready—
“ Dr Thompson’s attention was drawn to the fact that
a considerable proportion of the casM of plague had oc-
curred anion o, people who either had stables attached to
their premises, or whose occupations brought them into
connection with stables ; and it was suggested that this
fact seemed to point to the wide disseminations of infected
fodder.”
But how could the rats infect anything outside
the packages of produce in. which they were en-
closed without first infecting the produce itself ?
And if Dr. Thompson has no evidence that infec-
tion is carried by merchandise, what evidence has
he that it is carried by anything —or even that the
alleged plague is infectious ?
“ Some of these rats may have been conveyed in produce
to the premises of purchasers, and in that way the disease
could have spread. But it is not probable that the pro-
duce itself could have been infected, apart from the rats
which might have been concealed in it. We have no
evidence that infection is carried by merchandise ; on the
other hand there is a great deal of evidence that it is not.

AN.S.W, medico is amusing himself by
trying to grow a crop of beans from one which he
cut out of a child’s bronchial tube. It was the
same ingenious practitioner who chloroformed a
billy-goat and saved a man’s leg by introducing
two inches of goat bone where it was most needed.

The Acetylene Gas Company of Austra-
lasia, Ltd., is to be congratulated on its success
at the Royal Agricultural Show, having secured no
less than twenty-five first prizes for its exhibits,
consisting of automatic, non-automatic and port-
able generators, also buggy, miner’s, stevedore’s,
firemen’s, bicycle, table, hand and
other lamps for acetylene gas. Ordinary carriage-
lamps converted into acetylene lamps; correspond-
ence invited on all outdoor and indoor lighting.*
Re recent death from plague of “Joe”
Redman, managing-clerk for Sydney solicitor
Harry Levien. Said that on the previous Sunday
he was at Sans Souci, and, by way of joke, told
the publican that there was a case of plague at
neighboring pub., Sandringham ; then went to
latter place and told the same story there of the
house he had just left. Following Saturday
morning Redman was in a city hotel and com-
plained of feeling queer, joked a bit about the
trouble being plague, was sent home in a cab and
died that night. Doctors diagnosed his trouble as
true bubonic.
Thus ingenuously a N.S.W. warrior from
the front:—
One Boer commander (General Grobler) refused point
blank to give up his nfle, but when he saw we were about
to use violence, he handed the weapon over to our officer
quietly. Mrs. Grobler reminded one of our boys, in a
loud tone, that the bricks he was standing bn had
recently been whitewashed ; and impressed upon another
that she did not cultivate rose-bushes for him to lean his
rifle against. Miss Grobler told us we couldn’t have much
brains when we came so far to fight. Some of the farmers
treated us very kindly, and I felt sorry for the lady mem-
bers of the Dutch families. Their dress and expression
showed that they had lost someone who was near and
dear to them. They spoke to us in such a gentle and
broken-hearted way, with such a pretty Dutch accent,
that we could not help hut pity them.
After the Highlanders were beaten in their
Jacobite struggle, 1745-46, thus spoke Judge Lord
Hardwicke:—
The judgment of the law is, and this high court doth
award, that you, William, Earl of Kilmarnock ; George,
Earl of Cromarty ; and Arthur Lord Balmerino, and every
one of you, return to the prison of the Tower from whence
you came : from thence you must be drawn to the place
of execution you come there you must be hanged
hy the neck, but not till you are dead ; for you must be
cut down alive ; then your bowels must be taken out and
burnt before your faces ; then your heads must be severed
from your bodies ; and your bodies must be divided each
into four quarters ; and these must be at the king’s dis-
posal. And God Almighty be merciful to your souls.
Truly a delightful anti-climax.

New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.,
Limited, report on Produce market for week end-
ing 26th. of May 1900, that Hides were not so
eagerly sought for and had slumped by about £d.
per lb.; “sloppy” and badly cared for lots and
shipping section slumped most. On Friday, Sheep-
skins of the cross-bred variety seemed inclined to
soar, while all sound-pelted descriptions cheerfully
maintained their end of the stick. Unfortunately
Tallow still continues to slip down. In sympathy
with a London decline of from 5/- to 10/- per ton
the Sydney price slid down by fully half-a-
sovereign, with indications of another fall at an
early date. Marsupial Skins were off last week
but this week (Wednesday) will show a good
catalogue. Wheat is suffering from the effects of
Mafeking, in spite of which the Co. succeeded in
placing a fair quantity at good prices, to wit,
prime samples 2/8 to 2/B£, good 2/7 to 2/7 J, pinched
2/4 to 2/6, chickwheat 2/3 to 2/5 per bushel.
London reports big damage to crops in France
and America, but no change of values in Mark
Lane. Local millers only eager for samples to
blend with their stocks.
Unique In Memoriam, from Newcastle
(N.S.W.) Herald
O’DOOLEY. —In fond and loving remembrance of Sarah
Ann, the beloved wife of Thomas O’Dooley, who died
at Cow Flat, April 10, 1894 ; aged, 31 years. Also,
in fond and loving remembrance of Nora Alice, the
beloved wife of Thomas O’Dooley, who died at Cow
Flat, September 10, 1899 ; aged, 34 years.
The end lies ever in my thought,
To see two graves so close and deep ;
Mothers, young and beautiful, there,
Was brought that rocked their child to sleep.
Sleep on, dear wives, and take your rest,
’Tis hard to break the tender cord when love has
bound the heart;
’Tis hard, so hard to speak the words,
That we had to part.
Three locks, and they are wondrous fair
Left me the vision mild ;
The brown is from my wives’ dear hair,
And the bloom is from my child.
Weep not for us, our children dear,
We are not dead, but sleeping here ;
With patience we have run our race,
Kind death has set us free, we have gone to a better
place.
This world was not for us ; pray for us, that we may
be made worthy of the promises of Christ.
Inserted by their loving husband and children,
Thomas J. O’Dooley, Cow Flat, N.S.W.

Ihe practical cave-in of some at least of
the Federal delegates over Chamberlain’s pro-
posed alterations in the Commonwealth Bill sug-
gests that they are being politically poisoned, as
almost every Australian politician is when he is
dined and honored in .London. Their obvious
duty was to say: “Our instructions are to get
the whole Bill, but if you absolutely insist on
making alterations in it you can make them on
your own responsibility. We acquiesce because
we can t help it, but we don’t approve.” Instead
of this, the cable represents Alfred Deakin as
going mad (after a large dinner) with admiration
because Chamberlain proposes to tinker with the
peoples Bill, and assuring him that Australia is
quite agreeable. Who gave Deakin the right to
assure anybody after a large dinner that Australia
withdraws in any particular from the decision it
recorded at the Referendum ’
