The Incidental Mrs. Bracken-
ridge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was the calmest little woman in the worl
so demure, so quiet, that she was like a sma
sleek, brown mouse. One seldom heard her voice
—one never by any chance heard of her originat-
ing a conversation or doing anything definite ; and
her two little girls were as unobtrusive. So no
wonder big Dick Brackenridge sometimes forgot
their very existence and permitted himself the
freedom of a single man.
Dick came down to Sydney for the sheep-sales,
and took back a governess for the pruning and
cultivation of his two olive-branches. Mrs.
Brackenridge and the children were away on a visit
to her father’s station, and Miss Milby was settled
at Bundaroona before she returned. She meekly
acquiesced in her husband’s choice of an instruc-
tress, and if she noticed _ anything suspicious she
never betrayed her consciousness of it by even the
lifting of her eyelashes. '
She remained as dutifully in the background as
before—only, in a few weeks, Miss Milby began
to have a hunted look in her eyes, and to glance
quickly and apprehensively at Mrs. Brackenridge
when there seemed no reason. But Dick sus-
pected nothing, and his first intimation came with
a shock as he lounged on the verandah one night
after dinner.
Through the open window near him came two
voices—his wife’s and Miss Milby’s. Mrs. Brack-
enridge was speaking in her habitually low tone, so
that he needed to strain his ears to catch what she
said. .
“ You had better pack up your things to-night,
Miss Milby. Peter will bring the buggy round to
your side of the house at 5.30 to-morrow morning.”
Dick sat bolt upright.
“I should like to—to see Mr. Brackenridge
about it first,” said Miss Milby in a very hesi-
tating voice.
“Oh, there is no necessity.” Mrs. Bracken-
rid go’s voice was wonderfully suave. “ I will ex-
plain your sudden departure.”
The other plucked up a little spirit. “Sup-
pose I refuse to go ? ” she asked.
Mrs. Brackenridge laughed the smallost of
laughs. Dick had oraned forward and could
see Miss Milby standing up flushed, defiant, and
sullen, yet cowed and timid withal. Mrs. Bracken-
ridge, seated opposite, was as tranquil as summer
moonlight. Her hands were lying in her lap, her
whole demeanour serene and unruffled.
“Suppose you refuse to go,” she repeated
sweetly, “then my course is decided. Your
father is a clergyman, I believe ? ”
“Yes.”
“ Good !—he will the more readily believe any
evil about you. Of course I shall explain to my
husband that, finding you here in the position of
his mistress—pray don’t stamp your foot, Miss
Milby ; what an exhibition of temper ! —I shall
have to take my children to my father’s station,
and then communicate with your people. My
uncle holds a mortgage over this property. He
can—and will —foreclose immediately. Then my
husband will be ruined —and you, too ! ”
She never raised her voice*from its usual mono-
tone, and for a moment after she spoke there was
absolute silence. Then Miss Milby broke out —
“You are a fiend!—an absolute fiend! Ever
since you came home you’ve made my life a hell —
torturing me; letting me see you knew ; playing
with me like a cat with a mouse ! No one else in
the world could do it like you.”
Mrs. Brackenridge shook her head and smiled
pleasantly. “Oh, come—you misjudge me,” she
said. “lam a most harmless, inoffensive mortal
—in the township I believe they call me the
Woman of No Importance—but I know enough
to keep my husband to myself at any cost. If
these means fail, I think I can work up a very
neat charge of attempted murder.”
Miss Milby started back—genuine surprise and
horror in her face.
“ Murder ?’ she gasped.
“You brought my cup of tea into my bedroom
this morning—l have kept that tea for analysis;
and you got oxalic aoid from the store last week,
Miss Milby ! You can’t deny that!”
“It was to clean a sailor-hat,” gasped the girl.
“ You wicked woman ! —oh, you wicked woman ! ”
“ You will say so,” said Mrs. Brackenridge, plea-
santly, “but that—combined with wliat the tea will
disclose, and your motive for getting rid of me—
will be rather difficult to explain away. And now
I hardly think you’ll question the advisability of
leaving to-morrow morning— before Mr. Bracken-
ridyeisup.” . .
Brackenridge moved noiselessly away, and wiped
the cold perspiration from his forehead. When
bis wife presently brought him his. whisky-and-
soda, as usual, he took it, and drank it meekly
(after tasting it for oxalic acid). Then he stared
m a bewildered fashion after her as she went in.
“ Good God ! ” he muttered, “ to think I’ve lived
all these years with that woman without knowmg
that she, too, had a devil in her ! ” _
NELLIE M. DIELD.
A Mistake.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They had been engaged for two years— Bella and
the gloomy-looking overseer. They met when
Belle went up as a governess to Wirre station, and
she made short work of him. He was a most
passionate lover, just suited Belle, made her year
of bush life pass quite tolerably.
But the last young squatterlet went off to school,
and Belle had to go. Gray cut up badly about it,
for life on the run without her would be simply
awful for him. He was so solemn about it that
the girl felt uncomfortable. ‘ ‘ If you were to play
me false,” he told her, “ I believe that I could find
it in my heart to follow you and shoot you down ! ”
Belle, though she laughed, didn’t forget the
look on his face.
• * * * .
Belle had just locked the door of her little two-
roomed house, and settled herself for an evening’s
read. Music-teaching in a bush township doesn t
pay too well, and she “batched’ for economy s
iake. Besides, batching has its advantages.
A knock at the door made her jump. It wasn t
Charlie’s knock. He ; always came late.
“Who is it? An old friend. Open the
d Gray’s voice'. She staggered
against the wall. It was but a month since she
had given him his conge. Then he was at the
Gulf but even from that distance his bitter
answer had frightened her. And now—
Her face was white as chalk when she opened
the door, and she shrank visibly as he entered.
He did not even look at her, said nothing, out sat
down and scared at the fire, Belle watclnng him
furtively.
Suddenly he put his right hand in his coat
pocket, and looked at her with a strange, hideous
smile. Her flesh crept.
“I say, Belle,” he said slowly, “ I came a long
way to see you, didn’t I ? ”
Still that horrid smile. She remembered all
sorts of terrible things that she had read in the
papers. She could see him fumbling with some-
thing in his pocket.
“Yes,” she said, nervously— she could scarcely
restrain her desire to scream. She sprang from
her chair and hurried to the door, backwards.
She could bear it no longer. “ I think the fire’s
going out. Wait awhile, and I’ll get some wood,
she stuttered.
To run over the way and call the Smiths took
but a minute. “You see,” she explained, shiver-
ing, “ I was to marry him once, and he vowed he
would shoot me if I gave him up; and I’m sure
he’s come to do it. He looks insane.”
“By G —, we’ll see about that! ” Bull-necked
Smith got down his gun. “Two at the game’s
better.”
• * * *
Belle came back, presently, pale but smiling,
with two pieces of wood. Gray was sitting in the
same place, only with his back to her. He turned
round with a grin. “ Wood must be scarce in
these parts,” he said. “And now to business!”
He jumped up, still with that hand moving rest-
lessly.
The girl shuddered as he came and stood over
her.
“Well, Belle; and so you’re going to marry
another fellow, after all. That is, if I’ll let you.,
He wouldn’t like to hear how many times you’ve
hung round my neck and sworn you loved dear
Bob, nor the other little games we used to play,
eh ! You never told him, I’ll go bail. But it
would hardly do. ”
She winced. The door was open, and Smith
had such a long tongue.
“ Oh, don’t she wrung her hands.
“ Don’t be afraid. I’m not going to telltales,
and just don’t you split on me. You see, I’m in
the same boat. There’s a pretty little girl at
Wirre going to marry me soon, and she’s jealous ;
so it wouldn’t do for her to find out. I’ve brought
you your letters, and I want mine back, too. ’ He
brought out a packet from his pocket.
Belle nearly choked with rage and fear, but she
gave them to him. A whistle sounded. Gray put
on his hat. “My train goes in five minutes. Ta-
ta, sweetheart. Kiss for old times? No, thanks.
By the way, why did you get those two fools to
stand outside and listen ? Ha, ha!”

GRIZZLER.
The Flesh-Burden.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Crowds thrilled me! The rustling, hurrying
feet, the turbulent drone of voices, roused me to
wild dreams of power and glory, or soothed me
like gentle rain when swooning to sleep. I was a
Roman Centurion thundering at the enemy’s
gates—a Paris dallying with Helen while Troy is
lost, with the human sea around ?
My life was mechanical, but for this phantasy.
My work, the fellows with their fool jokes, thp
girls exacting monkey-tricks from their lovers, the
pious old couple I live with, who, though filled
with a dull hatred of each other, eat and sleep
together in deference to the bugbear decency, were
a tedious color-scheme that the crowd—my solace,
recreation, theatre —splashed out. Ihe joys and
sorrows of the crowd itself were nothing to me.
In a tempest of cheers and groans I scoffed at
their paltry excitement. The tumultuous, swaying
throng enthralled me by galvanizing my sensations
and emotions into life. .
I was musing on the fringe of a crowd one night
when some girls halted near me. The mob surged
and threw one girl into my arms. She laughingly
released herself and we stood together. Her face
attracted' me. It was kind and worn like one
tired of the deadly waiting for a laggard lover.
In some strange way I guessed all the genteel
subterfuges of her life; the yearning, reckless
wish to be loved; the hesitating retreat from
doubtful men; the hateful return to the old
genteel waiting. Here at last, I felt, was my
dissatisfied, longing, passionate complement. We
were so close that my breath stirred the curls on
her neck. I knew, by her tremulous gaiety to her
companions, that she was responsive to my sudden
tenderness. At last I touched her foot boldly—
my hand caressed and tightened on her arm, when
the crowd swept us apart. I let her go quietly.
Something told me that Pate had fore-ordained
this meeting, and a future one. I went home
elated. I was convinced that we would meet
again. For the time I had something tangible to
dream about, and I haunted crowds with a new
zest. My savage dreams were lost in tender
reveries. The time dragged often, but everi
gathering quickened my hopes.
Lost in vague memories, a year later, I wa,
hustled and displaced in a crowd watching a torch-
light procession. I found myself wedged next to
a group of laughing girls. _ “What a crush ! the
one thrown against me said apologetically, and I
turned startled, to see startled recognition in her
glance also. Fate had thrown the same girl in my
arms again ! The crowd threw me behind her. A
big man crushed where I had been. She looked at
him with disappointed resentment. I slipped my
arms firmly round her. She tried to loosen one
arm timidly, but I gently put her hand away.
The crowd rocked us in a celestial cradle. O the
witchery of that crowd ! Pale, dark faces, glowing
into radiant life, and paling to sad cameos in the
torch glare, soft bodies swaying together in a tense
lull now, and now a mad surging. God ! I could
have died then. My rapture was brief. Her
companions were cursed with the idea of inter-
cepting the procession. My love demurred. They
retired I had perforce to loosen my areas and re-
lease her. I, so often Alexander marrying myself
and my legions to a race of captive women by a
mere hand-signal, was desolated by a few foolish
eirls' I laughed aloud. The people near me
edged away. All I could do was swear solemnly
to second Fate’s suggestion the next time—if there
were forty girls with her I would drag her away
and detain her. I went home and dreamt of her
encircled by a ring of intoxication.
I haunted crowds—now, indeed, with a set pur-
pose. I lived for them—they were mv comfort
and calendar. Her'face and form were ahvavs be-
fore me. I followed any woman with a chance
resemblance to her. Again and again m-crowds I
looked in her eyes and read my welcome there.
Yet I never saw her, and I grew savage and
hatchet-faced. Crowds wearied me at last.
To-night I passed a man and woman in the
street. Weakness suddenly numbing me told me
it was she ! They turned and I drew back to the
dark. A wedding ring blighted, the hand by her
side. He looked a cad in a good government
billet.. The respectable niche he had fallen into
restrained his weariness, but he eyed passing
women stealthily already. The worn look was
still on her face, but she smiled continually. She
knew she must be gay to please this man.
I stumbled along cursing. All good things
ended then for me. The crowd filled me with
nausea. What is there left ?
M.E.L.
Unlimited demand for Australian rabbits
in England just now—one Sydney firm has in hand
an order for 240,000 head. The present figure
paid to trappers is Bs. per dozen pairs—not a bad
price—though there is something whimsical in the
contemplation that rats just now are costing 12s.
per dozen pairs in Sydney.
Sydney D.T.’s London correspondent for-
wards some passages of Maud Gonne’s article
which led to the recent suppression of Dublin
United Irishman by the British Govt. :
In truth, for Victoria, in the decreptitude of her 81
years, to have decided, after her absence of half a century
to re-visit the country she hates, and whose inhabitants
are the victims of the criminal policy of her reign—the
survivors of 60 years of organised famine—the political
necessity must have, been terribly strong. For, after all,
she is a woman, and however vile and selfish and pitiless
her soul may be, she must sometimes tremble as death
approaches when she thinks of the countless Irish mothers
who, sheltering under the cloudy sky, watching their
starving little ones, have cursed her before they died.
Every eviction during 63 years has been carried out in
Victoria’s name, and if there is a justice in Heaven, the
shame of those poor Irish emigrant girls, whose very inno-
cence renders them an easy prey, and who have been
overcome in the terrible struggle for existence on a
foreign shore, will fall on this woman, whose bourgeois
virtue is so boasted, and in whose name their homes were
destroyed. Taking the shamrock in her withered hand,
she dares to ask Ireland for soldiers to protect the exter-
minators of their race Queen, return to your
own land ; you will find no more Irishmen ready to wear
the red shame of your livery ”

N.S.W. railway Commissioner Fehon telF
Newcastle Jockey Club that he can’t reduce thfc
cost of carrying horses from Sydney to Newcastle,
but adds, “ If three horses consigned to one person
be conveyed in one box no enquiry will be made
as to whether the animals are all the actual
property of the consignee or not.” The reduction
for these is reminiscent of N.S.W. State Schools
Act, where three from one family get in cheap.
And an analogy also exists, “What’ll I do,” wrote
a pedagogue to his department, “in the case of
the O’Farrells? there’s five of them. But I’ve
good reason to know they’re not all one O’Farrell’s;
they throw them together to get in cheap under
the Act.” And the Department replied : “If
they enter under the name of one O’Farrell no en-
quiry need be made as to whether they are all
actually the children of that O’Farrell.” .

There is a big split in the ranks of the senior war corre-
spondents of the big London papers. The juniors got up
a dinner the other night at the club, and asked Lord
Roberts and Rudyard Kipling and others to dinner with
representative war correspondents. The big guns, Ben-
nett Burleigh and co., knew nothing of this, and now
there is war to the knife, and some of the juniors are
likely to be sent back by their seniors. There is no end
of a commotion, and now rules and regulations are being
drafted by a committee of war correspondents for the
future guidance of war correspondents generally.

The Pearl of Them All.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
G-aily in front of the stockwhip
The horses are galloping home,
Leaping and bucking and playing
With sides all a lather of foam.
But painfully, slowly, behind them,
With head to the crack of the fall,
And trying so gamely to follow,
Comes limping the pearl of them all.
He is stumbling, and stiff in the shoulder,
And splints from the hoof to the knee,
But never a horse on the station
Has half such a spirit as he;
Give these all the boast of their breeding,
These pets of the paddock and stall,
But ten years ago not their proudest
Could live with the pearl of them all.
No journey has ever yet beat him,
No day was too heavy or hard,
He was king of the camp and the muster
And pride of the wings of the yard;
But Time is relentless to follow,
The best of us bow to his thrall,
And Death with his scythe on his shoulder
Is dogging the pearl of them all.
I watch him go whinnying past me,
And memories come with a whirl
Of reckless wild rides with a comrade
And laughing gay rides with a girl;
How she decked him in lilies and love-knots,
And plaited his mane at my side,
And once in the grief of a parting
She threw her arms round him and cried.
And I promised—l gave her my promise
The night that we parted in tears,
To keep and be kind to the old horse
Till Time made a burden of years ;
And then, for his sake and one woman’s . . .
So fetch me my gun from the wall !
I have only this kindness to offer
As gift to the pearl of them all.
Here—hold him out here by the yard-wing,
And don’t let him know by a sign !
Turn his head to you,, ever so little,
I can’t bear his eyes to meet mine !
There—stand still, old boy, for a moment . . .
These tears! how they blind as they fall . .
Now, God help my hand —to—be —steady !
Good-bye—to the pearl of them all !
WILL H. OGILVIE.
The London Crimean monument has the
word “Crimea” in big letters. Passing it with a
friend, Bright said, “The ‘A’ is at the wrong
end.”
Driven to It.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was lying on a dishevelled bed. There was a
box against the wall with her name—Stella
Lorreyne—cut off a day-bill, and a portmanteau
with Other initials under the bed. A pair of worn
silk tights hung over the washstand for a towel,
and a kerosene bucket in the fireplace had been
used to make tea in.
Her features were pretty, but troubled; and
trouble was evidently a novelty to her.
A man—Poverty Point incarnate —entered the
room without knocking. “ The profession is dead,
and I am almost,” he said, flinging himself on his
side of the bed.
She rose with petulant tragedy.
“ Wuat are we to do ?’’ Her voice had few dis-
cords in it, as though little used to complaints.
“ I’ll go mad if I have to stay here any longer.
You, at least, are walking about seeing people,
the shops, and things.”
“ You can go out too.”
“ You know I haven’t a dress fit for G-eorge-
street. No, I must stay here, without a cup of
tea, while the odour of the lodgers’ dinner comes
up from the kitchen. Ted, you must get some-
thing——
“ How ? ” came sleepily.
“I don’t know—that’s your business—you are
the man. I’ve always had somebody to do that
for me.”
“ You’d better get someone now; I can’t.”
She looked reproaches. Her love was of the
patient, all-enduring variety, while his was merely
acceptance and toleration. He had been stranded
before this, and with tho easy optimism of his
class, felt assured of something turning up! Just
now he was tired and ill-tempered. After a day’s
hard idling round street corners, a home-coming to
reproaohes was not fair.
“Ted, isn’t there something you can do—l
mean outside the business. Aren’t there unem-
ployed offices where you-get work?”
“In Heaven’s name, woman, why don’t you
try to get something yourself ? Your face and
figure need never starve. The world is full of flats,
if you’re a pretty woman. Let me sleep, will
you ! I’m not hungry when I sleep, and I’ve got
no tobacco.”
He slept, and she came down the stairs, past the
door where the boarders were playing nap. They
whispered about her as she passed out. A young
man was standing on the verandah. He wished
her good-night. She looked at his prosperous
waton-chain, and thought how long it would take
her to make him give her a few pounds. But he
did not follow up nis civility, and she passed into
the street, her vanity dead within her.
It was early evening. She stood in the gaily-lit
streets, starving, ill-clad, and jostled by pleasure-
saekers. She was not ordinarily hungry. She
was beyond that, only miserable, weak, and con-
sciously growing weaker.
A man was whispering to a girl with a rouged
face. Stella heard—and remembered Ted’s last
remark. She knew she would be just as pretty as
that girl had she her rouge and clean print.
Suddenly she remembered a man who once said
he loved her. He was a chemist; his shop was
some way off, but it would be open.
“ Why didn’t you ask your Ted ? ” The chemist
had thrilled when his “adorable bundle of rags
entered the shop, but hardened at her request for
money, as she treated him too impersonally.
“ Oh, Ted !—l’ve left him this six months. You
see we—that is—the company are stranded ——”
She staggered and fainted. When she came to,
the taste of brandy was in her mouth and soft
words in her ear. He repeated his old offer. Two
pictures rose before her. One sordid and miser-
able, painted with despair, the garret, Ted, the
long scramble for the husks ; the other a suburban
cottage, a garden radiant with ease and elemen-
tary luxury.
But she could not love the chemist. She hated
herself because she allowed her love for Ted to
influence her, and hated the chemist because he
could not make her love him. Therefore she was
right in continuing to deceive him. He urged his
request, adding a piano to the visionary cottage.
She assented.
“ But not to-night—l must get my clothes.”
“ I’ll buy you new ones.”
He caught her in his arms. She struggled when
a knock came from the shop. It was a customer
who thus saved her. She passed out —making an
appointment for next day—with two sovereigns
in her hand, to buy clothes with.
* * * *
Ted stumbled up-stairs thirsty and footsore.
He at first thought it was a trick. Over the
washing-stand was a bottle of stout, a plate of
sandwiches, a plug of tobacco, and a greater,
though by him unnoticed miracle, a whole candle
burning. But it was all real, as were also a pile
of shillings by the sandwiches.
Stella was in bed, listening with delight. She
opened her eyes and caught his—flaming with
some terrible suspicion. She had her little lie of
explanation ready, but when he clutched the bottle
like a weapon some deadly fear made her leap
from the bed crying, “What are you going to do ? ”
He swung the bottle like a club, but the stout
gurgled and an overwhelming desire to drink rose,
grew, culminated, and finally swamped other
passions. He laughed.
“Why? drink of course. Here’s —looking at
her with meaning—here’s success to you, missus,”
and he put the bottle to his lips.
Then he devoured sandwiches. Stella was
surprised and hurt. She wondered why he did
not ask questions.
“ Ted, I got it from ”
He arose in fury. “ Don’t tell me —don’t speak
of it as you value your life.”
* * * *
This went on for a week. Stella kept playing
the chemist off —her clothes were in pawn, not
here, but in Melbourne —as soon as they arrived
she would ; so Ted had his three meals a day and
sundries. But he was morose and silent. One
afternoon Stella came in at dusk. A stranger was
in her room—bending over Ted’s portmanteau.
He turned—it was Ted in a new and gay summer
suit.
She knew in an instant, and between a choke
and a scream cried, “ You’re not leaving me ! ”
“ Yes, you flinging her off with the worst
of names—to a woman.
She put her hand to her ears.
“What—you think that of me ? ”
“ Where else did the money come from ? ”
“ How dare you —how dare you ? ”
Her small form dilated, her voice cut like a
knife, the purring kitten of a woman became a
tigress. He was frightened.
What else could I think—you never ex-
plained.”
The black passion of her vehemence faded sud-
denly. A horrid laugh came from her tense lips,
and her face grew like a white fire as she said with
the utmost deliberation and icy distinctness.
‘ ‘There—is—no—explanation— your—suspicions
are—-perfectly —true —l—am—what —you say
—so —you —who—have—been —living on—me—
are—a ” she retaliated with the worst of names
to a man, the nadir of reproach to which further
retort is impossible.
Then a clock struck ; he remembered he was
losing time. He turned to his portmanteau. All
her pride melted. She clung to him—weeping out
the whole story.
“ He only kissed me, I swear, and I hated him
for it. I did it for you ... it was all I could . . .
and he only kissed me . . . you won’t leave me
now, will you ? ”
He went on packing, telling her to go back to
her chemist.
“ I can’t. I told him this afternoon I was only
fooling him for money.”
“What!”
“I couldn’t keep it up any longer. He took me
to the shop to buy furniture for our—his—cottage,
and I couldn’t keep it up any longer—it was such
lovely furniture, and I wanted it, and I—l don’t
know, only—don’t leave me, Ted, don’t leave
me.”
“ What did he do?”
“Nothing—gave me this when I said I was
stranded and told me never to come near him
again.”
She showed a handful of money. “ Take it.
Ted.”
He put out his hand, obeying a habit, but a re-
vulsion came over him—and he told her to keep it.
The clock struck again. She clung to him, begged
piteously, promised impossibilities, vowed, sobbed,
screamed, raged, till he grew angry, struck her to
the floor, caught his portmanteau and rushed down
stairs.
The rattle of his cab revived her. She followed
and called another, losing time over the cabman’s
hesitation at her appearance. She showed money
and bade him drive to the wharf.
Ted was just in timo to meet a well-dressed
woman and carry her portmanteau, with his own,
up the gangway.
Stella was just in timo to see them disappear
into the salobn as the steamor swung in mid-
stream. The light went out of the sky and her
soul as the steamer passod out. She turned and
walked home as one condemned, stopping by the
way to purchase a pot of Rouge Fin.

cream, consequently the butter has a fine aroma that only Pasteurisation can give. There are a few, we are
pleased to say, only a few, idiotic folks who will not be convinced of the value of the process ; but it is satisfactory

ordinary make is only per lb. It would pay consumers well to give six times this difference. Heads of households should insist
that their grocer supplies Pasteurised butter, and if he will not, send yoiir Orders to US, and we will deliver it at vour doors.
